1st Lexember

graðleur /gʀaðˈlawʀ/ [gʁɐðˈlawɐ] volunteer

< Old Borlish (a) grad liure “freely, voluntarily”, literally “with free will”
< Latin grātus “will” and līber “free, unrestricted”.

donation /donaˈtsjɔn/  [ˌdonɐˈtsjɔn] donation, gift

< borrowed ca. 1250 from Latin dōnātiō, dōnātiōnem “an act of giving, of presenting”, from dōnō “I give”.

Il se ponen a plaç meg ny rout, graðleur e donaçon rovant.
They were standing in the street asking for money and volunteers.
/ˌɪl se pɔnˈdɛn a ˌplats ˈmi ni ˈʀut | gʀaðˈlawʀ e ˌdonaˈtsɔn ʀoˈvant/
[ɪw sɪ pʊnˈdɛn ɐ plas ˈmiː nɪ ˈʀʊt | gʀɐðˈlaw‿ʀɪ ˌdonɐˈtsɔŋ ʀʊˈvant]

##

2nd Lexember

porçon /poʀˈtsɔn/ [pʊːˈtsɔn] portion, share, allotment

< Old Boral porton, porcon “part, share” < Latin portiō, portiōnem “share, portion”.

porçonnar /ˌpoʀtsoˈnaʀ/ [ˌpɔːtsʊˈnɑː] to share, to allocate, to hand out

Verb derivation from porçon, ca. 1400. Displaced former partir in this sense.

porçonment /ˌpoʀtsɔnˈmɛnt/ [ˌpɔːtsʊmˈmɛnt] assignment, categorisation, sorting, (mathematics) partition

Nominalisation of porçonnar, attested from 1650. In mathematics from 1830, originally in translation from Dutch Austeilung “distributing”.

Y graðleur porçonaurn y mansc seyon coglt pall'eç joun orphan.
The volunteers shared the food they had collected with the orphans here.
/i gʀaðˈlawʀ ˌpɔʁtsoˈnoʀn̩ i ˈmaŋx sijɔn ˈkɔjlt palɛts ˈʒun ɔʀˈfan/
[i gʁɐðˈlawɐ ˌpɔːtsʊˈnoːn i ˈmaŋk sʊn ˈkɔwt pɐˈlɛs ˈʒʊn ʊːˈfan]

-

Ayent dou cougl W e X, un porçonment dy som W + X nommau nos un mission m ant font W e bers X ða casc porçon toc X cos unic.

Given two sets W and X, a partition of the sum W + X is called a function m with source W and target X if each part intersects W exactly once.

##

3rd Lexember

absouvr /abˈsuvʀ̩/ [ɐbˈzuvɐ] to acquit, to redeem, to absolve

[r-conjugation, -oy variant]

< Old Boral absovre, absolvre “to absolve”, a semi-learned descendant/borrowing from Latin absolvō “I loosen, untie”.

tort dell'ivan /ˌtɔʀt deliˈvan/ [ˌtɔːdɪlɪˈvaŋ] Christmas pie*

*literally “infant's pie”, in reference to the Christ child. Refers either to a specific savoury pie or to any of the pastry dishes traditionally prepared for the Christmas Eve feast (y Fest Reveglon), a celebration held in churches across Borland (Istr Boral). The practice of scoring a Catholic cross into the lid and hiding a small wooden object inside (traditionally also a cross) is a later innovation, but compare the French galette des rois. 

< Old Boral torte del ifan “child's pie” (first attested 10C), from Latin torta panis “twisted bread” and īnfāns, īnfāntem “child, infant”.

Y justiçour absoloirn my grandmaðr poll'ig delit a blasmant.

The judges found my grandmother innocent of blasphemy.

/i ˌʒɪstiˈtsuʀ ˌabsoˈlojʀn̩ mi gʀandˈmaðʀ̩ poˌlaj deˈlɪt a blaˈsmant/

[i ˌʒɪstɪˈtsʊː‿ˌʀabzʊˈlɔjɐŋ mi gʀɐmˈmaðɐ pʊˌlaj dɪˈlɪt a blɐˈzmant]

-

Nos eu l'oc annað dou tort dell'ivan apportað; stoven torrir ag casc aldr!
We've brought two Christmas pies this year - both my parents wanted to bake!
/no‿ˈzaw lɔk aˈnaθ du ˌtɔʀt deliˈvan apɔʀˈtaθ | faˈlɛn toˈʀɪʀ ɛj kax aldʀ̩/
[nʊ‿ˈzaw lɔgɐˈnaː du ˌtɔːdɪlɪˈvaŋ apʊːˈtaː | fɐˌlɛn tʊˈʀɪː‿ʀɛj kɐx ˈawdɐ]

##

4th Lexember

bors /bɔʀz/ [bɔːz] fund, allowance, stipend

< Late Old Boral borse “purse” (11C), from Late Latin bursa “oxhide, purse” via Old French. Originally from Greek βύρσα “hide, wine-skin”.

bors scholer /bɔʀz skoˈlɛʀ/ [bɔːz skʊˈlɛː] scholarship, grant

< Second part attested from 10C as skolar, scholair “pertaining to academia”.  Derived from schol “school, academia” + the adjectival -er suffix, itself from Latin -ārius with the same meaning. Phrase first attested 15C in reference to the New School in Vithor (Viðor).

Il voun calscon fallent fair por l'oc bors scholer cavir.
They will do whatever it takes to get this grant.
/ɪl ˌvun kalˈxɔn faˌlɛnt fɛʀ ˌpɔʀ lɔk ˌbɔʀz skoˈlɛʀ kaˈvɪʀ/
[ɪw ˌvun kɐwˈxɔŋ fɐˈlɛnt fɛː pɔː lʊ ˈbɔːz skʊˈlɛː kɐˈvɪː] 

##

5th Lexember

surmettr /sɪʀˈmɛtʀ̩/ [sɪːˈmɛtɐ] to allow, to permit; to condone, to excuse

< Old Boral surmetre, suvr mettre “to send/throw over”, from Latin super- “over, above” (with sporadic lengthening of the first vowel, cf. French sur) and mittō “I send, release”.

Y concegl surmettraun jamay ty messarf periglous d'œculux bressem.
The committee will never condone your dangerous misuse of ionising radiation.
/i kɔnˈtsil sɪʀmɛˈtʀon ʒaˈme ti meˈsaʀf peʀajˈluz dekiˈlɪks ˈbʀɛsɛm/
[i kʊnˈtsiju sɪːmɪˈtʀon ʒɐˈme ti mɪˈsɑːf peʀɐjˈlʊz dekɪˈlɪks ˈbʀɛsɛm]

##

6th Lexember

lon cost /lɔn ˈkɔst/ [lʊŋˈkɔst] free (of charge), freely given

< “without cost”. The first word is from Old Boral loin “away from”, from Latin longē “from far off”; the second a deverbal derivation of the verb costar “to cost, to be worth” (first attested 12C), from Latin constō “I consist of, I cost”. Both the verb and the deverbal are unattested outside of this construction after 1500.

Vail un bon trenc, lour dar un jorn cogmað lon cost.
It would be nice to give them a day off for free.
/ˌvel ɪn bɔn ˈtʀɛŋk | luʀ ˈdaʀ ɪn ˈʒɔʀn̩ kɔjˈmaθ lɔn ˈkɔst/
[ˌvew ɪm bɔn ˈtʀɛŋk | lʊː ˈdɑː‿ʀɪn ˈʒɔːŋ kojˈmaθ lʊŋˈkɔst]

##

7th Lexember

trocar entr /tʀoˈkaʀ ɛntʀ̩/ [tʀʊˈkɑː‿ʀɛntɐ] to reciprocate, to repay, to retaliate

< “to trade between”. Preposition from Latin inter “between, among”; verb possibly from an Old Boral trocar “to trade”, either an alternation of torcar < Latin torqueō “I twist, bend”, or more likely from Old Spanish trocar via Old French, of uncertain origin.

Ða meðes nos poðau lou fornir d'aïðanç, il trocausn noc entr tardessem. 
Even if we provided them with aid, they would not reciprocate later.
/ða meˈðɛz no poˈðo lu fɔʀˈnɪʀ dɛjiˈðants | ɪl tʀoˈkosn̩ nɔk ɛntʀ̩ taʀˈdɛsɛm/
[ða mɪˈðɛz no pʊˈðo lu fʊːˈnɪː dɛjˈðants | ɪw tʀʊˈkosn̩ nɔ‿gɛntɐ tɐːˈdɛsm̩]

##

8th Lexember

bakilau /ˌbakiˈlo/ [ˌbakɪˈlo] cod, marine fish of the family Gadidae

< Origin contested. Extant from 9C as cabeilau, bacalau (among other variants), from Middle Dutch (Mi-Willem), cf. French cabillaud “cod”. Entered Middle Dutch either from some derivation of baculum “stick, rod”, or else from Basque kabailao “cod”. Formerly assumed to be a direct loan from Vask into Boral – see also other naval/fishing terminology like hagr “mast" – but this is now considered phonetically untenable.

J'oyeu ne Horð il pascn for bakilau.
I heard that in Horth they only eat cod.
/ʒoˈjaw ne hɔʀˈða ɪl ˈpaxn̩ fɔʀ ˌbakiˈlo/
[ʒʊˈjaw nɪ hʊːˈða ɪw ˈpaxn̩ fɔː ˌbakɪˈlo] 

##

9th Lexember

banfoy /banˈfɔj/ [bɐɱˈfɔj] campfire, open-air fire

< partial calque of Old English bānfȳr “bonfire”, literally “bone fire”, meaning a fire in which to dispose of rubbish (such as bones). Generalisation to any temporary fire built outdoors attested from 15C.

banfoy quïr /kwaˈjɪʀ/ [kwɐˈjɪː] to pack up camp, to prepare for a trip

< Literally “to extinguish (the) campfire”, refers by metonymy to the entire disassembly of a campsite, or more generally to any preparation made for a journey.

Nos voum partir je Maucr, donc toð je Mart faldra banfoy quïr.
We'll leave on Wednesday, so we need all Tuesday to pack.
/no ˈvum paʀˈtɪʀ ʒe ˈmokʀ | dɔnk tɔθ ʒe maʀt falˈdʀa banˈfɔj kwaˈjɪʀ/
[nʊ ˈvʊm pɐːˈtɪː ʒɪ ˈmokɐ | dɔŋk ˈtɔː ʒɪ ˈmɑːt fɐwˈdʀa bɐɱˈfɔj kwɐˈjɪː] 

##

10th Lexember

rastel /ʀasˈtɛl/ [ʀɐsˈtɛw] rake

< Latin rastellum “rake, hoe”, diminutive of rastrum “rake”.

rastel fair /feʀ/ [fɛː] to be thrifty, to save money

< “to rake”, via the sense “to waste nothing”, seen from 13C. In the sense “be spendthrift”, it was originally a positive judgement, but by 18C was exclusively used to insult, in the sense “be miserly”.

Nien va par un bricot nuçal lour rovant, com i fay rastel!
There's no point asking him for a wedding present, he's so miserly!
/ʀjɛn va paʀ ɪn bʀiˈkɔt nuts luʀ ʀoˈvant | kɔm i fe ʀasˈtɛl/
[ʀjɛɱ va pɑː‿ʀɪm bʀɪˈkɔt nʊts lʊː ʀʊˈvant | kɔm i fe ʀɐsˈtɛl]

##

11th Lexember

scadom pisað /xiˈtɔm piˈzaθ/ [çɪˈtɔm pɪˈzaː] tomato sauce, purée

First word from Moorish Arabic (arab moresc) شطم <sheit·om> “tomato”, itself from the Classical Nahuatl (nawat normal) for “tomato”, scitomatl. Second word the past participle of pisar “crush, grind”, from Latin pīnsō “I beat, pound”.

A Portingal fo un method novel, ant carn aromað ne saus de scitom pisað.

In Portingale there was a new recipe, with spiced meat in a tomato sauce.

/a ˌlɪsˈtɛjn fo ɪn meˈθɔd noˈvɛl | ant ˈkaʀn ˌaʀoˈmaθ ne ˈsoz de xiˈtɔm piˈzaθ/

[ɐ ˌlɪsˈtɛjn fo m̩ mɪˈθɔd nʊˈvɛw | ɐŋ ˈkɑːn ˌaʀʊˈmaː nɪ ˈsoz dɪ çɪˈtɔm pɪˈzaː]

##

12th Lexember

barvier /baʀˈvjɛʀ/ [bɑːvɪˈjɛː] shepherd(ess), one who guides a flock

< many forms are attested in Old Boral (approximately 7C-11C); barviar and varvȝair, for example. All are from Vulgar Latin vervecārius, from Latin vervēx “wether, castrated ram”.

Y soldart eð y barvier vencoirn y dragon e soulor allagr vescoirn il.
The soldier and the shepherdess killed the dragon and lived happily ever after.
/i sɔlˈdaʀt eθ i baʀˈvjɛʀ vɛnˈkɔjʀn̩ i dʀaˈgɔn e suˈlɔʀ aˈlɛjʀ veˈxɔjʀn̩ ɪl/
[i sʊwˈdɑːt e‿ði bɑːvɪˈjɛː vɪŋˈkɔjɐn i dʀɐˈgɔn e sʊˈlɔː‿ʀɐˈlɛjɐ vɪˈxɔjɐn ɪw] 

##

13th Lexember

scurvog /xɪʀˈvɔj/ [çɪːˈvɔj] scurvy, pathological lack of sandrine*

< Old Boral skurvuȝ et al., presumably from Old Norrish skyrbjúgr “sour-milk tumour”. Discovered in the late 15C to be caused by diet, following the famed voyage to Mendeva** of Princess Alexandra of Vascony (the first time a European royal set foot in the Novomund), after whom sandrine is indirectly named.

Rey Ambrosc commandau casc vascel port frugt sandrin, ma faloy moneð demay.
King Ambrose required each ship to carry citrus fruits, but the cost was too high.
/ʀi amˈbʀox komanˈdo kax vaˈxɛl pɔʀt ˈfʀajt sanˈdʀɪn | pi faˈlɔj moˈnɛθ deˈme/
[ʀi‿jɐmˈbʀɔx komɐnˈdo kax vɐˈxɛw pɔːt ˈfʀajt sɐnˈdʀɪn | pi fɐˈlɔj mʊˈnɛː dɪˈme]

-

*Vitamin C

**North America

##

14th Lexember

boleð /boˈlɛθ/ [bʊˈlɛh] mushroom, porcini

< Latin bōlētus “mushroom”. Can refer by extension to other objects with a similar shape.

Infeliç, my sour poð noc boleð pascr, dec i lou support noc.
Unfortunately, my sister cannot eat mushrooms as she is intolerant.
/ˌɪnfeˈlɪts | mi ˈsuʀ pɔθ ˌnɔk boˈlɛθ paxʀ̩ | dɛk ˈi lu siˈpɔʀt nɔk/
[ˌɪɱfɪˈlɪts | mi ˈsʊː pɔh ˈnɔg bʊˈlɛh paxɐ | dɛk ˈi lu sɪˈpɔːt nɔk]

##

15th Lexember

formig se cavir /fɔʀˈmaj se kaˈvɪʀ/ [fɔːˈmaj se kɐˈvɪː] to have pins and needles

< literally “to get ants (on yourself)”; the first word either from Vulgar Latin formīcus, or directly from Latin formīca “ant”. The verb from capiō “I seize, take”.

Ah, j'ay formig me caut – jo seray dormið sull'oc braç!
Ow, I've got pins and needles – I must have slept on my arm!
/ˈa | ʒe fɔʀˈmaj me ˈkot | ʒo seˈʀe dɔʀˈmɪθ silɔk ˈbʀats/
[ˈa: | ʒe fɔːˈmaj mɪ ˈkɔt | ʒo sɪˈʀe dɔːˈmɪh silʊ ˈbʀats]

##

16th Lexember

quemant /kweˈmant/ [kwɪˈman(t)] comfortable, cosy

< Present participle of obsolete verb quemar “to be comfortable, to be suitable”, borrowed into Old Boral ca. 9C from Old English cƿēman “to please, to satisfy”.

Cossy asseïr sull'ig caðer no sera scur quemant.
Surely sitting on the chair like that is uncomfortable.
/koˈsi asiˈjɪʀ siˌlaj kaˈðɛʀ no seˈʀa xɪʀ kweˈmant/
[kʊˈsi asɪˈjɪː sɪˌlaj kɐˈðɛː no sɪˈʀa xɪː kwɪˈman(t)]

17th Lexember

nohas /noˈhaz/ [nʊˈxaz] (of economics) right-wing, conservative

< 1751, from Arabic نحاس (nuḥās) with the same sense, and originally “copper”. Inspired by the Copper faction in the court of Malik Hisham Al-Rashid (reigned 1601-1610) of Libya, who petitioned against the poor reforms he oversaw. The Copper, and their opposition, the Tin, took their names from the two principal colours of the Libyan banner at the time, which was quartered into two black, one white, and one orange field. These two terms – nohas and casdir – were borrowed into various European languages through the success of the Vascon writer David Bensaíd's treatise on statecraft The Three Cities, which was translated into over twenty languages. Compare for example French nouase, kasdir.

Stou y Duc coy l'eç nohas parmy sull'administration d'autortað may accarr.
The Duke must stop the right-wing in his regime gaining more influence.
/fal i ˈdɪk cɔj i ˌtsi noˈhaz paʀˈmi siladˌministʀaˈtsjɔn ˌdotɔʀˈtaθ me aˈkaʀ/
[faw i ˈdɪk cɔj (i) ˌtsi nʊˈxaz pɑːˈmi silɐdˌministʀɐˈtsjɔn ˌdotɔːˈtah me ɐˈkɑː] 

##

siemprað /sjɛmˈpʀaθ/ [sɪmˈpʀah] cheerful, content, in a good mood

< past participle of siemprar “to be cheerful, to celebrate” (now only in certain dialects), from some variant of the salutation siempr! “always!”, traditionally said at feasts and other get-togethers.

Il parmettoirn nos entraum siemprað.
They cheerfully let us come in.
/ɪl paʀˈmɪzɛʀn noz enˈtʀom sjɛmˈpʀaθ/
[ɪw pɑːˈmɪzɐn noz ɪnˈtʀom sɪmˈpʀah]

##

19th Lexember

corrovað /ˌkoʀoˈvaθ/ [ˌkoʀʊˈvah] amateur, recruit, enthusiast, novice

< from Middle Boral corrouað “invitee, recruit”, past participle of corrouar “to invite, to recruit”, itself from Old Boral coroar “to invite”, from Latin corrogō “I bring together”. Sense of “amateur, enthusiast” from 16C.

Jo vel for un corrovað me nommar, mathem partenent.
I'd only consider myself an amateur when it comes to maths.
/ʒo ˈvɛl fɔʀ ɪn ˌkoʀoˈvaθ me noˈmaʀ | maˈθɛm ˌpaʀteˈnɛnt/
[ʒʊ ˈvɛw fɔː‿ʀɪŋ ˌkoʀʊˈvah me nʊˈmɑː | mɐˈθɛm ˌpɑːtɪˈnɛn(t)]

##

20th Lexember

desamoraçar /dezamoʀatsaʀ/ [dɪˌzamʊʀɐˈtsɑː] to fall out of love

< from des- “reversal, negation” + amoraçar “to fall in love, especially suddenly”, itself from (now rare) amoraç “sudden lust, all-consuming desire”, either from Old Boral amor “love” + -aç “intensifier, pejorative”, or directly from Latin amoraceus “resembling love”.

Jan es desamoraçandessem ; alor i's luy y courstravay accommettent.
John is more likely to fall out of love, so he's the one having an affair.
/ˈʒan ɛz ˌdezamoˌʀatsanˈdɛsɛm | aˈlɔʀ ɪz ˈlaj i ˌkuʀstʀaˈve ˌakoˈmɛtɛnt/
[ˈʒan ɪz ˌdezɐmʊˈʀatsɐnˈdɛsɐm | ɐˈlɔː‿ʀɪz ˈlaj ɪ ˌkʊːstʀɐˈve ˌakʊˈmɛtɐn(t)]

##

21st Lexember

desir escapat /deˈzɪʀ ˌɛskaˈpat/ [dɪˈzɪː‿ˌʀɛskɐˈpat] desire for adventure, wanderlust

< phrase from 18C, literally “adventure want”; the first word is a deverbal (from at least 8C) of desirar “to want, desire”, from Latin dēsīderō “I want, wish for”. The second word is a 17C borrowing from Vascon escapat “adventure” < “escape, breakout”, past participle of escapar “to escape”. This in turn comes from Vulgar Latin excappō “I escape”, literally “I go out of the cloak” (ex cappō).

Sy maðr creye ig sy desir escapat sceus le coïr d'un marið trovar.
Her mother believed her wanderlust might stop her finding a husband.
/si ˈmaðʀ̩ kʀiˈje aj ˌsi deˈzɪʀ ˌɛskaˈpat ˈxawz le koˈjɪʀ dɪn maˈʀɪθ tʀoˈvaʀ/
[sɪ ˈmaðɐ kʀɪˈje ʔaj ˌsi dɪˈzɪː‿ˌʀɛskɐˈpat ˈxawz le kʊˈjɪː dɪm mɐˈʀɪh tʀʊˈvɑː]

##

22nd Lexember

Caresm /kaˈʀɛzm̩/ [kɐˈʀɛzɐm] Lent, Lententide

< named after the forty days Jesus spent in the desert. From Vulgar Latin quarēsima, from Latin Quadrāgēsima “Lent”, literally “fortieth”. Traditionally a period of penitence and abstinence.

Jusc oy, Rom foy prohibið ig taisson sein paut durant Caresm.
Hitherto, Rome had banned badger from being eaten in Lent.
/ʒiˈxɔj | ˈʀɔm fɔj ˌpʀohiˈbɪθ aj teˈsɔn sin ˈpot diˈʀant kaˈʀɛzm̩/
[ʒɪˈxɔj | ˈʀɔm fɔj ˌpʀohɪˈbɪh aj tɪˈsɔn sɪm ˈpot dɪˈʀaŋ kɐˈʀɛzɐm]

##

23rd Lexember

viol cemtir /vjɔl tsɛmˈtɪʀ/ [vɪˈjɔw tsɪnˈtɪː] graveyard fiddles

< The first word from Mediæval Tuscane viola, probably from Old Vascon viula, from Late Latin vitula (all with the same meaning). The second from Old Boral cemiteȝr, from Latin cœmētērium “burial ground”, from Greek κοιμητήριον “sleeping chamber”.

Refers to the mysterious sound of string instruments in the air, which in Borlish folklore is attributed to various spirits connected to death, and to cemeteries in particular. Usually said to be low, mournful melodies. Apparently a tradition peculiar to Borland, as no closely analogous figures are seen in stories abroad.

Featured  in the opera Ale in Fiamme “Wings Afire” (a retelling of the story of Icarus which famously takes place entirely after the events of the myth), composed by Venisian Chiarina Bruno and first performed in 1824. Her first work, she is said to have heard the tale of the viol cemtir from her brother Jacomo, who had fought alongside Borlish soldiers in the Second German War.

Vos orreð viol cemtir moutessem, com dit, all'hour pre y lugnt.
They say you will most often hear the graveyard fiddles the hour before dawn.
/vo‿zoˈʀɛθ vjɔl tsɛmˈtɪʀ muˈtɛsɛm | kɔm ˈdɪt | aˈluʀ pʀe i ˈlajnt/
[vo‿zʊˈʀɛh vɪˈjɔw tsɪnˈtɪː mʊˈtɛsɐm | kʊnˈdɪt | ɐˈlʊː pʀɛj ˈlajn(t)]

##

24th Lexember

trevold /tʀeˈvɔld/ [tʀɪˈvɔwd] novel, book, story

< circa 1640, from Dench bog trevold “octavo book” (that is, a book printed on sheets in octavo size). Literally “three-fold”, named as octavo parchment is produced by folding folios three times.

Jo te pieð, dig en mey nien – jo n'ay deja y cart trevold leit dell'oc cougl.
Please don't tell me anything – I've not yet read the fourth book in the series.
/ˌʒo te ˈpjɛθ | ˈdaj ɛn mi ˈʀjɛn | ʒo ˌne deˈʒa i ˌkaʀt tʀeˈvɔld ˈlit delɔk ˈkɔjl/
[ʒʊtˈpjɛh | ˌdajmːiʀɪˈjɛn | ʒʊˈne dɪˈʒa ɪˈkɑːt tʀɪˈvɔwd lɪt ˌdelʊˈkɔjʊ] 

##

25th Lexember

jonnovar zucarrað /ˌʒonoˈvaʀ zikaˈʀaθ/ [ˌʒonʊˈvɑː ˌzikɐˈʀah] candied butterflies

< first word from Old Boral ȝenhovar and similar forms, from Old Welsh gwenhwyfar “white spirit”, possibly via an Old English form *ġenovare, though this is disputed. Equivalent to English names Guinevere, Jennifer. Second word the past participle of zucarrar “to sugar, to sweeten”, from zucar “sugar”, coming via Old Italian zuccharo from Arabic سُكَّر‎ (sukkar).

A type of sweet, shaped pastry spiced with cinnamon or nutmeg, traditionally in the form of butterflies. From early Borlish symbols of rebirth, associated originally with the winter solstice but now linked strongly to Christmas Day.

Scan noc y jonnovar zucarrað ancour – nos despardreu eç fam.
Don't bring out the candied butterflies yet – we'll ruin our appetites.
/ˈxan nɔk i ˌʒonoˈvaʀ zikaˈʀaθ anˈkuʀ ‖ no ˌdɛspaʀˈdʀaw ɛts ˈfam/
[ˈxan nɔk i ˌʒonʊˈvɑː ˌzikɐˈʀah ɐŋˈkʊː ‖ no ˌdɛspɑːˈdʀaw ɛs ˈfam]

##

26th Lexember

neyanç vert /niˈjants vɛʀt/ [nɪˈjants vɛːt] green snowfall

< Phrase attested since at least 1292. First word is synchronically neyar “to snow” plus the gerundive -anç, from Late Latin nivat “it snows” (a regularisation of Classical Latin ningit) and the nominalising suffix -antia, respectively. Second word descended from Latin viridis “green, lively”.

Refers to the first snowfall of the year (that is, on or after the first of March), if there is one. Years with a green snowfall are generally considered unlucky, as a symptom of a long and bitter preceding winter.

Y preu osc borallesc dy Grant Morenç a 1519 fis loy ne Damvað a mesur dy neyanç vert.
The first Borlish victim of the 1519 Great Dying was in Damvað during the green snowfall.
/i pʀaw ˈɔx ˌboʀaˈlɛx di ˈgʀant moˈʀɛnts a ˈmɪl tsɪnkˈtsɛnt noˈvɛts fɪz ˈlɔj ne damˈvaθ a meˈzɪʀ di niˈjants vɛʀt/
[i pʀaw ˈʔɔx ˌboʀɐˈlɛh dɪ ˌgʀamːʊˈʀɛnts ɐ ˈmɪl tsɪŋˈksɛn nʊˈvɛts fɪz ˈlɔj ne dɐɱˈvah a mɪˈzɪː di nɪˈjants vɛːt]

##

27th Lexember

stif jacent /stɪf ʒaˈtsɛnt/ [stɪf ʒɐˈtsɛn(t)] recumbent satiety (food coma)

< Attested with current meaning from 19C; literally “load that lies down”. First word meaning “load, stuffing”, deverbal of stivar “to load, to stuff, to crowd”, from Latin stīpō “I crowd, I compress”. Second word the present participle of jacir “to lie down, to rest” (also in colloquial speech, “to sleep”), from Latin iaceō “I lie down”.

Refers to the sleepy state one enters when one has eaten perhaps too much food.

Jo dorme prell'hour un ; nos ayau tout stif jacent dy Fest Reveglon.
I was asleep before one; we were all sleepily stuffed from the Christmas meal.
/ˌʒo dɔʁˈme ˌpʁe luʀ ˈɪn | ˌno‿zɛˈjo tut ˈstɪf ʒaˈtsɛnt di ˈfɛst ˌʀeviˈlɔn/
[ˌʒo dʊːˈme ˌpʀe lʊː‿ˈʀɪn | ˌno‿zɪˈjo tʊt ˈstɪf ʒɐˈtsɛn dɪ ˈfɛst ˌʀevɪˈlɔn]

##

28th Lexember

jeunar /ʒawˈnaʀ/ [ʒɐwˈnɑː] to fast, to not eat

< from Old Boral ieunar, from Latin ieiūnō “I fast”. Synchronically analysable as jeun “fast” + verbal -ar. With prefix des- < Latin dis-, we have desjeun “breakfast”, desjeunar “to have breakfast”.

Nos som jeunant y jorn, ma colluïsc mey a masquet pos occas vars un grant quelt.
We're fasting during the day, but come with me to mosque after sunset for a big dinner.
/no ˈsɔm ʒawˈnant i ˈʒɔʀn | dɔnk ˌkɔlaˈjɪx mi a masˈkwɛt pɔz oˈkaz vaʀz ɪn ˌgʀant ˈkwɛlt/
[nʊ ˈsɔm ʒɐwˈnan‿tɪ ˈʒɔːn | dɔŋk ˌkɔlɐˈjɪç mi a mɐsˈkwɛt pɔz ʊˈkaz vɑːz ɪŋ ˌgʀant ˈkwɛwt]

##

29th Lexember

retrasent /ˌʀetʀaˈzɛnt/ [ˌʀetʀɐˈzɛn(t)] back and forth

< present participle of retrasir “to cross back over”, being the prefix re- “again, once more” with the verb trasir “to cross”. This verb is either a somewhat irregular derivation of Latin trānsvertō “I turn across”, or directly from the preposition tras “across” + -ir.

Y kenonaut vil casc meis volar retrasiscent entr y Tar eð y Lun.
The spaceship will fly back and forth between the Earth and the Moon each month.
/i ˌkenoˈnot vɪl kax ˈmɔnθ voˈlaʀ ˌʀetʀaˈzɛnt ɛnt‿ʀi ˈtaʀ ɛθ i ˈlɪn/
[i ˌkenʊˈnot vɪw kɐxˈmɔnθ vʊˈlɑː ˌʀetʀɐˈzɛn(t) ɛnt‿ʀi ˈtɑː‿ʀɛ‿ði ˈlɪn]

##

30th Lexember

gyr /ʒɪʀ/ [ʒɪː] orbit, circular motion

< 16C, from Latin gȳrus “circle, circuit”, probably via French gyre; from Greek γῦρος (gûros) “circle, ring”. Predominantly in technical contexts and in translations of astronomical texts until the success of Elsebeth Sneider's Durgh'nen Shorzen Shouer (Through a Glass, Darkly) in 1795, and the ensuing decades-long fashion for voidtales*.

Nos s'usareu l'ignifex posc y kenonaut arra dou gyr may complið.
We will run out of oxygen when the ship has completed two more orbits.
/no no‿ˌzizaˈʀaw ˌlajniˈfɛks pɔx i ˌkenoˈnot aˈʀa du ˈʒɪʀ me kɔmˈplɪθ/
[no nʊ‿ˌzizɐˈʀaw ˌlajnɪˈfɛks pɔx ɪ ˌkenʊˈnɔt ɐˈʀa du ˈʒɪː me kʊmˈplɪh]

*fiction set in space.

##

31st Lexember

disq a CMM /ˈdɪsk a ˌtse ɛm ˈɛm/ [ˈdɪsk ɐ ˌtseʔɪˈmɛm] record, vinyl disc

(in common usage, un CMM “a vinyl”)

< first word attested 18C, borrowed from French disque “disc”, from Latin discus “discus, dish”. Second word an initialism for catener a mitigor muriac “chained muriac mitigor*”. Catener “polymer” is a direct borrowing of Latin catēnārius “of a chain”, referring to the molecular structure thereof; mitigor** is likewise from mītigōr “ripener”, after its ability to ripen fruits; muriac*** is from muriacus “briny”.

A tu lour y derran disq a CMM oïð : “Soll'Autr Stel” ?
Have you heard their latest record “Beneath New Stars”?
/ˌa ti ˈluʀ i deˈʀan ˈdɪsk a ˌtse ɛm ˈɛm oˈjɪθ | soˈlotʀ̩ ˈstɛl/
[ˌati ˈlʊː‿ʀi dɪˈʀan ˈdɪsk ɐ ˌtseʔɪˈmɛm ʊˈjɪh | sʊˈlotɐ ˈstɛw]

*polyvinyl chloride (PVC).
**ethene.
***chlorinated.

##

Mondifex 2020 - 1r Janver

soccoðr /soˈkɔðʀ̩/ [sʊˈkɔðɐ] to shake, to jostle, to toss

An -r conjugation verb, past participle soccos (that is, s variant). From Latin succutiō “I shake, I fling” with usual collapse of the -iō verb class. Compare French secouer “to shake”.

Jo soccos my jacquot avant y foyer ne spien i sechisceus un pog.
I shook my coat in front of the fire so that it might dry out a bit.
/ʒo soˈkɔz mi ʒaˈkwot aˈvant i foˈjɛʀ ne ˈspjɛn i ˌsekiˈxawz ɪn ˈpɔj/

##


Mondifex 2020 - 2m Janver

Ambrose¹ III (17 March 1435 - 29 February 1518) was king of Vascony from 1456 until his death, making him the longest ruler in Vascon history. He rose to the throne with the country at its greatest extent during his reign: from Bordew in the north to Port Calè in the south, although in practice Vascon rule barely reached as far as Braga.

King Ambrose III pushed for naval development, establishing the New Navarre Enterprise in 1458. He aimed to capitalise on Vascon supremacy in the fishing trade, trading cod from Dunclothe and Valland throughout the Middlesea. And of course, it was just such a ship that landed on Mendevan² shores in 1471 and introduced Eurasia to the Novomund.

Despite losing Vascony's Middlesea coast to Burgundy and Barcelon, and despite never marrying, King Ambrose III is remembered well for presiding over a period of relative peace and prosperity. His death presaged the decline of Vascony; it lost over a fifth of its population in the Second Great Dying, and ceded Portingale (its economic heart, having benefited the most from Novomundine trade) to Andalus after the Romantic Wars of the seventeenth century.

———

¹in his native tongue, Ambròix.

²that is, North American.

##

3m Janver - 3rd January

japot /ʒaˈpɔt/ gap, break, opening

< attested 14C from Middle Boral jap “break, gap” plus the diminutive -ot (uncertain origin, probably a variant of -et imported from continental Romance; either French or Burgund). The word jap is from Old Boral ȝap, iapp, borrowed from Old English ġeap “open space, expanse”, with semantic influence from Old Norrish gap “gap, break”.

I vos menara an japot ny mur oyon j'ay un livr scos.
He will take you to a gap in the wall where I have hidden a book.
/i vo ˌmenaˈʀa an ʒaˈpɔt ni ˈmɪʀ oˈjɔn ʒe ɪn ˈlivʀ̩ xɔz/

##

4m Janver - 4th January

crous /kʀuz/ hollow, unfilled

also hour crous /uʀ kʀuz/ free time, time off (lit. hollow time)

< from Old Boral crose etc. “hollow, dug out”, past participle of moribund verb crour “to dig, to hollow out”, from Latin corrōdō “I gnaw, I eat away at”. Compare semi-learned verb corrodr “to corrode, to worsen” from the same root (past participle corros).

L'oc arbr es crous dec l'a un coup fourraç caut.
This tree is hollow because it's been struck by lightning.
/lɔk ˈaʀbʀ̩ ɛz ˈkʀuz dɛk la ɪn ˈkup fuˈʀats kot/

##

5m Janver - 5th January

The Brethin of Kernow [1] (Boral: Y Brethin a Cornou) were in the late mediæval period the most influential school of the Brethin religious society. They saw a substantial increase in membership through the twelfth century and counted among their members (Roman) Pope Damian VI (1199-1208) and the saint Victor of Dunchurch.

The Brethin (named for their distinctive heavy cloak; in Old Welsh, their bretin) were founded in Tremonnow [2], at that time in Devon, as an order devoted to record-keeping and the maintenance of history. Originally, they preserved in particular the history of the Christianisation of Albion [3] and Ireland, but quickly expanded their remit to secular history. They spread rapidly through the Briton states and later made inroads into France, Burgundy and even Andalus.

The Brethin of Kernow were notable for being a single organisation which admitted both men and women into their orders, although their institutions were segregated by gender. Some members were sent out of the monasteries to advise in courts across Europe, most (in)famously in the Crovatian [4] House of Domago, whose Duke so favoured his Kernish advisors that his siblings organised a coup against him.

[1] Roughly, Brittany.
[2] Monmouth (Welsh Trefynwy).
[3] Great Britain.
[4] … Croatian.

6m Janver

vain /ven/ wrist, cuff; (in a book) binding

< by metonymy from Middle Boral vain “cuff, hem”, itself from Latin vāgīna “sheath, scabbard”. Replaced in sense of “hem” by orlaç < “rim”, and replacing pognet < “little fist” in sense of “wrist”.

Y vain ag scuir stan sancant posc nos laiscaurn y traf n'eramn.
The boy's wrists were bloody when we took off his handcuffs.
/i ˈven ɛj ˈxajʀ̩ stan sanˈkant pɔx no leˈxoʀn̩ i ˈtʀaf neˈʀamn̩/

< Attested since 9C in Old Boral tarm(n) “end, boundary”, from Latin terminus with the same meaning. Is a doublet of termin “station, end point”.

Ag tarm dy rout, tras un pont sur y Barraut, yað y megl tarn n'Istr Boral.
At the end of the road, across a Barraut bridge, there's the best inn in Borland.
/ɛj ˈtaʀm di ˈʀut | ˌtʀaz ɪn ˈpɔnt ˌsɪʀ i baˈʀot | ˌlaz i ˈmil taʀn ˈnizʀ̩ boˈʀal/

##


8m Janver

- excerpted from “In Sickness and in Health: Medicine in pre-Arnoutszen Europe”, second edition, published by Winchester Scholastic Primers, 1961

… the death of up to one-fifth of the population.

Of course, despite the name, not everywhere in Europe suffered so badly from the Second Great Dying as did Portingale. Those more inland areas, such as Boham and Burgundy (having lost its coastal territory to House Amigòt in 1499) endured far fewer casualties simply by virtue of being further from the main mode of transmission: naval trade. It was not by chance that the first recorded death attributable to this plague was in Damvath in Borland [1], said at the time to be the most-trafficked port city outside the Middlesea [2].

It is a common misconception, popularised further by the famous scene in Maresca's 1931 film Frutto Amaro (Bitter Fruit), that people blamed cats and dogs for the sickness sweeping the continent. This idea, first attested in connection with the Second Great Dying only several decades after the fact, perhaps originated with the tale of the monastery at Bonn, which after several deaths to an “unknown illness” in 1512 (probably luetic pox [3]) banished all animals from the monastery and (unsuccessfully) from the neighbouring village, claiming they were a Satanic influence.

In fact, the reaction to the Dying varied significantly across Europe. The countries of Albion [4], having for the most part escaped the first continental plague the century before, and furthermore still recovering from the Wars of Fealty, were perhaps the least prepared for an epidemic, and indeed…

—-
[1] Damvað, capital city of Istr Boral, in the local language.
[2] in Europe, that is.
[3] Syphilis.
[4] Great Britain.

##


9m Janver

compagn /komˈpɛjn/ journeyman, tradesman, associate

< Old Boral compaȝn “housemate, companion”, from Late Latin compāniō “companion, messmate”, literally “with-bread” (a calque from Germanic gahlaibô “comrade” – cf. loaf). Enters Boral either directly from Late Latin, or via Old French compaing, from the same source.

A journeyman is one who has completed an apprenticeship and is employed by a master tradesman. May refer more generally to any competent but undistinguished member of a trade, or even to the rank-and-file members of a given guild or society.

Y grant atelier sta un tapeçment de laboriostur; compagn agen a casc tabr.
The great workshop was a hive of industry, journeymen working a every table.
/i ˈgʀant ˌateˈljɛʀ sta ɪn ˌtapɛtsˈmɛnt de laˌboʀjosˈtɪʀ | kɔmˈpɛjn aˈʒɛn a ˌkax ˈtabʀ̩/

farvour /faʀˈvuʀ/ enthusiasm, spirit, zeal

(esp. in collocation farvour aïr “to be eager, to look forward to”)

< either a direct descendant of Latin fervor “boiling heat, vehemence”, or a later nominalisation of the verb farvr “to be eager, to be passionate”, which is now obsolete. The verb is itself from Latin ferveō “to boil, to be hot”.

J'ay magn farvour de te veir com y pardent.

11m Janver

“Look through the glass, into the dark! Find me a star with no name, that we might pen the first chapter of its story.”

from Durgh'nen Shorzen Shouer “Through a Black Mirror” by Elsebeth Sneider, 1795

___

excerpt: New Skies, Old Stories by Simon d'Estiala, published by Prindor Marsillès, 1980

… in her twilight years she campaigned for reforms to Saxony's inheritance law, ultimately unsuccessfully, although her political society would (after her death) spearhead the suffrage movements in Saxony, Denmark, and across the Latin republics.

The Voidtale craze sparked by Sneider's seminal work persisted until the Second German War, when its optimistic and exploratory character dropped rather rapidly out of fashion. Nonetheless there are examples generally counted as Voidtale works to be found later: most notable is Narayanan's series of landscapes depicting the surfaces of other planets orbiting our Sun. Importantly, he portrays these places as barren and inhospitable, in stark contrast to the xenic [1] worlds of Sneider or her imitators. This illustrates the shift in tone in many areas of art following the war, as industry swept the world and the age of the Global Workshop dawned.

It is perhaps foolhardy to attribute the decline of Voidtale trevolds [2] solely to the gloom of post-war Europe. We might say, for example, that the idea of exploring new lands and meeting new civilisations had lost its shine, with the opening of Caddisow [3] leaving few “mysterious lands” to discover, and the developing steeple-mesh [4] allowing people everywhere to communicate far more rapidly (in particular, to discover that distant nations were often much less exotic than one imagined!).

In any case, when it comes to parachthon romance [5], the middle of the nineteenth century was somewhat of a nadir. Even once Europe had recovered and the general prosperity of the Workshop decades had truly got going, the…

[4] optical telegraphy, in the vein of semaphore; mostly superseded in the ensuing decades by the electric telegraph.

[5] speculative fiction, such as fantasy or science fiction.

13m Janver

jast /ˈʒast/ zinc; colloq. loose change

< 17C, from Scholastic Latin [1] iastum “zinc”, either from Middle Italian jasto or Vallack jastu “zinc”, from Persian جاست (jast) “zinc”, from Hindi जस्ता (jastā) “zinc”.

Discovered as an independent metal in 14C India, then traded west along Arab routes. Nonetheless, jast alloys such as brass were known in Europe from antiquity, as well as igniac jast (that is, calamine [2]), but the structure of these materials was not fully understood.

Jo vent for pisment y reloðessem, ne jast e n'eramn.

I sell only the finest clockwork, made of zinc or bronze.

___

For example, Thieves' Script was a simple replacement code used to convey secret messages between Merch forces in the final years of the Wars of Fealty. It is said to have been adapted from similar systems used by gangs of housebreakers in Nottingham, though how such systems came to the attention of King Andrew's court is unclear. It worked by converting each letter into a sequence of three symbols, and is a very early forerunner to codes like steeplescript [1] or even modern one-zeffre encoding [2].

##

17m Janver

-excerpted from A Cyclopædia of British Persons (third edition), by Wolfston Potter, published by the Yievel Brethin Primers, 1870

King Vigo the Magnificent (Vigo Magnificens, rex Brittonum)

The Kernish kingdoms at the dawn of the eighth century had only recently been unified, ruled by widowed Queen Maud of Angeus. Her son Vigo ascended to the throne upon her death in 720, shortly after the Catumagin Accord which ended hostilities and established an official border with the Kingdom of the Franks.

[Recall that the region now known as Kernow had at the time many names, including Armorica, Brittania and Dumnonia (this taken from the region in Albion whence the Britons had emigrated); the modern name dates from the eleventh century when the House of Kernow allied with the Normans to take the throne.]

King Vigo is remembered chiefly for spearheading the reconquest of Devon, which had been under the sole authority of the Kingdom of Wessex since the final defeat at Gaulford in 656 (although much of Cornwall had never entirely submitted to English rule). He had married Guinnumar, daughter to King David I of Wynneth, and had managed to secure Welsh assistance with the invasion.

After over two decades of intermittent warfare with Wessex and the victory at Petherton, Vigo was crowned King of Greater Devon (rex Magnæ Dumnoniæ), unifying Devon and Kernow. He had at least three children: among them were Cadern the Doubter, who would become king upon Vigo's death in 761, and his daughter Iseld, who later became the first patron of the Brethin of Tremonnow, and for whom the Iseld House in Bristow is named.

##

18m Janver

spallaç /spaˈlats/ shrug, raising the shoulders

also spallaçar /ˌspalaˈtsaʀ/ to shrug, to be non-committal

< from spal “shoulder” plus the suffix -aç, used to indicate certain diminutives (often pejorative). The former from Old Boral ispadl etc., from Late Latin spathula “flat, broad piece”, diminutive of Latin spatha “straight sword”. The suffix from Latin -ax, -acem, forming adjectives.

Jo peðeu aïðanç dell'instroyour, ma i spallaçau ne nien dignt.
I asked the instructor for help, but he shrugged and said nothing.
/ʒo peˈðaw ˌɛjiˈðants deˌlɪnstʀoˈjuʀ | pi i ˈspalaˈtso ne ˌʀjɛn ˈdajnt/

Y cat ac veunt am particuler y ci remorcour ennoyar ny campaner.
The cat that lives here especially likes to annoy the ringers in the belltower.
/i ˈkat ak ˈvawnt ˈam paʀˌtikiˈlɛʀ i tsi ˌʀemɔʀˈkuʀ ˌenoˈjaʀ ni ˌkampaˈnɛʀ/

##

20m Janver

- in translation, from the collected correspondence of Absolon Mortenszen, playwright and commentator in Alburgh, Denmark

[Penned the morning of the 18th of March, 1560 Nascentiæ]

To the Good Sir Anscon d'Aupont,

I hope you are well recovered from the ague of which you spoke in your last missive; I need not remind you to consider leaving the city until the bulk of the trading has ended. One never knows what foreign ills merchants may bring, as my grandfather could have attested [1]…

The town remains as dull since you departed as it was before we met, you will be pleased to hear. In point of fact, even the decent lodging-house on the strand is poorer for having banned all the interesting people in town; perhaps it speaks poorly of me that they yet allow me entrance, though I will at least avail myself of the views while I can.

My dear brother (you may remember his shoes) is celebrating the birth of his daughter in the usual way; I continue to be astonished Beatrys consented to marry him, though she has been managing to escape him and the baby often enough to visit myself and Clara. We, you will note, have not yet succumbed to the child-bearing impulses the new friar insists we all harbour within.

There is a new friar at the hostel, less talkative and (if you will believe my wife) less handsome than Daniel. More than anything he reminds me of the moralising mentor one sees in the dreadful morality plays they still show down in the city [2]; he seems the sort to dream of visiting Rome and upon arriving declare it insufficiently Christian! One supposes Lorenzo's new Machines of Helios sculpture about which you droned on at me for hours might send the good man directly into an apoplexy, which would at least be entertaining…

The new play is a mild success [3], despite the inclement weather rather betraying the fact that Alburgh is not, to my regret, Barcelon. Clara has had marvellous fun with the new fabrics and dyes; please accept my sincerest gratitude for the billrod [4], although it is far too expensive to use regularly.

I leave with you my kindest regards,

A. Mortenszen

—

[1] Records suggest Absolon's grandfather lost his wife and at least two brothers in the Second Great Dying.

[2] Presumably Copenhagen; although manuscripts of the mentioned plays have not survived, similar opinions are found in other contemporaneous letters.

[3] Mortenszen understates the matter here; his High Society was received so well that the royal family altered the route of their Grand Tour to visit Alburgh and see a performance.

[4] Literally “beetle-red”, a crimson dye produced from the shell of an insect and imported from Lower Mendeva.

/no fɛˈjo lɛts ˈʒɔjnt ˈɛntʀ i kloˈʒɔn | aˈpaʀt viˈʒɔn di maˈʒon/

23m Janver

A Borlish folk song, first attested in the early 1600s, first written down in this form in 1770. Melody may be older; evidence is inconclusive.

L'oc jorn Prinvir es avenuð
Con saphir violet e petal
Y joun d'ovegl e vacq son nað
Eð y sol accent camp a sial

De Març a Jugn e vars Decembr
Y Tar es tojorn autrant.

L'oc jorn y Stað es avenuð
Coll'aubon ne fugnt dy crevoscr
Nos, pascent sou y ciel ovart
Y bel cant all'aucel des y bosc

De Març…

L'oc jorn Harvest es avenuð
Con fougl a banfoy cercondant
Coglau y jorn, voutau y nogt
Y scuiroul sy car nouç quagçant

De Març…

L'oc jorn l'Yarn es avenuð
Con jonnovar e Reveglon
Nos nos tosteu ne majon quemant
E fruyau ny neyanç all'aubon

De Març…

##


25m Janver

aðief /aˈðjɛf/ (Damvað: [ˌaðɪˈjɛf]) enough, sufficient(ly many)

< from Old Boral að iev “enough, the same amount”, analysed as að “to” < Latin ad and iev, stem of ievar “to compare” (which became Middle Boral yevar “to measure” before falling into disuse). In fact, the Old Boral phrase is probably not a synchronic development but a direct descendant of Latin adverb adæque “equally, likewise”.

Unlike many modifiers of quantity, f.ex. plusour “several”, aðief follows its head noun.

I beyeu caffay aðief je Lun por un centuir fair svanir.
He had enough coffee on Monday to knock out an army.
/i biˈjaw kaˈfe aˈðjɛf ʒe ˈlɪn poʀ ɪn tsɛnˈtajʀ fɛʀ sfaˈnɪʀ/

##

26m Janver

from “An Abridged History of Northern Albion” by Aidan Donblane, published by Cloddale Primers, 1940 (with commentary by his daughter Anhart, 1980)

…and thus, with the aid of Harald the Blue, established the Githel Kingdom of Scotland in what had been Pictland. Records of the Picts quickly become scanty, suggesting an acceleration of the encroachment of Githel language and culture from the west, into Murray [1].

With the Northumber conquest of Eidin (modern Edinbrough) in 928, the Kingdom of Dunclothe lost the remaining vestiges of its eastern coast. However, the extended Viking in the east had left many of their eponymous Isles severely underdefended [2], and over the ensuing decades, Dunclothe under Roderick would invade many of them: first Arran in 934, and most importantly, Mann fell in 942.

While the inevitable acrimony this expansion engendered with both the Irish kingdoms and with Denmark [3] is beyond the scope of this book, we shall briefly examine its effects on Northumber, on the new Scotland, and on Wynneth to the southwest.

Relations with the Kingdom of Northumber cooled from the moment Edinbrough fell; the common enemy both states had in the Norrish (recall the conquest of Yorwick) presumably helped speed the process along. According to the relevant copies of the English Chronicle, the bride of King Radulf, who succeeded his uncle in 951, was one “Atre of Cheswic”; this is suspected to be the same town as Cumbrian “Keswick”, although whether she was a daughter of King Roderick is unclear…

—

[1] Recent research calls into question the then-prevailing theory that Alba (and in turn Scotland) only experienced significant Norrish influence during and after the eleventh-century migrations. Placename analysis (see F. Berger, 1977) has been correlated with dig sites and suggests that the kingdom had a significant Norrish subtrate from its inception.

[2] This is a simplification: the Norrish ascendancy through the ninth century and the beginning of the tenth had at last reached its peak, and politics further east (as well as the interference of the French Normans) diminished the resources that the Sodrick kings had at their disposal.

[3] That is, the various states making up what are now Denmark and Horth, and so on.

31r Janver

descombr /dɛsˈkɔmbʀ̩/ to go to bed, to lie down to sleep

fourth conjugation, -oy variant (past participle descont)

< from Old Boral de(s)combr “to recline”, from Latin dēcumbō “I lie down, I fall”. Evidence from Taglour etc. suggests the pronunciation [deˈʃɔmbr̩] was common until at least the sixteenth century, but analogy with other verbs in des-, such as descovrir “to discover” produced the modern pronuncation.

Com os tu suggerir ig jo so ja descont pre minogt ?
How dare you suggest I have ever gone to bed before midnight?
/ˈkɔm ɔz ti ˌsɪgʒeˈʀiʀ aj ʒo so ˌʒa dɛsˈkɔnt ˌpʀe miˈnɔjt/

##


1r Fevruer

Let us understand the extent to which the European languages are an incestuous foudoir “clusterfuck” by looking at four Borlish words which all derive from the single Ancestral Eurasian word ƕnōghs “finger/toenail”.

ongr (a pie) /ˈɔngʀ̩ a ˈpje/ [ˈɔŋg‿ʀa ˌpje] toenails, hooves

< Old Boral ongl' að pie with the same meaning (nails of the foot), from the Latin words ungula “nail, claw”, a diminutive of unguis (idem.), and pedem “foot”.

Jo pieð ig vostrell'ivan foln noc mell'ongr a pie.
I ask that your children don't step on my toes.
/ʒo ˈpjɛθ aj vɔˌstʀeliˈvan ˈfoln̩ ˌnɔk meˈlɔngʀ̩ a ˌpje/

ongulað /ˌɔngiˈlaθ/ [ˌɔŋgɪˈlah] hooved animal, ungulate

< late 17C, borrowed from Latin ungulātus “having hooves or claws” with influence from ongr “hoof”, or perhaps via French ongulé with the same meaning and influenced by French ongle. The Latin from ungula “claw, hoof” plus the adjectival derivation -ātus. First used as a noun in Borlish in Jan Prestr's 1780 work Classifaction of the Child-bearing Beasts.

Il saven noc ig balen son comportandessem a ongulað.
They didn't know that whales were most closely related to ungulates.
/ˌɪl saˈvɛn nɔk ˌaj baˈlɛn sɔn ˌkɔmpɔʀtanˈdɛsɛm a ˌɔngiˈlaθ/

nagl /nɛjl/ [ˈnɛjʊ] peg, dowel

< 8C, from Old Boral naȝl “nail, peg”, borrowed from Old English nægl “peg (tool for fastening), plectrum”. This from Classical German naglas “(finger)nail”, a derivation of the ancestral ƕnōghs.

Un ellips es y form traçað n'un cort boucr a dou nagl vardað tis.
An ellipse is the shape made by a pen in a taut loop attached to two pegs.
/ɪn elɪps ɛz y fɔʀm tʀatsaθ nɪn kɔʀt bucʀ̩ a du nɛjl vaʀdað tɪz/

onyx /oˈnɪks/ [ʊˈniks] onyx (gemstone), scallop (species of mussel), variety of pasta in the shape of a shell

< Seen variously in Old and Early Middle Boral as oneç, onniç, etc.; relatinised to onyx by 14C. The Latin onyx “onyx, yellowish gemstone, scallop” borrowed from Greek ὄνυξ (ónux, “nail, onyx”), from the ancestral ƕnōghs as before.

Y lagç onyx con saus a kes er collüið de bou aromað.
The scallop pasta in cheese sauce came with spiced beef.
/i ˈlɛjts oˈnɪks kɔn ˌsoz a ˈkɛz ɛʀ ˌkolaˈjɪθ de ˈbu ˌaʀoˈmaθ/

##


2m Fevruer

cordamn /kɔʀˈdamn̩/ [kɔːˈdamɐn] rigging, tackle (on a sailing vessel)

< from Mediaeval Latin cordāmen “(collection of) ropes”, from cordō “I tie up” with the nominaliser -men. Probably an independent development, unrelated to the parallel Italian derivation cordame, with the same meaning (although later confluence is probably due to contact).

Y ci annað seyon fait sur mar, i fruya soul parmy toð y cordamn.
While he was away at sea, he would dance around in the rigging.
/ˌi tsi aˈnaθ siˌjɔn ˈfet sɪʀ ˈmaʀ | i fʀaˈja ˈsul paʀˈmi ˌtɔθ i kɔʀˈdamn̩/

rouvaç /ruˈvats/ (bank) heist, robbery, stick-up

The getaway after the heist would have gone better, had any of them spoken Dutch.

##

4m Fevruer

First few paragraphs from L'Étranger, by Albert Camus. The original French may be found here. An audio recording of the below can be found here.

L'oc jorn, mama es mort. Ben parcanç yer, jo saif noc. Lonc y parol campaner meyon receut dell'edifiç : .maðr partið. .a sevelir souscen. .humour condolendessem. L'ig signfy nien, alor. L'oc pos star yer.

L'edifiç a hom veil se situ a Goday, a novant stadion de Damvað. Jo montaray sur y vectoir l'hour dou e j'hiembray l'edifiç ag ralley. Cossy jo farray surveil toð y nogt e soulor y repaðr tardessem a souscen. J'ay dou jorn crous rovað de my caf e stant y contingent i poðe noc m'en privar. Toð mod i parisce noc allagr, donc j'ay dit meðes : “A mey la no's falt.” ; i no's respos. Apres j'ay saut noc deïr le digr. Luy val y sorc me beudar, a loy. Lon hougr i le farra souscen doutr, candon i me verra a cordogl. Oy meðes, tout es un pog com ða mama no sey mort. Posc nos arreu le sevelið, malgrað, i sera un affer prent, oc a digr robað d'un accostur reallessem.

Jo so montað sur y vectoir l'hour dou. Caye magn calt. J'ay repasc fait ag tarn cas Celest, par usual. Casc gent ayen tendreç bempart por mey, e Celest a me dit : “O n'a maðr for l'un.”. Cant jo parte, il aun me colluïð vars y port. Jo so hastað com picq n'y fugnt complir, car all'hour final me fale pausar cas Emmanuel por i me prest y capel plat e manc neir. Sull'yam follau y sougl deur plusour monð.

Jo so cors fin a pie mettr ag vectoir. L'oc hast, l'oc porsief meðes, fus accougl certan coll'eç rouð peyaçant, oc puð a foyail montant, no dign oc lumner rebondiscent dy rout e dy ciel, desig tout j'ay m'assovið. A mesur dy sojorn pres enter so jo dormið. Cant apres j'ay sveilað, jo sta pressað a cost d'un soldart ; luy a m'entrovað a souriar ða j'er venuð de lontan. Jo n'ay dit for : “Vray.”, desirant besogn plu aïr de parolar.

L'edifiç es a just trey stadion dy bourg. J'ay y camin follað a pie. J'ay bel desirar mama veir direct, y porter ay me dit ig paravant fale jo rajogn con y surveilour. Dec i no sta ancour pront, j'ay l'attis un moment. Durant ig y porter a me roulað e soulor, jo so vas y surveilour encontrar ; l'a me receut ne sy scrittoir. L'es segr mogn de cavel gris, coll'insign martial sur tabr. L'a me sgardað profont d'ogl mar. All'ig nos eu man pognað, luy mell'un tenent lontemp aðief por ig jo saveus noc le recovrir.

Apres, l'a y summer avant leit eð a dit : “Domn Morsaut venoy all'oc edifiç deur trey annað. Sy redon enter foy de vos.”

Jo creye i me blasma uncos e j'ay me mis a luy explanar, ma l'a m'interront.

“Vos deyeð noc raçon forgar, bon pavr. J'ay y narrað leit de vossy maðr ; vos poðeð noc le fornir ig seyon recas. Luy fale un hom por le curar, eð untal joun ant untal empley n'a saler y hautessem. Toð cas final, i sta lonc luy meðes fort allagherrem ac.”

J'ay dit : “Oc, surveilour.”

L'es continuað con : “Vos comprenð l'au eç bon amig, eç gent de sull'eld con ig i poðe amanç communal aïr, ci d'autr annað. Vos soð joun demay e l'aus star annoyað con vos soul.

Cossy sta l'ig. Candon i hamta cas mey, mama passa ci hour ne me gasant lon viscr. Atorn y preu semman all'edifiç, i plora cos frequent. Ma just mama n'au ancour s'ig habituað. Posc plusour monð, i fos plorað ða l'er emmenað dell'edifiç. All'oc, tant, a i s'habituað. Por ig parmy n'ay jo apen le vis a mesur dell'annað derran. E tan dec le veir usaus my Sol enter ; lon ig contenent, y defy por y vectoir yembr, y moneð contar, eð y dou hour fallent ag sojorn passar.

Y surveilour a ancour me parolar, ma jo luy dona plu magn cur. Soulor l'a me dit : “Or, jo figur ig vos veleð desirar vossy maðr veir ?”

J'ay me leyað lon alcun respos e l'a me menað vars y port. Y ci scagl descendent, l'es lançað all'explanation.

“Nos eu le fis ne nossy picq mortuer. Por ig ci autr n'ain angoist may. Candon casc edifiçour fol y sougl, ci autr dienn tis par dou ben trey jorn. Certan l'ig forc novel trevagl por toð gent.”

Nos eu un gardin trasað oyon la sta magn segr veil, dignt a picq cougl. Il son tait tandic nos approismau. Portant, reðr nos, lour y collocq son relançað. Il sceusn apparir com parrochet cloistað, sou manton gros. Y surveilour es stoppað avant y port d'un picq immoubr.

“Ac vos laisc jo, Segr Morsaut. Ða vos fal uncos de mey, vos porreð me trovar ne my cambr. Nos suggereu vossy maðr sevelir l'hour deg all'aubon. Nos creyeu vos farray surveil jusc ig cossy, com vos vouð desirar. Uncos final : vossy maðr, lonc sull'amig, a parið y desir declarar an seviel religious. J'ay l'eç tal en arrancað, ma jo desira vos en addocir.”

J'ay graç luy dar. Mama, a loy de star atheos, n'aye un cogtaçon soul pos a religion sy við enter.

##


5m Fevruer

fondamen /ˌfɔndaˈmɛn/ basis, fundamentals, base, basics

also fondamen corpore /ˌkɔʀpoˈʀe/ chemical element(s), periodic table

< 15C, borrowed from Latin fundāmen “foundation, basis”, probably via French fondamen (with vowel shift by analogy with the inherited fonder “to found, to set up”). Note the final stress due to borrowing; in direct descendants we instead see scamn < exāmen “swarm”, for example. Sense in chemistry (literally, “material basis”) from early 19C, though attested in various Romance languages from the late 18C.

Fal jo t'instroy l'enter fondamen, pre tu attent uncos novel.
I must teach you the basics before you try something new.
/ˈfal ʒo tɪnˈstʀɔj lɛnˈtɛʀ ˌfɔndaˈmɛn | ˈpʀe tu aˈtɛnt ɪnˈkɔz noˈvɛl/

Y fondamen corpore n'aug son aquifex eð ignifex.
The elements found in water are hydrogen and oxygen.
/i ˌfɔndaˈmɛn ˌkɔʀpoˈʀe ˈnoj sɔn ˌakwiˈfɛks eθ ˌajniˈfɛks/

##

5m Fevruer

fondamen /ˌfɔndaˈmɛn/ basis, fundamentals, base, basics

also fondamen corpore /ˌkɔʀpoˈʀe/ chemical element(s), periodic table

< 15C, borrowed from Latin fundāmen “foundation, basis”, probably via French fondamen (with vowel shift by analogy with the inherited fonder “to found, to set up”). Note the final stress due to borrowing; in direct descendants we instead see scamn < exāmen “swarm”, for example. Sense in chemistry (literally, “material basis”) from early 19C, though attested in various Romance languages from the late 18C.

Fal jo t'instroy l'enter fondamen, pre tu attent uncos novel.
I must teach you the basics before you try something new.
/ˈfal ʒo tɪnˈstʀɔj lɛnˈtɛʀ ˌfɔndaˈmɛn | ˈpʀe tu aˈtɛnt ɪnˈkɔz noˈvɛl/

Y fondamen corpore n'aug son aquifex eð ignifex.
The elements found in water are hydrogen and oxygen.
/i ˌfɔndaˈmɛn ˌkɔʀpoˈʀe ˈnoj sɔn ˌakwiˈfɛks eθ ˌajniˈfɛks/

generally-proven

oho, elements! what elements are known already? what are their names?

  Great question! :D I imagine by, say, 1950 at latest all naturally occuring elements had been discovered (in real life the last such was rhenium in 1925), but I have only fixed the Borlish names of seventeen of them so far. Here we go:

Connuð des aubon dell'hour: / Known since antiquity:

argent - silver
aur - gold
far - iron
cuivr - copper
plom - lead
stan - tin (the above six all from Latin)
azauc - mercury (from Morrove Arabic via Portingale azouco)
brunstan - brimstone [1] (from Old English brynstān “sulfur, lit. burn-stone”, often incorrectly linked to stan “tin”.

Aer: / Airs: [2]

aquifex - aquifex [3], from Scholastic Latin aquifex “water-maker”.
coronal - coronal [4], from Latin corōnālis “of the crown”, referring to the corona of the Sun, where it was first detected.

ignifex - ignifex [5], from S. Latin īgnifex “fire-maker”.

muria - muria [6], from Latin muria “brine, salt liquor”, via muriatic acid.

xanthal - xanthal [7], from Ancient Greek ξᾰνθός (xanthós) “yellow, golden”, with ending by analogy with coronal.

Clað dur: / Stiffs [8]:

adamant - adamant [9], from Latin adamans “unbreakable, hard as steel”.
jast - jast [10], from S. Latin [1] iastum “zinc”, originally from Hindi जस्ता (jastā) “zinc”.
machovin - machovine [11], named for the Machow mine in Poland near where it was first isolated.
natron - natron [12], from A. Greek νίτρον (nítron) “nitre” via Arabic and French, among others.

—

[1] i.e., sulphur. [2] Gases. [3-7] hydrogen, helium, oxygen, chlorine, neon.

[8] Solids. [9-12] titanium, zinc, strontium, sodium

##


6m Fevruer

fler /flɛʀ/ smell, scent, odour

< 14C, borrowed from early Middle French flair “odour, smell (especially in hunting)”, a deverbal of flairier “to smell of”, from (with dissimilation) Latin frāgrō “I reek of”. Any connection to Latin flagrō “I burn, I blaze” is disputed. Often used in the construction fler aïr a “to smell of”.

Y roum enter au fler a cendr eð acier e cogr.
The whole space smelt of ash and steel and leather.
/i ˈʀum ɛnˈtɛʀ o ˈflɛʀ a ˈtsɛndʀ̩ ɛ‿ðaˈtsjɛʀ e ˈkɔjʀ̩/

7m Fevruer
Y Preyer “Nossy Paðr” - The Lord's Prayer

In the modern day, this is one of the most widely-used translations of the Lord's Prayer in Borlish churches, coming from a popular sixteenth-century Bible translation.

Nossy Paðr stant ne ciel
/ˈnɔstʀ‿i ˈpaðʀ̩ ˈstant ne ˈtsjɛl/
sagntfiað sey ty nom.
/sɛjntˈfjaθ ˈsi ti ˈnɔm/
Vin ty reyaum, advin ty grað
/ˈvɪn ti ʀiˈjom | adˈvɪn ti ˈgʀaθ/
cossy ne tar e ne ciel.
/koˈsi ne ˈtaʀ e ne ˈtsjɛl/
Don nos l'oc jorn nossy pan cottidian
/ˈdɔn no lɔk ˈʒɔʀn ˈnɔstʀ‿i ˈpan ˌkotiˈdjan/
e pardon nos nossy deut*
/a paʀˈdɔn no ˈnɔstʀ‿i ˈdawt/
com nos lou pardau nos deyent.
/kɔm ˈno lu paʀˈdo no diˈjɛnt/
E dug nos noc vars tentation
/e ˈdaj no ˈnɔk vaʀz ˌtɛntaˈtsjɔn/
ma descaðen nos de mal.
/pi ˌdɛskaˈðɛn no de ˈmal/

The doxology appears in most mainstream Borland churches:

Ke* tien* es y regn, y possenç eð y gloir, siecr siempr ; Amen.
/ke ˈtjɛn ɛz i ˈʀijn | i poˈsɛnts ɛθ i ˈglɔjʀ | ˈsjekʀ̩ ˈsjɛmpʀ̩ ‖ aˈmɛn/

*These words are archaic or obsolete in their contemporary definitions in Modern Borlish:

deut “sin” < past participle of deïr “to owe”. Displaced by synonym peccað, and now usually restricted to adjectival senses “due, owed, necessary”.
ke “for, since, because” < from Latin quia with the same meaning. Already old-fashioned at the time this was written, it now survives only in certain expressions. 
tien “thine, yours” < formed after mien “mine” < Latin meum, now also obsolete. The modern construction would be tell'un “your one”.

8m Fevruer

sauð /soθ/ ewe, female sheep, nanny goat

see also kes de sauð goat's cheese

< 10C, from Old Boral sauð, sawð from Old Nose sauðr “sheep”, borrowed along with veð < veðr “ram”.

Nos vasm tras y camp, genoil dessou parmy sauð e lour agnel.
We crossed the field, knee-deep in ewes and their lambs.
/no ˈvazm̩ ˌtʀaz i ˈkamp | ʒeˈnɔjl deˈsu ˌpaʀmi ˈsoθ e ˌluʀ ɛjˈnɛl/

9m Fevruer

cainç a ney d'aost /ˈkents a ˈni daˈɔst/ fortuitous coincidence, once-in-a-blue-moon chance

< first attested 18C, literally “(an) August snow event”, usually referring to a positive occurence. The first word analysable synchronically as cair “fall, happen” plus the nominaliser -nç, but probably just directly from Late Latin cadentia “result, occurence”. The other words are simple descendants of Latin nivem “snow” and augustus “August”.

Nossy nuçal ant regnbou doblað ny ciel sta un cainç a ney d'aost.
Our wedding having a double rainbow in the sky was a happy coincidence.
/ˈnɔstʀ i niˈtsal ant ʀijmˈbu doˈblaθ ni ˈtsjɛl sta ɪn ˈkents a ˈni daˈɔst/

##

10m Fevruer

in translation from An Overview of Revitalist Art in Sixteenth-Century Rome, by Safourian Amigòt, published 1870 by Prindor Nicês

By 1550, Rome and the surrounding Italian states had for the most part played catch-up over the last century, with respect to the study of Classical mathematics and science. Having neither the Novomundine explorations of Vascony, Kernow and Morrack to fund patronage of the new inventors, nor the very new medical advances in Denmark (that is, in the Dane Concert) to incite renewed interrogation of Classical medical texts, the various Italian states were further disadvantaged by their unchecked monarchs; and of course, nowhere more so than Rome herself, seat of a papacy that at the time still governed the spritual lives of most of Europe south of Venise.

This is not the place to discuss the causes and immediate political effects of the Great Fracture; for our purposes it suffices to note that, while on paper the Pope in Rome remained an absolute ruler (along with political dominion over several smaller Italian and German provinces), his temporal powers were in practice much diminished. In particular, the venerable prohibitions over blasphemous arts and philosophical publications within the city limits were swftly dissolved by the new Triunvirato, who ordered the obtention of great minds from around Europe; for example, this led to the establishment of the first Brethin House in all of Italy. They undertook this course of action, one assumes, less out of a sincere zeal for scientific discovery (although the personal correspondence of Lord of Houses Josforo Desmono suggests a passing interest in the subject) than a wish to introduce various new political factions into Rome in opposition to the See.

The influx of artists surrounded by scholars of antiquity led to an outpouring of artworks taking inspiration from the great legends of the Greek and Roman pantheons, dubbed in retrospect the Rivitalisto period. Apart from the many paintings and stories (usually retellings of previous myths), probably the most well-known work from this time is I Macchine di Helio (The Machines of Helios) by sculptor Lorenzo dell'Acchedia, a stylised depiction of Helios the Sun and the six known planets gyring around him. The question of exactly where Lorenzo heard about the new astronomical theory remains open: he certainly had enough opportunity in the various city fora…

tegndr /tijndʀ̩/ to soak, to dye, to affect, to influence

< attested as early as 7C in Old Boral as teȝnr et al. “to dye, to immerse”, from Latin tingō “I wet, moisten, tinge” (with possible influence from the derivation intingō “I plunge, saturate”). Metaphorical senses in evidence from 13C.

My ci fraðr e mey tegnsm toð my camis ne roug fosc.

/ˈmy tsi ˈfʀaðʀ̩ e ˈmi ˈtijnzm̩ ˈtɔθ mi kaˈmɪz ne ʀuj ˈfɔx/

12m Fevruer

sevrar /seˈvʀaʀ/ [sɪˈvʀɑː] to separate, to split up, to wean (a child)

also mission sevrant /miˈsjɔn seˈvʀant/ injective function, one-to-one map

< 11C; from Vulgar Latin sēperō “I divide, I distinguish”, an alteration of Latin sēparō with the same meaning. Possibly via Old French sevrer “to separate, to sever”.

Sense in mathematics from 1830, in the fundamental work of mathematician Marc Ausbagn, possibly influenced by the existing term mission covrent “surjection”, a translation of the Kentish “covrend (map)”.

Un mission sevrant sevr y sunt ; y ci destegnt accaçað a destegnt.
An injective function separates; distinct inputs are sent to distinct outputs.
/ɪn miˈsjɔn seˈvʀant ˈsevʀ‿i ˈsɪnt | i ˈtsi dɛsˈtijnt ˌakaˈtsaθ a dɛsˈtijnt/

##


13m Fevruer

The bookseller of Hawthorn Street was a handsome man of perhaps thirty years, and the first time Frederick met him, on a balmy summer evening in 1755, he had (by dint of some quick gossip with his sister) learnt two things about the man: first, he sold books of a higher quality and a lower price than anyone else in Sowbruck [1], or maybe even all of London, and yet always seemed as impeccably maintained as any professional in the borough (despite the reportedly small size of his household); and second, he would vanish for days at a time with little warning and still less in the way of excuses.

Fred had been privately convinced that the man was discreetly employed by the Lord Mayor's office, or by one of the better-concealed deparments of Her Majesty's administration - though bookseller seemed an odd role to foist upon a spy.

taken from the tale A Door, Once Opened by Clarissa Bellamy, published 1920 by Sagittarius Editions

Mr Talbot: Your most recent book has proven remarkably popular, especially for what many would argue is a parachthon [2] story, a genre which hasn't seen much critical success in recent years. It is also somewhat of a departure for yourself, having published primarily in the lovetale [romance] market until now. What inspired you to write this book?
Mrs Bellamy: In some ways I see it as a natural extension of the character development I had worked on before. Really, I had wanted for a while to read para-stories which focus on how people react to the fantastic around them, rather than great battles and politics and mysterious xenozones [aliens]. In the end my husband simply asked “Well then, why don't you write one yourself?”. So I did.

Mr Talbot: You have mentioned that the setting required a lot of research to portray accurately. What drew you to the London of the late eighteenth century?
Mrs Bellamy: I must continually thank my sister-in-law Heleth, who is a historical scholar at the New Lyceum; her interest and counsel were both invaluable to me. Though she focuses more on the Democratic Wars themselves, I found myself more interested in the aftermath, with London a newly independent state, surrounded by uneasy allies and vulture-like enemies, waiting for a single misstep.

Mr Talbot: How would you describe the main moral or theme of your work?
Mrs Bellamy: The most apparent image to me, as I was writing the trevold [novel], was about reciprocity, the idea that any relationship between two people, two places - or indeed two times - is a mutual one. In fact, the title only came after I had finished the first draft; there's a line in one of Davis' plays that goes “A door, once opened, can be stepped through in either direction”. Aside from referring directly to some of the tale's events, of course, I think that quote is emblematic of the relationship between Frederick and the bookseller.

questions from a 1921 colloquium [interview] with writer Mrs Clarissa Bellamy for the Trevold Weekly

—
[1] A borough in London, in much the same area as Southwark.
[2] A genre of writing including most of what we would call fantasy and science-fiction, although the central examples here tend to involve a secondary world of some description. The first story usually attributed to the genre is Saxon writer Elsebeth Sneider's Through a Black Mirror (about which see the 11th January post).

##

16m Fevruer

from The Story of Borland (Y Stoir d'Istr Boral), a school history textbook published 1982 by Cordin Editions

Let us compare how the fall of Rome affected Borland and Britain (when we talk about this part of history, remember that Britain refers to all of Albion, not just the Briton states, and not all of them, either). The end of Roman life in Britain occurred quickly during the first three decades of the fifth century. By contrast, although a lot of people did leave Borland for the continent during this time, it seems like the way Borland was a little more independent from the rest of the Empire helped it. In fact, there is recent evidence that some Romans left Britain to Borland, especially from the major cities of Londinium [A19] and Camulodunum [Ð17] (now London and the Merch town of Conster).

At this time, Germanic people, particularly the Angles and Saxons, came from the mainland of Europe and settled in Borland and Britain. In Britain there was a complete shift away from Roman culture over the fifth and sixth century; the Angles established East England [D15] and Northumber [B8] in the east, while the Saxons founded Wessex and Markland in the west.

The story in Borland is similar, but less drastic. Much of the south and west coast was settled by Angle immigrants through the fifth century, where they founded Angland [J11], Southbar [L13] and several more minor kingdoms. At the same time, the informal and decentralised political structures throughout the self-described Roman towns was changing to adapt to the new condition. Several towns in the south were abandoned completely to emigration or assimilation into the Angle kingdoms, and most of the more urbanised east was unified under the rule of Prase Victor Rossetus as the dioces boralis, the Diocese of Boral.

##


17m Fevruer

benoçar /ˌbenoˈtsaʀ/ [ˌbenʊˈtsɑː] to use, to make use of, to employ

< from Old English benotian “to use, to consume”, attested 10C in Old Boral as benotiar etc. and originally used primarily in religious/charitable contexts. It came to mean “to make use of (a service)” by 13C and soon after “to make use of (a tool)”, partially displacing native usar < Mediæval Latin ūsō “I use”.

Il figuran ig tu vel l'aïðanç benoçar.
They thought you could use the help.
/ɪl ˌfigiˈʀan aj ˈti vɛl ˌlɛjiˈðants ˌbenoˈtsaʀ/

/ʀoˈbaθ paˌlakɔsˈtɪʀ pʀɛsˈtʀal | niˈkɔl me ˌʀaniˈðo ˌkɔm saˈlɪθ ˌne paˈsant/

##

19m Fevruer

text from a steeplecard [1], sent on the 3rd of March 1822 and addressed to Matilda Torricks

Dearest sister Matty -

Wishing you a belated new years [2]. Office closed until this morning with the snow and the municipal holiday. Jane says I am to be secluded for at least a fortnight hence with the baby imminent. Anxious to be presentable for the melee [3] before the 75th anniversary celebrations start next month. We hear the whole south Tamside is to be transformed.

Do visit soon. Ebbasham [4] is not so distant after all.

- Your sister Vallie

excerpt: The Communication Toolset (El Herramente de Communicazòn) by Joan Bensaíd, published 1951 by Quaterno Theorético a Cordova

In the usual way of things, multiple nations claim the title of the first steeplemesh [5]. The very earliest incarnations of the idea arose during the Democratic Wars of the mid-eighteenth century, but these tended to be single lines of temporary signal towers set up to span terrain that made it too costly to simply send a rider with a message. Moreover, these were set up only to transmit simple messages; for example, an hour to launch an attack.

There were two major developments required for the first true steeplemeshes. First, the means of communication. Early wartime towers usually relied on lampfire and thus were reliable only at night, or else a collection of patterned flags, which were usable at day but required a shorter distance between watchtowers [6]. The two-arm system, allowing up to nine separate states to be transmitted, was probably first tested in the Willem town of Thynen [7], where the church's weathervane was reportedly commandeered to send the first lines of the Pater Noster to the neighbouring town Bost. (Hence, probably, the name “steeplepost”.)

Second, the encoding of a true alphabet, rather than a fixed sets of messages. One early Andalusian attempt (from Cordova to Málaga, usually given as the first line connecting two major cities, and essentially just formalising the signal system that had existed for the duration of their conflict with Morrack [8]) used a variant of the ancient Polybius square as discussed in Chapter 3, adapted for Arabic script. The first modern steeplescript was developed in Cracow [9] by engineer Sigmund Adamich and his son Andrei as part of the royal initiative to connect Cracow to Bitom, using a set of four symbols, traditionally referred to as “dot, dash, bar and hook”.

—–

[1] Very much like a telegram, although somewhat less efficiently terse.

[2] The year in this world begins in March, not January, as was the case in ours across much of Europe until the sixteenth century.

[3] A high-society term for “the ton”, or “Society”; that is, the gentry.

[4] A small town within the borders of independent London, on the site of real-life Epsom.

[5] An optical telegraphy network.

[6] Compare our semaphore system for boats.

[7] Tienen, Belgium. [8] Morroco. [9] Kraków.

20m Fevruer

enclinar /ˌɛnkliˈnaʀ/ to tip, to lean, to tilt (transitive)

also to shock, to startle; (military) to rout, to make retreat

[For intransitive senses of all definitions, use s'enclinar.]

< attested as enclinar et al. in Old Boral with most of the modern meanings, along with “to bend; to assuage; to dispel (disease)”. The word descends transparently from Latin inclīnō “I tilt, etc.”.  The generic meaning “to shock” is in evidence from at least 14C.

L'enclina sy quil por y caf se pon pieç polç dessur.
He would tilt the head of his bed up a couple of inches.
/ˌlɛnkliˈna si ˈkwɪl pɔʀ i ˈkaf se ˈ̟pɔn pjɛts ˈpɔlts deˈsɪʀ/

21r Fevruer

affis /aˈfɪz/ tip, gratuity, service fee

< literally “attached”; past participle of affigr “to attach, to add, to append”, attested in this usage from 18C. The verb through Old Boral afiȝr “to affix, to fasten onto” descends from Latin affīgō “I fix to, I imprint on”.

Nos parsoloim y quelt e laiscaum un affis munfiant.

##

22m Fevruer

from The Story of Borland (Y Stoir d'Istr Boral), a school history textbook published 1982 by Hendric Tambour et al. for Cordin Editions

There was a steady influx of Christians into the Diocese of Boral (and later the First Kingdom of Borland) from the latter years of the fifth century, well into the seventh. Early on many of these were Roman or even Celtic Christians displaced by the Germanic migrations, and by the time of Pope Mercury II in 590N, Rome had taken a particular strategic influence in the small kingdom, which at the time was on the defensive against a stronger Southbar [1].

In the first instance, the new Kingdom was viewed as a vital Christian outpost from which Rome could send missionaries east and west to aid the conversion of Albion and the Danes. Thus the early Borlish monasteries and schools of theology were much more outward-focused than the comparatively insular Irish groups. It was to this end that the Golfhaun sanctuary was first erected (with the Latin name Asylum Æstuarii [2], which contains the oldest surviving church building in Borland.

The opinions of the Papacy regarding Borland were as inconstant as the Italian lands it claimed and the men themselves. Many also saw the distant land as a convenient way to make sure inconvenient elements were far away and unable to make trouble. In this way, any given Borland monastery might house semi-heretical monks “entrusted with the conversion of the pagans”, or perhaps several awkwardly-Hellenic men with a suspicious sympathy to one of the Greek heresies.

—-

[1] A medieval Anglian kingdom located along the south coast of Borland.

[2] The Estuary Haven, since it is situated on the large island Nestu in the Labbað Sound (upriver of which lies the capital Damvað).

29m Fevruer

sautellar /ˌsoteˈlaʀ/ to hop, to jump a small distance (especially on one leg)

also of animals to bounce, to bound, to jump

< 15C, verbal derivation of sautel “hop, bounce”, diminutive of saut “jump”, descendant of Latin saltus “jump, leap”. Extends by 19C to any short and quick journey, particularly between two similar places.

Par vec se poð sautellar a Ausçagm con tor dou pavar soul.
/paʀ ˈvɛk se poθ ˌsoteˈlaʀ a oˈsɛjm kɔn tor ˈdu paˈvaʀ ˈsul/
You can pop over to Ausçagm by bus for like less than a quid.

##


1r Març

kestar /keˈstaʀ/ [kɪˈstɑː] to beg for goods or money, to be destitute

also kestour beggar, panhandler

< 9C from Old Boral kestar, quastar “to search for” (primarily in northern dialects), from Late Latin quæstō “I seek, I pursue”, from past participle quæstus of Latin quærō “I seek”. Modern sense in use from 13C; sense of “to look for” displaced by similar verb carr.

Ne spien il dinn noc kestant la sey calscon, sy paðr au un majon caut a Londr.
/ne ˈspjɛn ɪl ˈdɪnn nɔk keˈstant la ˌsi kalˈxɔn | si ˈpaðʀ‿o ɪn maˈʒɔn kot a ˈlɔndʀ̩/
So that they wouldn't be ruined no matter what, his father bought a London house.

##

2m Març

from The Latin Republics: Jewels under Tyrian Standards by Jacob Trelaune, published by the Yievle Brethin Primers, 1902

Part VI: The Republic of Provence

It would be ahistorical to claim that the republics of the Middlesea were brought about due to the unusually totalist monarchies they neighboured, or even to stake out the somewhat more popular position that those Italian monarchies grew so tyrannical as a reaction to the republics. Nonetheless, one case in which the link between republicanism and totalism is undeniable is with the Republic of Provence.

In the first half of the second millenium, Provence was first a county in the West Francian Kingdom, and following the Emergency at Dijon, the largest province (so to speak) in the Kingdom of Burgundy. The county was elevated under the house of Savoy to a duchy in the thirteenth century, and as the direct link between the capital at Geneva and all of Middlesea sea-trade, it became known as lo panier de Borgogne (the Burgundian breadbasket).

However, with the Novamundine landfall in 1471, and the accompanying Ascendancy of Vascony under Ambrose III, piracy (which had long plagued the very coast which made Provence so profitable, and which was the scourge of the venerable republics of Jeans and Venise, among others) thrived off the general prosperity. In Marsella, the raiding became so frequent that many treated it more as an occupation, and many of the wealthy fled to safer pastures inland.
 
The aid requests presented to Rudolf VIII went largely unanswered. Charitably, one might say he was distracted by the ongoing Bavarn conflict, and the religious difficulty attendant to being the northwesternmost polity yet answerable to Rome. But it is generally allowed that the young king was simply concerned only with himself (or by extension his many lovers) and with resplendent lakeside banquets.

Things came to a head in the last years of the fifteenth century. Lord Daniel Menton of the house of Amigòt had secured the aid of King Marc V of France (France having finally reached reconciliation with Normandy and avoiding the tangle that was the Albion Wars of Fealty), on the advice of the foundling-general Guillen daus Sanz. Thus prepared, he and several other noble families declared independence from Geneva on the twenty-first of June, 1497 (which to this day is a national holiday in Provence). Though this declaration would not be formally recognised until Burgundy's defeat two years later at the Battle of Margès, it was this event which cemented House Amigòt's position as primus inter pares in the new republic.

At least, that is, for the next eighteen years…

8m Març

“For surely the Lord has not in his infinite majesty ordained Rome, the ancient seat of the Christ-killers themselves, to be the most holy city in this world? Heaven is high above and the Holy Land is yet distant, to the clockmaker [1] as it is to the pilgrim's steps.”

Wilhelm Bruez, mayor of Augsburg, to his close friend Paul von Burchardt in June 1225 (quoted from the historical film With the North Star to Guide Us, which was released in odyssey [cinemas] 1961)

—

…Bruez's statue outside the famed Rathaus.

The years between Pope Paul I/XI's (Whether or not to include the predecessor Roman Popes, or indeed some other count including any of the antipope lines, was a controversial issue for much of the thirteenth century, so both appellations are seen. The reformist Damien I established that they should not be included, although Paul himself usually did.) first announcing the establishment of a sedes sanctissima in Augsburg and the eventual capitulation of Rome are frustratingly ill-documented, for obvious reasons. Nonetheless, we can reconstruct much of the sequence of events from outside commentary, such as the various Brethin Houses [2] to the west, and the exilic Lombard accounts among the Hellenes.

As we must start the analysis somewhere, let us begin with the 1210 declaration of a renewed Crusade for Jerusalem. It is nowadays usually acknowledged that Pope Just was persuaded to call the new crusade by King…

included in an 1901 theological edition [publication] of Proclamatio Divior, the scholastic writing "Comparing the German Secession to the Great Fracture [3]; how the Final Antipope emerged from a popular reaction to the Failed Crusade, and how to understand his successors' reactions to Rome in the sixteenth century."

—

[1] An anachronistic analogy, as the first clockmakers would not appear in Europe for another century (and not in the German states for several decades more).

[2] An originally-Celtic Christian order of monks and nuns, dedicated to the documentation and preservation of history and biography.

[3] An event in the mid-sixteenth century that greatly weakened the Pope's temporal power in the states the Papacy had hitherto ruled directly.
