11m Març

extract from Borlish author Carl Havatr's Identað Partenent “A Question of Identity”, published 1981 by Œcumen Azur and translated into English 1986 by Joseph Cook.

Y ci ogl a Luc Osgaut vain cos sot tras y lumner nievrous all'oc fest. Ac afont cay sombr, plen d'ombr, e calt demay comoscon - alor jamay broucau jo allagr un jalicq portant. La no m'es colour aðief.
Lucas Osgood's eyes are soft and foolish in the gauze-light at the party. It's dim in here - shadowy - and a trifle too warm, but then I never did like wearing a jalick suit [tuxedo]. Not enough colour for me.

L'es segr magnfiant, ant visaç lon alcun cur l'oc jorn toð cas ; ne spiant exact vos porreð y ci folt veir a cost de sull'ogl, un lign entaccant veir sy laur parlocant. Y soury dað es idiot cos minutious.
He's a handsome man, face unlined by cares, at least tonight - if you look close you can see the creases at the corners of his eyes, the slight mar of a line on either side of his lips. His smile is carefully stupid.

Ma j'ay lontemp zest aut, ig Luc Osgaut a dou character sevrað. Frequent es i y fol avant l'aucoum eð un scandal jacquottað, ma Osgaut Corporal es parfait gournað e profit faint demay por no star for y molin moneð prement y við a libertin soutenent. Sey l'a astuçour y starchessem acatabr ja raðunað - l'a vray Agatha Rat, bempart valent ne bon mont - ben l'idiocy e masq.
But I've suspected for a long time that Lucas Osgood is two people. He's been a fool in public and a scandal often enough, but Osgood Corporal is too tightly-run, too profitable to be the money-making machine that feeds a libertine's habits. Either he has the best advisors any man ever assembled - he does have Agatha Ratte, which ought to count for a lot - or the stupidity is a mask.

Por ig me pon jo ac, or.
Which is why I'm here, really.

Alcun hom sceurn jamay Osgaut faucar - alcun hom a y lacq idiot penetrað dessou. Parcanç la no's nien a trovar, l'oc es fort plausibr. Me parisc attendr es prouf dy soldart por collocquour ne Vanda, ma mey jo so pavr a Arcady, eð ac ageu nos un pog different. Brutal a bas, ne mejan certan toð cas.
Nobody's ever been able to crack Osgood – nobody's gotten under the idiot lacker. There might be nothing underneath, for all we know. I suspect it's a rite of passage for Vandham reporters, but I'm an Arcadian boy, and we do things a little differently here. Less brutally, at least in some ways.

E jo so magn feliç, jo crey.
And my luck is very good, I think.

< from 8C as Old Boral vaȝar “to wander” (possibly a dialect form with uncharacteristic retention of intervocalic /ɣ/ from Latin <g>), from Latin vagō “I wander”. 14m Març

excerpts in translation from the diary of Nadàlla fi Carol, daughter of the town reverend in Santrafew, an early trading post and latterly a significant settlement in the territory of New Provence.

1590 Nascentiæ

Monday 17th April

Penelope told me this afternoon that there's a nobleman at the docks' lodginghouse, some French fop who's run away from his grandmother. Honestly I'm surprised he survived the crossing; not every such man would last two months with Marco's crew, especially with their new Libyan friends. They'd never have let him hide from work belowdecks, no matter how generous the bribe he gave them for discreet passage…

…moreover, said grandmother is allegedly the Countess [1] d'Avosche! One cannot imagine the scandal of the heir apparent's flight (for he was, we are to understand, to inherit everything) has not ruined her influence over King David's court. No-one has any idea why this mysterious newcomer should have exchanged a chasteau on the Rhin for our humble blankpine rooms on the Becouin.

Wednesday 19th April

Along with the usual bevy of whichever goods the Concert wishes to foist upon the Mastoisèt (Father believes nothing will do as much good for our diplomacy as sending more bronze and iron, or at least more men to mine and smith any we can dig up for ourselves), Marco was good enough to stockpile the Marsella weekly papers, so we have news of the old country for the first time all year. Penelope wants me to read them all to her properly tomorrow morning, but a first glance suggests at least that our heretical cousins haven't yet found themselves on the wrong end of a short rope…

[1] This is Nadàlla's mistake; Avosche was by this time a duchy.

fold /fɔld/ crease, fold, wrinkle

also turning point, waypoint; in literature, plot twist

parlocar /ˌpaʀ.loˈkaʀ/ [ˌpɑː.lʊˈkɑː] to straddle, to be either side of

< 15C, formed from prefix par- “by, through”, from Latin prefix per-, and from Latin locō “I put, I place”. Although no contemporary verb perlocō is attested, Scholastic Latin had by 1500 readopted it from these later compounds; cf. French parloquer, Vascon pellocar.

"Ouch. Like being born in Marsella at midsummer.”

“Didn't they just build a replica of the Ambrosian Steeplepost Tapestry for its two hundredth anniversary? I saw that it took over the local mesh that afternoon, and Mum said one of her friends helped with the construction.”

“It's not just that, obviously, or it wouldn't have been such a big deal! You see, there's a whole story with the canals…”

< 17C, literally “soldier's test”. 23m Març

from Handbook of Natural Theory, by Jason Ridley and others, published 1967 in Markland English by the Lincon Collegiate Editors

In mechanics, œculux (commonly referred to simply as light) is a form of propagant lux that carries energy through the barrick extent, most commonly experienced as light or heat. In vacuo, œculux moves at the normal speed, commonly written œ; in more common units, at a rate of 300 dichilomecon per second.

The word œculux is a chimer (to the certain dismay of our more classically-minded readers!): the first half is related to such words as œcumenical “of the secular world”, and here is intended to denote “universal, the entire extent of”. However, its origin is in fact the Greek οἰκουμενικός “the whole world”, literally “the inhabited places”. The suffix -lux, coming directly from Latin lux “light”, is more familiar, perhaps from the new threshold mills and their unsettling martial applications. It is important to keep in mind that luxes aren't just the scary invisible rays from a blacklayer film, but include all the colours of the rainbow.

We conventionally grade œculux by its period, since we interact with it in many different ways.

Light with the shortest periods are scattering, having enough energy to interact with atoms and give them a deficit of plenty; we say the atom becomes deficient. All shortform and penetrating œculux is scattering, and depending on convention some further surblavic light may be included. The maximal period of light which can in theory scatter an atomic base is around 320 trihekimecon, which is still shorter than any visible light. High exposure to light of such brief period is dangerous, and fortunately the atmosphere blocks the majority of such luxes that the Sun emits (although not all, as a sunburn will attest!).

##

24m Març

machinal /ˌma.kiˈnal/ mechanical, automatic

< 16C, literally “done by machine”, from machin “machine, engine” + adjectival -al. Noun borrowed 14C from Latin māchina “machine, plan”. Alternatively, directly from Latin māchinālis “of machines”, although the semantic shift is less plausible.

Sy respos sta monoton e machinal, ogl fis a paið.

His reply was monotone and automatic, eyes downturned.

/si ʀɛsˈpɔz sta ˌmo.noˈtɔn e ˌma.kiˈnal | ˈɔjl fɪz a ˈpeθ/

##


25m Març

calm /kalm/ [kawm] still, immobile, (archaic) calm

also calmar to still, to stop moving

< late 14C, borrowed from Italian calmo, calmare via Old French calme, calmer (or possibly via some Spanish intermediate). Originally only a noun calma “calm, stillness”, from Greek καῦμα (kaûma) “heat, burning”. The semantic shift appears to be “burning > midday heat > rest during the midday heat”, cf. Late Latin verb caumō “I rest in the shade”.

Note calmar does not mean “to calm” as many cognates do (for example, French calmer); for that sense, use keðar < quiētō).

I resgardau e vis y tarnour ponent e calm a cost y sougl.

/i ˌʀɛzgaʀˈdo e ˈvɪz i taʀˈnuʀ ˈponɛnt e ˈkalm a ˈkɔst i ˈsujl/

He looked back and saw the innkeeper standing still by the door.

26m Març

translation into Borlish and into West Vascon (León) of the famously-circuitous first sentence of A Door, Once Opened by Clarissa Bellamy, published 1920 by Sagittarius Editions. Translated into Borlish by Gonnilt Dispað, and into West Vascon by García Ramires.

Y livrer a Rout Hawthorn sta segr formous parcanç trent annað eld, eð y preu veg oyon Frederick l'encontrau, un crevoscr preuç ny stað de 1755, ja l'au (a mesur de raut rouðar con sy sour) dou vertað appris dy segr : y primer, ig i vende livr d'un qualtað hautessem e noc obstroint un preç boncoppessem a toð hom autr ne Sowbruck, ben Londr meðes, ma parisce a sgart calscon soutenuð cos immachiabr com casc vocationnað dy muncef (degnant ig lonc y mont sy seut majon fosn picq) ; y doussem, ig i sperisce par ci jorn plusour con prevart magr e fort men por explanation.

El lirrero a Cay Hawthorn yera un hombre appuesto que tenía trienta ańos quisá, y quando Frederick attopólo a primera vez, una nochina suave de verano 1755, savía pueste un rumoscitino con su ermana dos hechos sorre el hombre : primer loar, vendía lirros de mayor calidá y stesso de menor precio que hace nadie a Sowbruck, o bien todo Londre, pero conducíase tan immacquiablemente como todos los vocazonistes del coceyo (malla su casa fuera pequina) ; segundo loar, sumíase días seguidas casi espontáneamente y a fondo sin pretexto.

The bookseller of Hawthorn Street was a handsome man of perhaps thirty years, and the first time Frederick met him, on a balmy summer evening in 1755, he had (by dint of some quick gossip with his sister) learnt two things about the man: first, he sold books of a higher quality and a lower price than anyone else in Sowbruck, or maybe even all of London, and yet always seemed as impeccably maintained as any professional in the borough (despite the reportedly small size of his household); and second, he would vanish for days at a time with little warning and still less in the way of excuses.

…will at least suffice to discuss the transformation of the Order of Saint Hemma of Still Water, commonly the Convoy Australier, from a glorified band of mercenaries to one of the principal military forces under Roman control.

Despite the name history has given the Failed Crusade of the early thirteenth century, the Christian states in the Holy Land were in some ways more secure in their position than they had hitherto been. Nonetheless, it is irrefutable that Pope Just revoking the Convoy Australier's rulership of Antioch and Port Simon in 1208 saved the order a great amount of trouble in the long run. Their subsequent assignment to the courts of Svonimir of Crovatia and Taxon of Hungary meant they were the natural choice to lead the Vatican's grand project to bring Christianity to the Far North, even as the German troubles began to stir…

1r Avril

    Excerpt from The Man From Everywhere (El Hombre de Jallondes) by Estevan Mazon, published 1893 and translated into Borlish 1901 by Michæl Norman

Marc cossirau ða luy val sortir. All'oc pognt i cogleu y bottel avant David ne sy man, e le sarveu por y veðr noc-a-vin emplir avant luy meðes.
Mark considered walking out. Instead he picked up the bottle in front of David and used it to fill the empty not-a-wine-glass in front of him.

David se leyau un sorcegl. “Jo save noc alcohol te toc.”
David's eyebrow lifted. “I didn't know alcohol affected you.”

“I le fay noc,” respos Marc n'engolliscent con gourf lað dy vin. “Pu parveg j'am fort en parformar.”
“It doesn't,” Mark answered and took a hearty mouthful of the wine. “But sometimes I like to pretend.”

Un ricot. Bas, e sincer cos souprendent. “Ða tu desir en parformar, beu lentessem, fros un pog ty laur, figur tu osc y gost, ma noc tan com tu l'ador.”
A laugh. Low and surprisingly earnest. “If you want to pretend, drink slower, pucker your lips a little, pretend you hate the taste, but not as much as you love it.”

“Lonc ty parol tu en a experiç.”
“You sound like you speak from experience.”

“Oc, jo so dou man por m'eurar faus,” promis David. “Par outr, cos allagr, jo so tan dou man por m'eurar ne realtað.”
“I am an expert on pretending to get drunk,” David promised. “And, as it happens, I am also an expert on getting drunk.”

5m Avril

melle /meˈle/ high society, good company, the activities of the rich and powerful

< Borrowed 15C from Middle French meslee “brawl, confused fight, mixture” variably as mesle, melley, etc. (whether the orthographic <s> which had already been lost in French pronuncation was ever pronounced in Boral is debated). The French itself from mesler “to mix”, from Vulgar Latin misculō, a variant of misceō “I mix”. Original sense of “brawl, tournament (especially one of hand-to-hand combat)” ameliorates to “social gathering, party (in particular, of the aristocracy)” by the late 17C. Modern sense probably influenced by Kentish melee, a later borrowing popular in London in the decades following the Democratic Wars and its independence.

    Nascra l'ivan tost aðief por jo vel y melle rassistr l'oc saçon ?

    Shall the baby arrive soon enough that I might rejoin society this season?

    /naxˈʀa liˈvan tɔst aˈðjef pɔʀ ʒo ˈvɛl y meˈle ʀaˈsɪs.tʀ̩ lɔk saˈtsɔn/

also noðriçar /ˌnɔð.ʀiˈtsaʀ/ [ˌnɔ.ʀɪˈtsɑː] to babysit, to nurse, to nanny

< late 12C as nodriç and others (with the same meaning), either directly from Latin nūtrīx, nūtrīcem “wet-nurse” or more likely from a Late Latin from *nutrīcia (note the short initial /u/). Verbal derivation attested from 17C.

Scey ja un hom ny hal a pascr all'hour oyon Claudia vien entarn – Victoria, arrancað parfait ne sy curtel a scarlat, tag beint tandic y magð met y tabr.
There is someone already in the dinehall when Claudia enters – Victoria, perfectly arranged in her scarlet red robe, drinking tea while the maid sets the table.

L'oc no's sigt rar – vray, y Tarner de Perla Solare accar moutessem ag majortað y villa Condottore, preseyent confiabr un dy colluïsc moneð premendessem ne Fontanelli. Ma cos usual l'ig accar ag ralley, noc ag cocoro dell'aubon.
It's not an unusual sight – the Hostess of Perla Solare is one of the more common visitors to the Condottore villa, being responsible for the most successful business in Fontanelli. But usually she should rather visit later in the afternoon, not first thing in the morn.

Com y bempart de sull'armað, Victoria faïsc y stel colluïr por tout – ig inclus sy sveilanç. Claudia beut y bonjorn ag domn antir eð y basc a cost n'approismant.
Like most in her employ, Victoria generally keeps late hours in all things – including when she gets up. Claudia greets the older woman and goes to give her a kiss on the cheek.

“Vos eð vos smars tost, Tarner. Jo sper ig nien se produg gra.”
“You're under the sun early, Hostess. I hope there is nothing grave.”

“Diabr, cas ig jo volois vossy port martellar – noc, alcun production tan gra. Bonjorn, Domn,” dig Victoria ne souriant. “L'un de my doncel er tart vant, e detectau uncos ne repaðrent la vos desirareð, lonc mey, apprendr hastous.”
“Oh, I should have been besieging your door if it were – no, not as grave as all that. Good morning, my lady,” Victoria says, smiling. “One of my girls was about late, and she spotted something on her way home which I thought you should like to know post-haste.”

“Por tant, noc un production tan gra ? Alor certan vos sceust un doncel m'accaçar coll'eç novel ?” rou Claudia, asseyent eð y magð ablegant cant i cour le sarvir.
“And yet it's not as grave as all that? Then surely you could have sent one of your girls to tell me?” Claudia asks, taking a seat and waving off the maid who rushes to serve her.

“Jo sta toð cas deur quelt, e creye uncos luxuir benoçar,” dig Victoria cos conjuraçonnant, sy cop leyant. “Parcanç un bris meðes d'entrig. Oc a digr, Annetta vis un monc entrant ag bourg.”
“I was up anyway, and I thought I could use a little luxury,” Victoria says, a little conspiratorially, and lifts her teacup. “And perhaps a spot of gossip. You see, Annetta saw a monk entering the town.”

Ig, Claudia paus. “Un monc,” dobr i un pog souvars, contr ig Victoria soury con gien fossettant. “Ac ?”
Claudia pauses at that. “A monk,” she repeats, somewhat incredulous, while Victoria grins, her cheeks dimpling. “Here?”

8m Avril

foran /foˈʀan/ foreign, abroad, of a different people of state

< 13C in later Old Boral foran “foreign(er)”, from Vulgar Latin forānus “outsider”, derived from adverb forās “outside” (which also gives Boral for “only, except”. Mostly displaced parrin < peregrīnus in this sense, now used only in religious contexts (cognates include English pilgrim).

L'oc noðriç foudent es nostrell'ivan addocent ne lengaç foran eð a diabr!

10m Avril

(audio here)

    lines from the early nineteenth-century trevold Vista Terrena (Earthly Scene), originally published by Antòin Herrel in the Vascon city of Santander. However, it was swiftly banned on charges of indecency (the resurgence of the Modest Arrangement in Spain and Gaul no doubt inflamed by the inevitability of its decline), and the book was only saved due to Antòin's swift flight to Portsmouth, where he changed his name to Tony Smith and republished the story in Kentish (presumably aided in the translation by his companion Hal Brennan). The original manuscript was eventually published in 1870, some years after his death.

“You have?” the monk asks. Haloed in gold, he looks poor, humble, beautiful – and forbidden.

11m Avril

< 10C, from Old Boral anteȝr, anterr “earlier, preceding”, from Latin anterior “further ahead, previous”, comparative of the preposition ante “before, in front of”. 13m Avril

    questions taken from the January 2003 N scitation documents for the course 1471 - 1517: War, Peace and Exploration after the Novomundine Landfall, 37st avenue in Concurrence History at the Tremonnow Open School (formerly the Brethin Mesh Institute).

Question 1 (thirty minutes)

Discuss the effect of three new foodstuffs introduced to North Africa and Europe via either (i) the Morrack involvement in Western Cappatia, or (ii) the New Navarre Enterprise.

Question 2 (ninety minutes)

“Perhaps we should have sent for a Kentish witness! They might learn the subtle craft of ending the wars they start.”

- General Guillen daus Sanz on the eve of the Battle of Margès, 1499

Answer any two of the three questions related to the above quote.

(i) A colleague of yours commends daus Sanz and Lord Menton for achieving independence in just two years, noting that in Albion the conflict lasted for forty-six. In what ways might you dispute their characterisation of events?

(ii) The quote suggests that, although Burgundy was not directly involved in the Albion Wars of Fealty, knowledge of the conflict was widespread in Europe. Examine the opinion of any relevant courts (for example, that of Ambrose's Vascony) towards Kent under the Two Henries, and how these impacted Kent's standing between Albion and Gaul.

(iii) To what extent can we say that the Provincial Secession from the Kingdom of Burgundy inspired the development of the principles of Vicinity? You may wish to consider the writings of Jaume fi Gustau advocating for the Doccian language, and, if you have time, compare the Secession to the Emergency at Dijon in 1170 (although this falls somewhat outside the bounds of this course).

15m Avril

entrig /ɛnˈtraj/ 1. inbetween, within, among (used as an adverb)

< 14C, from Middle Boral entr “between” < Latin inter and ig “there” < Latin ibi. Corresponds to the preposition just as we have the pairs vars~avars, ne~entarn, sur~dessur and deur~apart.

    Nell'oc icon veyað mell'aldr a cost e mey entrig.

    In this photo there's my parents on each side and me inbetween.

< 17C, deverbal noun from entrighar “to plot, to scheme, to gossip” (now obsolete), itself from Latin intrīcō “I entangle, I perplex” via Old Boral entriȝar “to complicate”.

    Entrig cortal produis l'er ablegað soutan ag 1r Octobr 1768.

    Court intrigue led to his sudden dismissal on October 1st 1768.

16m Avril

(audio here)

    from One Quiet Day by Roberta Bluemore (published in parts from 1939 until 1943 in the Pattinson Issue HM), a retelling of the plot of the 1921 sensation The Day We Let the Chaos In, an illustred trevold collaboration between the siblings Martha and Dean Armstrong. Although, as with most generally-popular works, there were many such derivative pieces published (Markland having at the time an eighteen-year withholding), Bluemore's enjoyed widespread fame, leading to a run on Pattinson's primers and a copy shortage. The full work was edited singly in 1945, and translated into Borlish in 1946 by Margret Courleon.

Ricart zoum, lor mort y carn bou. “Or, y cligs partenent. Dig mey honest – cal pes tu de Fraðr Desmont ?
Richard hums, then bites into the steak. "Now, about the church. Tell me honestly – what do you think of Brother Desmond?”

“Tu es l'en passað con oy, toll'oc bourg son s'en pleitent,“ remarc Carolin. "Ne bon mont volois tu noc ci noçon en forgar ? E vray es ig tu landa sy pie, com untal racont des scrittur ?”
“You've spent time with him now, it's the talk of the town,” Caroline points out. “Shouldn't you have formed your own opinions? And did you really wash his feet, like in some story from scripture?”

Richard smothers the urge to grin smugly and gives her an innocent look instead. “He fell and injured his foot,” he says. “I was only helping him.”

I jovisc, amusað. “Fort certan no'r tu,” murmur i. “Oy j'ay l'ig stoir oïð moutessem apen ag racont de sy mellacr. Veyað y humbr e devouð, y dedeyaðessem es Segr Ricart, saluçon carnt n'act de humiltað. Tu me fay scam.” Carolin soupir. “Tu no's jamay hom religious. Cal es ty conjuraçon ?”
She snorts, amused. “Of course you were,” she mutters. “I've heard that story almost more times now than the story of his miracle. Oh how humble and pious, how dedicated Sir Richard is, seeking salvation in acts of humility. You are an embarrassment.” Caroline sighs. “You've never been the religious sort. What are you plotting?”

“He seems good for the town.”

“I haven't!” Richard says, before he can stop himself.

“Pu tu desir,” accus Carolin, coll'eç ogl sagaç lor i l'indic con deit. “Diabr, Ricart, l'oc segr es monc! Tu va noc – tu va noc un monc sedugr! Y plurem, noc avant y bourg enter!”
“But you want to,” Caroline accuses, her eyes sharp as she points a finger at him. “Good God, Richard, the man is a monk! You can't – you can't seduce a monk! And not in front of the whole town!”

17m Avril

gien /ʒjɛn/ cheek, jaw, jowl

< 10C, from Old Boral gien “cheek” (probably a dialect form given the uncharacteristic vowel breaking after /dʒ/), itself from Latin gena “cheek, eye socket”.

    Remane un cavel ag gien dy babe, eð i le deleu a toccot.

    A hair remained on the baby's cheek, and he brushed it away.

    /ˌʀe.maˈne ɪn kaˈvɛl ɛj ˈʒjɛn di baˈbe | eð i ˌle deˈlaw a toˈkɔt/

18m Avril

souvartr /suˈvaʀ.tʀ̩/ [sʊˈvɑː.tɐ] to upend, to overturn, to take aback

-r conjugation, -s variant (past participle souvars)

< from 11C in Old Boral so(b)vartre “to turn upside down”, from Latin subvertō “I overturn, overthrow, destroy”, itself from sub- “under” + vertō “I turn, reverse”. Modern metaphorical senses perhaps retained from Latin but not in written evidence until 16C.

    Jo far bon com deibr, tandic y mont enter es me souvars atorn.

    I'm doing as well as can be expected, with the word upended around me.

    /ˈʒo faʀ ˈbɔn kɔm ˈdi.bʀ̩ | tanˈdɪc i ˈmɔnt ɛnˈtɛʀ mɛz suˈvaʀz aˈtɔʀn/

##

19m Avril

Latin cognitus “known, recognised, noted”, perfect passive participle of cognōscō “I know, I recognise”.

1. Vulgar Latin connōscō with passive participle connūtus regularly gives Boral verb connoscr /koˈno.xʀ̩/ [kʊˈno.xɐ] “to know (of), to meet” with participle connuð “known, met”.

    Jo n'ay deja un segr cullighessem connuð.
    I've not yet come across a stranger man.
    /ʒo ˈne deˈʒa ɪn ˈsiʀ ˌkilaˈjɛsɛm koˈnɪθ/

2. cognitus gives Old French coint, queynt “clever, intelligent” (among other forms), borrowed into Old Boral and surviving to the modern day as queint /kwint/ “intelligent, sophisticated, charming”. (In French we now have quain, quainte /kɛ̃(t)/ “nice, pretty”).

    L'es queint e polið ; untal vel jo mariðar pront e direct!
    He is charming and polite; I should marry such a man in a heartbeat!
    /lɛz ˈkwint e poˈlɪθ | ɪnˈtal vɛl ˈʒo ˌma.ʀiˈðaʀ ˈpʀɔnt e diˈʀɛkt/

3. The antonym incognitus “unknown, untested” gives Old Italian incognito “unknown” also, “disguised, hidden”, reanalysed as in cognito “in hiding, in disguise”, giving cognito “disguise, hiding”. This phrase borrowed into Old French as en cognite with the same sense, and by 1400 into Middle Boral as ne cognit “in disguise” and cognit /kɔjˈnɪt/ “disguise, hidingplace, secret plot”.

    A cal bon un cognit lon masq ?
    What good is a disguise with no mask?
    /a kal ˈbɔn ɪn kɔjˈnɪt lɔn ˈmask/

##

20m Avril

viker /viˈkɛʀ/ [vɪˈkɛː] on behalf of another, by proxy

also as a noun: proxy, steward

< 11C as vicair, semi-learned borrowing from Latin vicārius “substituted”, from vicis “interchange, alternation”. Noun senses attested from 14C; surname Viker from the same time. Phrase viker pondr “to steward, to manage” from 17C.

    Vos saveð deïr viker pondr a toll'oc majon des je Lun.

    You know you'll have to steward the whole house from Monday.

    /ˌvo saˈvɛθ diˈjɪʀ viˈkɛʀ pɔn.dʀ̩ a toˈlɔk maˈʒɔn dɛz ʒe ˈlɪn/

21r Avril

colorn /koˈlɔʀn/ [kʊˈlɔːn] hazel tree, hazelwood

also light brown (esp. of hair or eyes)

in particular, noiç colorn /ˌnɔjts koˈlɔʀn/ [ˈnɔjskʊˈlɔːn] hazelnut
-
< 9C in Old Boral colorne, from Latin adjective colurnus “made from hazelwood”, an idiosyncratic derivation from corylus “hazel tree”, from Greek κόρῠλος (kórulos) with the same meaning. Colour sense from 15C, constrasting with lighter miel and darker castagn (lit. honey, chestnut).

    Jo sgardau vars l'Adriaç ne yaurt a noiç colorn pascent.

    I looked out over the Adriatic eating hazelnut yoghurt.

    /ʒo sgaʀˈdo vaʀz ˈlad.ʀiˈats ne ˈjoʀt a nɔjts koˈlɔʀn ˈpa.xɛnt/

##

22m Avril

    taken from Middlesea Meals, published bilingually in Wessern and Kernish 1892 by Iscombe Editors. It was translated from the acclaimed 1880 mealbook Pasti Mezziterreni, written by traveller Josforo Sant'Angelo and intersperses anecdotes from his journeys with his favourite dishes.

King James Stew (Frigotta de Rei Jaume)

Of all the fish stews I sampled along the solarium coasts of Barcelon and Provence (not to mention the singular mussel-olive dish I was served on the beaches of Mayurca), none compare to the King James. Locally and affectionately called the jaumetia “little James”, this stew is named for James VI (1691-1749), the longest-ruling monarch in Barcelon since its establishment in 1067. Although similar stews existed in the region before his reign, most simply functioned to use up whatever seafood remained from a day's catch. The King James is perhaps the first such dish made for its own sake, and indeed should for best results use hake or haddock, or a similar whitefish.

Wares (for four)

5ι sweet aumonds
eq. grain garlic, olive oil
1 large onion per cubos
1 whole pepper, sliced
some sweet drypepper, petersly and fendle (fine)
½ pint Empordan or other white
8ι pit shadome or sauce thereof
2п6ι whitefish per frangos
½ limmon, juced

Method

First bake the aumonds until golden and grind to a coarse powder. In a broad pan heat the oil, then gently fry the aumond, garlic, onion and pepper for roughly fifteen minutes, or until the vegetables are soft.

Add the drypepper and half the herbs and stir together. After two minutes add a dram of the wine and allow it to mostly boil off. Now add the shadome sauce or chopped shadomes, pour in the rest of the wine, and cook for fifteen minutes more.

Salt the strips of fish and push them into the sauce. Cover the pan and leave for five minutes, until the fish is just cooked. Uncover, add the rest of the herbs (or whichever amount suits) and cast the juce over the dish. Serve bowled with hardbread.

##

23m Avril

slognað /slɔjˈnaθ/ [zlʊjˈnah] remote, unlikely, distant

< past participle of slognar “to recede, to distance, to move away”, via Old Boral isloȝnar from Vulgar Latin exlongō, with the same meaning. The Latin is a late derivation from longē “far off”. Nowadays usually either meaning “distant in time” (the spatial sense mostly reserved by lontan) or else the metaphorical “unlikely”, which is first attested 18C.

    Slognað comoscon ig nos parvendreu allagr-vescoirn-il, jo vil te colluïr.

    I will stay by your side, no matter how unlikely it is that we'll get our happy ending.

    /slɔjˈnaθ ˌko.moˈxɔn aj no ˌpaʀ.vɛnˈdʀaw aˈlɛ.jʀ̩ veˈxo.jʀ.nɪl | ʒo ˈvɪl te ˌko.laˈjɪʀ/

##

24m Avril

quinar /kwiˈnaʀ/ [kwɪˈnɑː] to whack, to hit, to thump, to bash

also to collide (without bouncing)

< 10C, from Latin cuneō “I force in a wedge” based on cuneus “wedge”, via Old Boral quȝnar, couȝnar “to chisel, to strike”. Replaced in this sense by kesellar, a derivation from kesel < Vulgar Latin cæsellus “chisel, cutting tool” < cæsus, passive participle of caedō “I cut”. Modern senses of “to whack” from 13C, and “to collide” from 16C.

    Nos oyeu ig pas quinan e scanen y scagl tras port.

    We could hear footsteps thumping up the stairs outside.

    /no‿zoˈjaw aj paz kwiˈnan e xaˈnen y ˈxɛjl tʀaz ˈpɔʀt/

25m Avril

    scitation questions lifted from Safford Technic Scole's Spring 89 course Along Natural Theory V: Astronomy from Lorenzo to th'Alluning, last in a series following the history of scientific development [reformatted for English.JK]

Answer any number of the following questions in the next three hours.

- - -
Question 2

With reference to Bensaíd's The Communication Toolset, discuss the 1677 global effort to observe the transit of Mercury as an early example of international scientific collaboration.

    Chronicle either the voyage of Danes Gudmund and Rebecca Scradder to the Seldivan court, or the political difficulties at the Observatory at Trapoles under Malik Lisan Al-Mostansir.
    Illustrate mathematically how the threepoint method allowed people to calculate the solar distance using their combined transit records.
    Was it a lack of sufficiently-accurate timekeeping that prevented such an effort during the Venusian transits of the 1630s?

- - -
Question 5

“And all at once Adelaide understood the terrible infinitude cast out before her. She could let the Captain lose her under any xenic sky, and become at once and entirely the most isolated person in history.
The thought appealed.”
- from Through a Black Mirror (1795, Elsebeth Sneider ~ Siegert)

    Describe the state of astronomical knowledge at the time Sneider wrote the above quote, on the eve of the nineteenth century.
    With the conclusion of the First German War in 1770, Saxony was ascendant and controlled more territory than it had at any point in its history. Consider Sneider's writing about the attendant early urbanisation of Lepzi. To what extent did this and her 1772 marriage to Sternkundhaus scholar Otto Sneider influence her writing?
    “The Voidtale fashion reared a generation of star-gazing men and women and thus was ultimately responsible for the cosmic discoveries of the mid-nineteenth century; bint Inám locating the planet Janus, and the normal speed being fixed, for example.” To what extent do you agree with this quote?

crullar /kʀiˈlaʀ/ [kʀɪˈlɑː] to hit, to knock, to ring, to tap, to knell, to clap

that is, to strike so as to make a sound

also to play (a percussion instrument), to pluck (a string), to fuck

< first attested 13C in later Old Boral crullar (possibly 9C ?cunlare “cleave” but this is ambiguous), believed to be from Old English cnyllan “to strike, to knock”. Sense of sound-making established by late 14C; extension to music from 16C. Vulgar sense probably old but only in writing from 19C.

    Tonneðr crullau dessur cant Damian hasta vars y tarm vectoir.

    Thunder clapped overhead as Damian hurried towards the bus stop.

27m Avril

Novomondin /ˌno.vo.mɔnˈdɪn/ Novomundine, of the New World

< 17C, from Scholastic Latin novomundīnus “from the New World, from Mendeva or Cappatia”, presumably based on the phrase dē novō mundō “from the new world”. This phrase attested from early 16C to describe places and people both mendēvalēnsis and cappātiānus. This word continues the habit of transliterating Latin “short <u>” as Boral <o>, on the basis of sound similarity.

    Fort certan, Onnacra parloc y cascat stoniscendessem Novomondin.

    Certainly the Onnacra has the most breathtaking waterfall in the New World.

    /ˈfɔʀt tsɛʀˈtan | o.naˈkʀa paʀˈlɔk i kaˈxat ˌsto.ni.xɛnˈde.sɛm ˌno.vo.mɔnˈdɪn/

28m Avril

    Borlish and Merch translations from 1 Corinthians. These lines were used as the forenote for (and inspired the title of) Elsebeth Sneider's 1795 work Through a Black Mirror, usually cited as the original parachthon romance.

Oy veim nos par spegl n'oscurtað ; la cossy verreu nos visaç a visaç. Oy connosc jo ne part ; la cossy le connoscray jo com so jo connuð.

/ɔj ˈvim no paʀ ˈspil no.xɪʀˈtaθ | la koˈsi veˌʀaw no viˈzats a viˈzats ‖ ɔj koˈnɔx ʒo ne ˈpaʀt | la koˈsi la ˌko.noˈxʀe ʒo kɔm ˈso ʒo koˈnɪθ/

Thogh seeth we now as by a black mirre, yet shalth we ben in sight yathre. Thogh know I now but by deals, yet shall I know all as am I knoun to God.

/xu ˈseːx we ˈnuː əs bi ə ˈblak ˈmɪ.ɾə | yɛt ˈʃɔx we ˈbɛn ɪn ˈsiːt ˈja.ɣə ‖ xu ˈknuː i ˈnuː bʊt bi dɛːls | jɛt ˈʃal i ˈknuː ˈal əs am i ˈknuːn tə ˈgɔd/

    excerpt from first-edition Borlish translation of the above novel. Released by Hautcligs Edition in 1798 (a remarkably short delay for translation at the time, although in this case a French copy was published yet three months earlier). 

Eð accougl ne cainç Adelait reconnoy l'abject perpetuitað luy jait avant. I poðe parmettr ag Capitan de le pardr sou un ciel xenic calscon, e l'hom denir a coup e cos enter l'istraðessem de jamay.

And immediately Adelaide understood the terrible infinitude cast out before her. She could let the Captain lose her under any xenic sky, and become at once and entirely the most isolated person in history.

L'idea attraghen.

The thought appealed.

##

29m Avril

ausnaç /ozˈnats/ [ʊzˈnas] abstinent, unruffled, taciturn, shy

in essence, antonym of exuberant, loud, indulgent

< 11C, from Latin abstināx “abstinent”, an adjectival derivation from abstineō “I abstain”, via Old Boral avstnaç “teetotal, abstinent, having taken holy orders”. In 15C, briefly shifted to mean “zealous, evangelical, stubborn”, but this sense is soon lost to the modern “shy, taciturn”, which dates from roughly the same time.

    Ða vos sereð ausnaç demay por fruyar, façað collocq!

    If you're too shy to dance, at least talk to somebody!

    /ða vo seˈʀɛθ ozˈnats deˈme pɔʀ fʀaˈjaʀ | faˈtsaθ koˈlɔk/

##

30m Avril

issugt /iˈsajt/ dried, wrung out, unexciting, uninspiring

< Attested 14C but presumably extant earlier, from Latin exsūctus “dried out, extracted”, passive participle of exsūgō “I suck out”, via postulated Old Boral *issuȝt. It was used predominately of fruit until 17C; the first metaphorical use comes from the trial transcripts of notorious Viðor robber Marc Couvorpeg (known internationally from the play-biography The Foxtail Gang, which was performed in at least twelve countries and composed in libro in 1711.)

    Cascif vel un hom foudent un desc scadom aromar con narichel issugt ?

    Why the fuck would anyone put dessicated coconut in a tomato dish?

    /kaˈxɪf ˌvɛl ɪn ˈɔm ˈfu.dɛnt ɪn ˈdɛx xaˈdɔm ˌa.ʀoˈmaʀ kɔn ˌna.ʀiˈkɛl iˈsajt/

##

1r May

    from the collected correspondence of Marian Siegert ~ Miller (1725–56), illegitimate daughter of Elias Villingen, second Duke of Far Suebia (the title having been created by King Frederick of Bavarn after the Blue Trans-Willem War and the dissolution of the Kingdom of Burgundy).

[received at Castle Carlsfeld in the winter of 1744]

To my dear brother Thomas,

Albert suggested that I write to you first, and I was only too happy to do so; ideally your witticisms shall be as entertaining on paper as in person, and in any case I cannot help but feel some kinship with you. We are the two fledgeling sparrows cast out from Father's nest to fend for ourselves.

I am of course exaggerating; doubtless the King can provide for any number of wards (no matter how grudgingly accepted; your evenings with Father were perhaps not as private as you hoped! Shall we just say that everyone downstairs is aware of Prince M's Indiscretion?), but neither can I complain overmuch. Albert understated the extent of his cobbler's empire here in Lepzi; it cannot compare to home (as still I think of it) but I had been prepared to face far greater hardships when I accepted his hand.

You were, as ever, entirely correct to insist on extra language tutors (and entirely too generous in letting me attend!). The compulsory Latin has revealed itself less than useless—we run in entirely the wrong circles—but the Dutch they have here is almost as awful as that of those Flemish performers we had in some summers past. I expect I will acclimate, but for now I am having far too much fun speaking Burgund and Boheme with Albert's company.

Does the news we get of bread troubles have any basis? We know not to trust anything the Lepzi weeklies have to say about life south of the Danaw, but the rumours about Venise and Crovatia are bad enough; do abscond back to Stuttgart at the slightest sign Bavarn is along the same track…

Your sister,

F. Marian Siegert

2m May

irrompr /iˈʀɔm.pʀ̩/ to break in, to burst in, to enter by force or without warning

-r conjugation, -oy class, past participle irrout

< Borrowed late 14C into Middle Boral from Latin irrumpō “I invade, I intrude”, from in- + rumpō “I break, I tear”. Conjugation modelled by analogy on the inherited rompr “to break, to snap”.

    Sophia connoscent, l'irrompra l'oc fest parlocað de sull'adolant.

    Knowing Sophia, she'll make a grand arrival flanked by her sycophants.

    /soˈfja ˌko.noˈxɛnt | ˌli.ʀɔmˈpʀa lɔk ˈfɛst ˌpaʀ.loˈkaθ de siˌla.doˈlant/

##

3m May

vorpeg /vɔʀˈpi/ fox, vixen, refinement, sophistication

also beautiful person

< Old Boral vorpeȝl, voupil “fox” via a northern dialect form demonstrating the general [ʎ > j] change, attested by early 13C. The Old Boral from a Vulgar Latin diminutive vurpiculus < Latin vulpes “fox, cunning”. The errant [r] presumably a result of early dissimilation. Used of people for erudition from 17C, for appearance from 19C.

    Eð vos y japot trovað la y ci vorpeg irromen apar ?

    Did you find the hole through which those foxes were breaking in?

    /ɛθ ˈvo i ʒaˈpɔt tʀoˈvaθ la ˌi.tsi vɔʀˈpi ˌi.ʀoˈmɛn aˈpaʀ/

##

4m May

    further extract from The Man From Everywhere (El Hombre de Jallondes) by Estevan Mazon, published 1893 and translated into Borlish 1901 by Michæl Norman.

Toð pris accougl, Marc no'r certan de noc faïr y tuyaut recevir, ma ancour l'oc no sta total extrane. Prefaibr untal aucoum par gent aye lou sgardað e pesað a lour y jorn a cortisment meðes, n'y bel per admirant.
On balance, Mark wasn't sure that he wouldn't rather have had the cigar, but there was still something in it. Probably any number of people had looked at them and thought of their own courting days, admiring the handsome couple.

“Vos eð y fest broucað ?” rovau Anna, posc il aurn ci may follað.
“Did you have a nice time?” Anna asked, once they'd walked a bit more.

“Oc fort, vray,” admis i. “Dig le noc a alcun hom.”
“I did, rather,” he admitted.  “Don't tell anyone.”

Y magð scarneu. “Jo vos support may cant vos parformað noc star dessur de tout.”
She huffed.  “You're more fun to be around if you aren't trying to pretend you're above it all.”

I luy dau un sgart fix, e dis sec, “Jo so vray dessur de tout.”
He gave her a sardonic look, and said archly, “I am above it all.”

“No soð vos,” dis i enter serious. “Vos soð paregl com nos autr.”
“No you aren't,” she said, quite seriously.  “You're the same as the rest of us.”

Vuscau vos ayað bon. “Jo promet de noc ig star.”
If only that were true.  “I'm really not.”

I soupirau, pariscent a pognt d'uncos digr, ma se taçoy.
She sighed, seeming about to say something, but chose not to.

Il follaurn y sougl kieð ; Marc fey verroil ag port ados. Candon il yemoy la oyon l'eç segr eð eç domn stan a asmuth sevrar—e posc il ascuraurn ig Magret e Willaum se parteurn toð idone e con poliðeç maxim—Anna se pos a ortegl e bascau raut, cast, sy gien.
They let themselves in quietly, Mark locking the door behind them.  When they reached the place where the men and women went their separate way—and after they'd seen that Daisy and William parted with no funny business—Anna rose up on her toes and quickly, chastely, kissed his cheek.

L'er partið pall'ig scagl domn pre Marc sceus y façon decidr de sy reaction.
She was gone up the women's staircase before Mark had quite decided how to react.

5m May

tuyaut /taˈjot/ cigar, pipe, cigarette

also dick, cock

< from early 16C, borrowed from French tuyau “pipe, tube” and immediately narrowing in meaning in Boral. Unetymological <t> is probably hypercorrection, either after borrowing or in the now-moribund Flanders dialect. Pronounced as would be expected in Boral from at least 1689 (cf. Barton), although a former fransc variant is likely. The French from Old French tuial, tudel “pipe”, from Classical German theutō via Frankish thūta “tube”. Related to Merch theet “channel, watercourse”. Vulgar sense in evidence from the twentieth century.

    L'oc bourg a recar dy molin a tuyaut ; lourell'empley, mendrem.

    This town needs the cigar factories, for the jobs if nothing else.

    /lɔk ˈbuʀg a ʀeˈkaʀ di moˈlɪn a taˈjot | luʀelɛmˈpli ˈmɛndʀɛm/

6m May

cortisment /ˌkɔʀ.tɪsˈmɛnt/ [ˌkɔː.tɪzˈmɛn(t)] courtship, dating

< 16C, nominal derivation from cortisar “to court, to woo”, a borrowing of the French synonym courtiser, and modelled on Boral cort “court” (or perhaps directly from Italian corteggiare, whence the French). In any case, a verb from Latin cohors “courtyard”, referring to courtly chivalry.

    Vos fal seg monð, ben un annað meðes, por cortisment idone.

    You need six months, or even a year, for an entirely proper courtship.

    /vo ˈfal sij ˈmɔnθ | bɛn ɪn aˈnaθ meˈðɛz | pɔʀ ˌkoʀ.tɪsˈmɛnt i.doˈne/ 

##

7m May

    censor commentary on a question from the 1985 scitation paper Consequences of Viking Statecraft, set as part of Lester Academy's series on northwestern European history.

Question 1: What factors enabled Dunclothe in the mid-tenth century to conquer the heartland territory of the Dane Sodrick (also known contemporaneously as the Kingdom of the Isles)?

Censor: On the whole a well-answered question, with the majority of respondents correctly noting the troubles further east - a typical good answer included the following:

    the several famine years during the 920s and 30s across Denmark and Horth (some incorrectly referred to the Eurasian Warming, but this began only several decades later, and in any case did not lower crop yields in the region).
    the skirmishes between Dane-ruled Borland and Sothbar, now harbouring the exilic Roman king and his family (the wife, of course, being King Roderick's cousin).
    the inevitable slowdown of the flow of wealth obtained by raiding; excellent responses compared Sodrick to Danish Jarvick, which managed to transition to a more diplomatic stance by virtue of inheriting (so to speak) a city well-positioned in its trade mesh. By 980 N Jarvick had even successfully reunited Northumbre under its own rule, although Edinbrough and her fief remained de facto independent until increasing Gallic incursion necessitated a more active defence.

Less common but by no means unwelcome was discussion of the Norman French, to whose relative prosperity many left Sodrick entirely; the extent of the depopulation is difficult to quantify but we read several good arguments along these lines. Of particular note is one student; she managed to find convincing records of triplet brothers, born in Mull around 915 N, and mentioned again in reference to a Norman raid of Marsella in 946 N.

It was somewhat disappointing that few respondents mentioned the aggressor Dunclothe in any detail - the lack of decent contemporary sources outside the Tremonnow gravatory does make discussing Roderick's court a challenge. Nonetheless, we appreciated any discussion of, for example, Oscar of Eidin's influence among the northern lords.

##

8m May

platabac /ˌpla.taˈbak/ cupboard, sideboard, chest of drawers

often and traditionally a (necessarily free-standing) item of furniture used to store crockery or utensils, and on which food might be plated before a formal dinner.

< 13C platabac(q) “sideboard, cupboard (often literally a board attached to a wall)”, from late Old French plat abaque “sideboard”, literally “flat board”, retaining a then-rare sense of abaque < Latin abacus “board, abacus”. The mathematical sense of abaque was so predominant that one occasionally sees the reanalysis plat à bac “raftboard” in the contemporary literature, despite the incongruous semantics.

    Des y platabac pris i dou peutr blau e pos i l'un a sy fraðr.

    He took out two mugs from the cupboard and offered one to his brother.

    /dɛz i ˌpla.taˈbak pʀɪz i du ˈpaw.tʀ̩ blo e ˈpɔz i ˈlɪn a si ˈfʀa.ðʀ̩/

##

9m May

mesveir /mɛsˈviʀ/ [mɪzˈvi.jɐ] to miss, to ignore, to not see, to be incorrect

-r conjugation, s variant (past participle mesvis, future stem mesverr-)

< transparently mes- “badly” < Old English mis- via Old Boral (retaining its sense), plus veir “to see” which descends from Latin videō “I see”. Cognate with French mévoir “to not understand”.

    Port un grant capel ben nos te mesverreu nell'aucoum.

    Wear a big hat or we won't see you in the crowd.

    /ˈpɔʀt ɪn ˈgʀant kaˈpɛl bɛn ˈno te ˌmɛs.veˈʀaw ne.loˈkum/

##

10m May

    extract from the Borlish translation of 1981 novel En haut, en bas “Upstairs, Downstairs” by Vincent Margouille, Norman author and former professional lineball player (part of the team that led Kent to its only cup victory in 1965). The story is a period piece set in a fictional manor house late in the Grand-Jeu period; later books in the series follow some of the characters through the Millstone War.

L'oc commenç con sarf dar a desjeun—y soul repast tojorn paut cas lou.
It started with serving at breakfast—the one meal that they always took at home.

Poll'ig stove just se pondr a cost y platabac com vas de grant hautour particuler, ma se poðe noc vayar apart ny ci cogtaçon personal e retenuð, por noc un hom mesveir ant man leyað an autr tay, ben form visaç commettr.
That was mostly a matter of standing by the sideboard like a particularly large vase, but you couldn't drift off into your own private thoughts, lest you miss someone signaling for more tea, or commit a facial expression.

Seveurn l'hour d'accarenç, des y desjeun ne haut er emmenað e fin all'apprestment dy repastin.
Next came the calling hours, from just after the upstairs breakfast was cleared to a bit before tea-time.

Dalvan cos usual parmis ag ci armað de l'oc stent passar asseyent ne hal d'agent e respir prendent un pog, sauf cas y bon Domn au fait reconnuð a accarent ig l'er Recevent, oc avenent noc moutessem a un veg par monð.
At Dalvane, those times of day were usually when you got to sit in the servants' hall and catch your breath a bit, unless her ladyship had put out word that she was In Reception to callers, which didn't happen above once a month or so.

Melle a Gaunt sta enter destegnt, ant collanç incoïbr de tarsc annonç e de convoc a prendr.
The Gaunt melee was a different story, with a constant trickle of calling-cards and invitations turning up.

incoïbr /ˌɪn.kɔˈji.bʀ̩/ [ˌɪŋ.kʊˈji.bɐ] inevitable, fated, imminent

< corresponding to unattested Latin adjective incohibilis “not confinable”, seen in Old Boral as incoyible, then as now analysable as in- “not” + coyir~coïr “to prevent, to stop” + -ible~-ibr “-able”. The verb comes from Latin cohibeō “I confine, I hinder, I limit”.

##

13m May

    taken from A History of Timekeeping (Cторıя Oбмера Часу), written by Igor Arcadich and published 1961 by Halich Quaternus Editions.

…Meneva (now Tyðewi, or in Wessern tradition Tythew, literally meaning “David's house”), and died on the first of March at the end of the seventh century. He was canonised by Pope Agatho (1001-1026); it is commonly told that this was a signal of Papal support for the foundering Kingdom of Wales, which had been losing people and land both to Markland and to Devon.

[Pictured left, the flag of the modern Kingdom of Wynneth (Y Deyrnas Gwyneð), using the colours of Saint David]
image

The story of Saint David, while interesting, is somewhat tangential to our plot. In the early years of the twelfth century, amidst the great successes of the First Crusade, Pope Nuntius instituted a reform of the heretofore universal Julian calendar. Most relevant to us was the shift of the New Year from the Annunciation on the 25th of March to David's Day, on the first. (In fact, during the Roman period, the first month was January, and to this days certain Eastern heterodoxies celebrate the yearturning at the beginning of that month). It is thus unsurprising that the Welsh New Year celebrations are a global legend. I myself was fortunate enough to be in Deganny for the 200th anniversary of the coronation of the People's King—my hearing has never been the same!

Of course, despite Rome's unparalleled power at this time, not all of Christendom implemented the Nuntial reforms at once. The spiritual significance of the Annunciation proved a particular sticking point; this formed one of the famed Pauline Denunciations—“the reckless diminution of our ordained measure”, as he characteristically put it. Thus did the Augsburg dominions retain the Julian calendar until the Rhine Compromises much later.

The second and more involved change to the Julian calendar regarded the placement of leap years. It had been noted that the equinoces under the Julian system would drift from their astronomical ideal by one day for every 128 years…

##

14m May

tarsc /taʀx/ [tɑːx] card, note, small paper, memo

< 11C from Old French targe “small shield, buckler”, itself from Frankish targa with the same meaning. Probably conflated with the Norrish and English cognates, which would alone have given *tarc or *targ. The shift in sense to paper dates to 16C, from clipping of fougl tarsc “buckler sheet”, the term for the paper size one-half of a trevold. Specific meaning “memo” from 19C.

See also:

    tarsc annonç “calling card, business card”
    tarsc nascenç “birthday card”
    tarsc d'acat “shopping list”

    I revelau e leu y tarsc cort ; un scoul luy creu ag visaç.

    He unfolded and read the short note, a scowl growing on his face.

    /i ˌʀe.veˈlo e ˈlaw i ˈtaʀx kɔʀt | ɪn ˈxul laj ˈkʀaw ɛj viˈzats/

##

15m May

apprestment /ˌa.pʀɛstˈmɛnt/ [ˌa.pʀɪsˈmɛn(t)] preparation, planning, arrangement

< 18C, nominal derivation from apprestar “to prepare, to set out”, itself a causative verbal dervation ca. 11C from Old Boral prest “ready”. This originally from Late Latin præstus, from adverb praestō “present, ready”.

    Parcanç sera i megl vos ignoreð l'apprestment de bilrod.

    Perhaps it is better you not know how billrod is prepared.

    /paʀˈkants seˈʀa i ˈmil aj vo‿ˌzaj.noˈʀɛθ ˌla.pʀɛstˈmɛnt de bɪlˈʀɔd/

##

16m May

    excerpted from the Borlish translation of Clarissa Bellamy's best-selling 1920 romance A Door, Once Opened, translated 1921 on personal request by her friend Gonnilt Dispað (or Gunna Despath, as she was known in London).

Y magð soupirau. “L'am noc l'asmuth dell'eç autranç, nos derreu declarar. I pogn tis toll'oc seyon connuð.
She sighed. “He doesn't like the way things are changing, and that's a fact. He's clinging to what he knows.

Fair ig voos e Willaum dað sarf a tabr—i strayau par le prefair de vos, stant y contingent, ma i desirau y mont com i sta pre y var. Val vos ig comprenað.”
Having you and William waiting at table—he was wrong to expect it of you, in the circumstances, but he wanted it to be as it was before the war. Surely you can understand that.”

Fred luy sgardau. Veg derran oyon l'au attis a Godman explanar, Fred au se tait por y grant bempart d'ig seyon desirað digr. Ac i vole noc fair cossy.
Fred looked at her. The last time she'd tried to make excuses for Goodman, he'd bitten back most of what he wanted to say. He wasn't going to do that now.

“Sey yað un hom soul nell'oc majon,” dis i plat, “stant soffrið par y var men a Godman, jo poð noc savir cu l'oc sera. Certan noc mey. Ben Hasting.”
“If there is anyone in this house,” he said levelly, “who has suffered less from the war than Goodman, I don't know who it could be. Certainly not me. Or Hastings.”

“Or, noc,” admis i. “Portant—”
“Well, no,” she admitted. “But—”

“Jo n'ay l'hour por sy figuration realisar n'y mont stoppant sur far,” continu Fred. “Ða meðes jo le desira—e j'ig clam cos honest faus.”
“I don't have time to indulge his fantasies about stopping the world in its tracks,” Fred went on. “Even if I wanted to—which, to be frank, I don't.

Desirað vos savir cal er jo faint, y moment la Godman fey sy picraug inutr poll'ig fest crevoscr ?“ L'attis alcun respos.
Do you want to know what I was doing, when Goodman was having a temper tantrum about the dinner party?” He didn't wait for an answer.

"J'attene a camin trovar por ascurar ig Hasting yem aubon lon stant denuð sull'occisour meðes.”
“I was trying to figure out how to make sure Hastings got through the night without killing himself.”

minister /ˌmi.niˈstɛʀ/ [ˌmi.nɪˈstɛː] deparment, ministry, bureau, office

that is, a discrete part of a governmental administration, or more recently of a quasipolitic guild [1].

< 11C, from Old Boral menester, from Old French menistere, borrowed from Latin ministerium “ministry, employment” (and by 13C remodelled on the Latin). Extended from the original sense of “office run by somebody appointed directly by the monarch” to general governmental administration in 18C.

##

19m May

    excerpted from The Secret Guide to Ausbagn (English edition), sold 1967 for 1⍚30 in the Calder Museum of Health's leavingstore [1]. Published first in 1962 on behalf of the Maglen Formeuç Caritativia, as part of a series of tourist guidebooks for cities along both coasts of the Rustigh Strait [2].

…reign, and this at the greatest extent of the First Drengotian Empire in the west. For context, the Ausbagn of the late twelfth century is the de facto seat of the County of Sothbar; by 1190 Count Steven is known to have resided year-round in the coastal town, bequeathing the old house in Heitriç to his younger brother.

The location of Ausbagn on one side of the Rustigh Strait proved to be of immense strategic value, both militarily and economically. The Great Flie Flood of 1171 (which created the island Texel, about which more later) swept away much of Lower Frisland, and was devastating for all of Willemy. This was not least because the Duke, one Lovis the Tall, was in Amstel at the time, and was killed in the deluge [3]. In any case, Ausbagn was for several decades afterward the obvious choice of harbour for any trader moving from the Danish lands through the Drengot domain and onward to Spain and the riches of the Middlesea.

More relevant to history (or at least to the good reader's moneypurse!) are the Order of Saint James, in whose name was built the Peninsular Monastery which overlooks the city. Founded in the tenth century under the patronage of Amelia of Bruges, they were implicated in a popular uprising against Geoffry II and forced to flee to Borland in 1067 [4]. Today the Order (with its modern name, the Jacobin Fraternity) is in the main known for its Jaibos breweries and for owning much of the land around the city, including the site of one of the ancient Roman bathhouses…
image

[In blue, Ausbagn and Heitriç; in green, Amstel and Texel; in orange, the Rustigh Strait]

—

[1] in essence, a gift shop.

[2] the waters between the southern coast of Borland and what in OTL are the northern coasts of the Netherlands and western Germany.

[3] possibly; the elderly Duke may already have been dead at the time of the flood - he is known to have been suffering from consumption. The perennial theory that he was swept away on a raft and living his remaining years as a Borlish hermit can be safely ignored.

[4] believed to have the backing of local nobility - however, the emperor's supporters in Normandy were able to muster enough forces that the rebellion was quickly disavowed.

20m May

borser /bɔʀˈsɛʀ/ [bʊːˈsɛː] bank teller, cashier

more recently shop assistant, also ATM

< 12C as “official working in a bank”, from late Old Boral bors “fund, stipend” with the adjectival suffix -er (here used as an agentive), from a confluence of Latin -ārius and Old English -ere (itself ultimately from the Latin). The noun bors from Old French borse “purse, funds”, from Ancient Greek βύρσα (búrsa) “hide, leather” via Latin. Extension to other jobs working with money from 17C. Use for automated tellers from 20C.

    Y grant segr portau un bevis 20⍚ de sachet e le dau ag borser.

    The tall man took out a 20⍚ note from his pocket and gave it to the cashier.

    /i gʀant ˈsi.jʀ̩ pɔʀˈto ɪn beˈvɪz vint ʒeˈma de saˈkɛt e le ˈdo ɛj bɔʀˈsɛʀ/

21r May

envouvr /ɛnˈvu.vʀ̩/ [ɪɱˈvu.vɐ] to involve, to interest, to implicate, to surround

in formal writing to envelop, to swaddle

-r conjugation, sporadic (as souvr; past participle envoluð)

< from 8C in Old Boral envovre, ivouvre “to wrap up, to involve, to devote” (in early sources we see primarily the religious “to devote oneself to a cause”, but this is presumably just a function of the available sources), from Latin involvō “to roll up, to envelop”. Sense “to interest” from 14C; generally “to surround” from 16C. Northern Old Boral dialect form eunoir leads to modern eunovir “to pledge, to swear”.

    Jo vusc il envoloirn noc y bourgmer all'oc delusion.

    I wish they hadn't involved the mayor in this deception.

    /ʒo ˈvɪx ɪl ˌɛn.voˈlɔj.ʀn̩ nɔk i buʀgˈmɛʀ a.lɔk ˌde.liˈzjɔn/

##

22m May

    extract from the Borlish translation of We Wake to Broken Windows, by Norman author Mark Deuthex. It was published by Maison Bateller (Ferryman House) in 1973, in the hey-day of the spycraft romance. It draws on the author's own experience working for the Red House in Roun, whose activities were revealed to the public as part of the Furore of 1961. The events of that year led to the collapse of the Drengot Collusion (in particular, Darchild's tinplate government) and eventually to the Kentish ostracon reforms, used as a model in several polities around the globe.

Lourell'ouçon remanent, parisc i toð cas final, es l'oc administration e cultur fugr ag saluçon d'ig il aun tout oblatað deull'eç við.
So their remaining option, as it stands, is running from the very government and society that they have given everything but their lives to save.

L'oc es indigndað, ma Paul Lacan es hom prest a savir ig bempart dy mont en es.
It isn't fair, but Paul Lacan, of all people, knows that most things aren't.

Par moment pes i dy ci ogl a Hector situað ne visaç blanc eð incertan, agr apen.
He thinks for a while about Hector's eyes in a pale, uncertain face, almost eager.

Paul s'entrouf oyon porraun il var, e comoscon un við enter laiscar, costroit a catr man des y fondamen – y soul act dont Paul acaf a star fier.
Paul wonders where they will go, and how they will possibly just drop a whole life built from ground up – the only thing Paul has ever been proud of.

Ma vray direct, Paul porra eð i vil. I vil Albion e famigl eð y Minister fugr ne Hector amant, e Hector en vil ne við amant.
But of course, Paul can and he will. He'll run from Albion and family and the Department for love of Hector, and Hector will do it for love of life.

Primer, luy fal y moneð tan possibr portar outr.
First, he has to withdraw as much money as possible.

I possey y cla a Hector, e tandic y borser le men dessou vars y cambr tesoir de Hector, i parform pesar a haut vois commet a fin, parolant ag segr noc envoluð –
He has Hector's key, and when the teller takes him down to Hector's vault, he pretends to think aloud the whole way, talking at the uninterested man –

sy grant ojanç por Hector explanant, proclamant ig l'arra un picq retorsion idiosyncras ne toll'aur a Hector parsount.
explaining how much he hates Hector, and how pointlessly spending all Hector's money will be his own personal little revenge.

pog signfiant oc Hector volois noc reclamar ða Paul er vray conjuraçonnant novant orlosc a caðen n'adamantin acatar coll'aur ; y remarcabr es y sgart luy lançað dy borser cant i part, untal pietað a biasc.
It doesn't matter that Hector wouldn't have cared even if Paul did plan to buy ninety titanium watches with the money, the important thing is the look that the teller gives him as he leaves, a kind of sideways pity.

Just exact ig seyon vuscað.
Exactly what he'd been hoping for.

dezein /deˈzin/ decade, period of ten years

in older writing a group of ten things

scap fair de /ˌxap ˈfɛʀ de/ to escape from, to get out of (somewhere)

also to get out of (doing something), to avoid the consequences of

see the expression L'en fey scap! He got away with it!

##

25m May

    first message sent using a Vilmanas Teleclavia, arguably the earliest household tachygraph [typewriter] capable of sending messages on the steeplemesh. It was sent on Michael's day in 1859 (less than a month after the Auroric Anomaly [1], about which much has been made in film retrospectives), and received manually by Vilmanas' friend Margreta Legaite in Tvaniste – a machine capable of transcribing directly from the steeplemesh having not yet been developed.

A B C D E F G. HAEC TUA PENELOPE LENTO TIBI MITTIT ULISSE.
a b c d e f g. This your Penelope sends to you, long-delayed Ulisses (first line from Ovid's Heroines).

    extract from Vitoria Queiros' 1998 quaterno [textbook] Thirteen Inventions which Changed the World (Trize Invenções que Refiz o Mondo), published by Pontudra Editions.

..be tempting to connect these early developments in distributed communication – so vital to the modern concept of Vicinity – directly to our own tachslates and meshfora [2], but it is important to keep in mind the machinery of the era. Even the self-contained tachygraph was still in its infancy, with most models only capable of setting type at at most half the speed of practised handwriting. The Armanburg Tachygraph (Tachygrafia Armangrod), which was commercially available in Poland from 1842, was so awkward to use that it was lampooned in the bit-sheets as the Bradygrafia (literally the “slow-writer”, tachygraph meaning “fast-writer”).

Moreover, the barrick [electric] steeplepost was still so young that it had not displaced the earlier flag-lamp system, widespread across Spain, Gaul and Italy. With some exceptions (notably the Cracow-Bitom line in southern Poland), the ground was clear for the spread of wired post across northern and eastern Europe, and indeed it is from these places that we get most of the barrick innovations of the late Workshop period (a similar phenomenon is at play in Arabian Cappatia and Lower Mendeva).

So the early wired tachygraphs were cumbersome to use and expensive to…

——
[1] Known in our timeline as the Carrington Event, or the 1859 Solar Storm, this coronal mass ejection caused stong aurorae and wrought havoc on telegraph equipment from August 29th to September 2nd. Some operators even reported being able to send messages along disconnected wires.

[2] A tachslate (Kentish /ˈtak.slɛjt/) is a handheld device capable of sending text messages to other such devices. A meshforum roughly corresponds to our idea of an Internet message board.

##

26m May

lougir /luˈʒɪʀ/ [lʊˈʒɪː] to shimmer, to glimmer, to gleam

that is, to shine intermittently or while flickering

first -ir conjugation, s variant (past participle lous)

< 9C, from Old Boral louger, fouger “to gleam, to flash”, from Latin fulgeō “I flash, I lighten” (see also fourraç “lightning”, from the same root). The initial consonant shift from f to l is difficult to explain, with two main competing theories. First claims metathesis to floger around 6C with subsequent loss of f, although this has holes. More accepted is simply corruption from the similar verb luȝr “to shine” and noun lumnair “light”.

    Y ci ogl de my fraðr lousern sou clar dy lun oppos.

    My brother's eyes gleamed in the light of the full moon.

    /ˌi tsi ˈɔjl ˌde mi ˈfʀa.ðʀ̩ ˈlu.zɛ.ʀn̩ su ˈklaʀ di ˈlɪn o.ˈpɔz/

27m May

porrujon /ˌpo.ʀiˈʒɔn/ balcony, mezzanine

< 10C, from Old Boral prodrujon, podruscon “overhang, charge (military)”, nominalisation of verb prodrure “to thrust forwards, to bulge out”. This from Latin verb prōtrūdō “I push forwards, I protrude”. First r presumably lost to dissimiliation. Architectural senses from 16C; military sense displaced 13C by assaut, from French.

    Poll'un ant porrujon vars aust vos faldra moneð may spendr.

    For one with an east-facing balcony, you'll need to spend more.

    /poˈlɪn ant ˌpoʀiˈʒɔn vaʀz ˈost vo falˈdʀa moˈnɛθ me ˈspɛn.dʀ̩/

##

28m May

    from Portingale author Ezio Carvallo's 1860 trevold Tejan de Masquira ou Jalico (released in Kentish as We Arive in Unwemmed Clothes, translated 1865 by Christopher Wood), after which is named the originally-Spanish masquira genre of stories. These typically feature vigilante characters and plots driven by hidden identities, high society and complicated schemes. It has some overlap with the later spycraft genre, especially in modern works.

    The narrative focus on characters with hidden identities has led several marginal groups to embrace masquira tales, most notably the semi-public tovarick communities in the Riverine Cities. Indeed, the tovarism connection was barely obfuscated in some works (see particularly Takach's We Will Burn Together), and the popularity of the genre is widely understood to have contributed to the Household Renovation.

Ag moment oyon Matthias acavau a scap fair ne cognit dell'aucoum lougent eð y porrujon yembr dessur, y ciel innuoðous au toð residu ne ros soluð e sta affauðant direct vars blau Dachin ; y preu stel lusern freit sur y ci montagn slognað norðer.
By the time Matthias managed to slip away from the glittering crowd and step out onto the balcony, the cloudless sky had lost all trace of pink and was fading directly to a Dackin blue; the first stars twinkled cold above the distant mountains to the north.

Y lumner exiguous dell'Ors Majour vole bogr sy form connoscr par febiltað cant Matthias retornau ac pre lugnt ; all'uncos sarve ig l'yemoy un convoc Escriva, un noit de lun neir.
The scant light of the Great Bear was going to be far too weak to catch his form when he returned in the hex hours; not for nothing had he obtained an Escriva invitation on the night of a new moon.

Un manton a nuot – ben ploy meðes, y heloit survale tojorn y frescur – fos idone parfait, ma l'œcumen Aragonesc avant sta steðig pittoresq com ja.
Cloud cover – or even rain, the noise was always worth the chill – would have been ideal, but the Aragonese landscape before him was as stubbornly picturesque as ever.

29m May

affauðar /ˌa.foˈðaʀ/ [ˌa.fʊˈðɑː] to fade, to weaken, to dilute

< 14C, verbal derivation of fauð “bland, dilute” (now obsolete or archaic), itself from Old Boral fauð “tasteless, useless, boring”. This from Latin fatuus, having the previous senses as well as “foolish, silly, awkward, clumsy”. This word also gives Boral fatuous /fa.ˈtjuz/ “unwieldy, awkward”, a later borrowing. See also Norman affadi “to disgust”, Vascon fat “annoying, boring (of people)”.

    Ne bon mont l'oc hougr affauð pre y fest souscen.

    This discontentment should fade before tomorrow's party.

30m May

exiguous /ˌeg.ziˈgjuz/ scant, meagre, paltry

< late 16C from Middle Boral, borrowed from Middle French exigu “inadequate, paltry” (with addition of the unetymological adjective ending -ous), itself borrowed from Latin exiguus “strict, meagre”. This a derivation from verb exigō “I demand, I test”.

    Ant verdur exiguous com manton, ploy a l'œcumen hamarr gravað.

    With scant greenery for protection, rain has carved out the jagged landscape.

    /ant vɛʀˈdɪʀ ˌɛg.ziˈgjuz kɔm manˈtɔn | ˈplɔj a ˌle.kiˈmɛn haˈmaʀ gʀaˈvaθ/

##

31r May

     continued extract from Vitoria Queiros' 1998 quaterno Thirteen Inventions which Changed the World (Trize Invenções que Refiz o Mondo); see 25th May.

…period (a similar phenomenon is at play in Arabian Cappatia and Lower Mendeva).

So the early wired tachygraphs were cumbersome to use and expensive to get hold of – and furthermore there was little incentive for businesses, who could simply hire a tapper [1] already fluent in the Adamich system (a professional could transmit at a rate of two hundred words per minute). Thus was the wired tachygraph adopted slowly, in fits and starts.

However, in those places where there was early adoption, the machines proved astonishingly popular. These were usually dense cities and urban areas undergoing rapid growth. In Safford [2], for example, which already had a robust postal infrastructure, the young Mayor Crawford oversaw the installation of the first redirection banks in post offices. These pioneered the system in which a given missive would be prepended with a borough code – for example, Makerfell's was MKF – allowing the switchers [3] to send the message through to the correct local post office, followed by a house code – eventually standardised to four digits followed by a letter – whence the message could be transmit to the correct machine.
image

    (above) An outgoing redirection bank, 1879. Not pictured are the spools of paper on which the house codes would be printed in Adamich notation.

    (below) Sender's copy of a letter sent via the Aiga d'Angoixa tachygraph mesh, 1882; additions in parentheses

IVS (Illa de Vinna Salvatge [4]) 86814 J

Good evening, brother! My husband is hosting a evening sorty this weekend; are you and yours otherwise occupied? If not, you are certainly welcome to attend. He wants a lakeside bonfire and connoqueria (connock dance [5]) and everything. It'll probably be the last chance we have before the new-year thaw. Yours, Marta.

——

[1] One employed to operate the steeplepost (that is, a telegraph operator).

[2] Second-largest city in Markland, corresponding to Salford in our timeline.

[3] People who worked at redirection banks, allowing messages to go where they were sent.

[4] literally “Wild Grape Island”, named for its plentiful fruit. We call it Île d'Orléans, in Quebec.

[5] from Russian конeк “ice skate”.

1r Jugn

febiltað /ˌfe.bɪlˈtaθ/ [ˌfe.bɪwˈtah] weakness, feebleness, infirmity

< 10C, nominal derivation from Old Boral feble “weak” (modern Boral febr), itself from Vulgar Latin fēbilis “lamentable, fearful”, a dissimilated form of Latin flēbilis which has the same meaning. Possibly directly from a Latin nominal *flēbilitās, but this is unattested.

    Y rey a febiltað larc ; tu saf i comfy fort demay sull'eç astuçour.

    The king has many weaknesses; you know he trusts his advisors too much.

    /i ˈʀi a ˌfe.bɪlˈtaθ laʀk | ti ˈsaf i kɔmˈfi ˌfɔʀt deˈme si.lɛts ˌas.tiˈtsuʀ/

2m Jugn

survalir /ˌsɪʀ.vaˈlɪʀ/ [ˌsɪː.vɐˈlɪː] to be worth more than, to surpass, to outclass

first -ir conjugation, sporadic (as valir; future stem survaldr-, past participle survalt)

< attested first in 11C as Old Boral sovrvaler “to outdo”, transparently the prefix sovr-, sur- “over, above” with valer “to be worth” (modern valir). Nevertheless, possibly a direct descendant of the Latin verb supervaleō “I am stronger than”, which is seen (though rarely) in Classical sources.

    Accaç campaner survale toð mejan actual de correspondr remot.

    Steeplepost outclassed every other method of long-distance communication.

    /aˈkats ˌkam.paˈnɛʀ ˌsɪʀ.vaˈle tɔθ miˈjan akˈtjal de ˌko.ʀeˈspɔn.dʀ̩ ʀeˈmɔt/

##

3m Jugn

    taken from Silvia Bagnasco's 1937 lovetale romance You Don't Believe in Destiny (originally released in Napolitan as Nu nGolpa sott'e Stelle), first of an acclaimed series of trevolds. The books each follow one member of the Josforo family, and can be read in any order; Bagnasco made it a challenge to her readers to discover all of the correspondences she included between the books.

Macaria Josforo saf ig l'yembra noc y ferscip mattin pront la i sal y vec e respir nell'aer fum e mar de Weston, oc bourg commovent.
Macaria Josforo knows she's missed the morning ferry the moment she steps off the veck and inhales the smoke-sea air of bustling Weston.

Or, par vertað enter y ferscip nað ancour ne havan, ma y ci rig de catoum e de souscrit e de “Carraçon sereð vos sojornant ?” son voit dell'eç cogmaðer ne bonet e marcant ne tædium seyon vis normal.
Well – the ferry is technically still at port, but the queues for stamps and signatures and “What is the purpose of your trip?” are empty of the usual bonneted holidaymakers and bored businessmen.

Lon dout, y vascel arra se deshormejað pre Sr Treman porra sy carc e masctig coglir.
No doubt they'll have unmoored before Mr Tremane can collect her luggage and ticket.

“Crerreð vos nos meritau un hostel sur mar, Treman ?” Macaria a s'aðelið n'Anglesc, toð cas, ða meðes y gendr local reman cos sporadic incomprensibr.
“Do you suppose we merit a sea-front hotel, Tremane?” Macaria's English is improving, at least, although the local variety is still occasionally incomprehensible.

Contrig, sy capabiltað ne Cambresc no's provað in tesquo, eð oy porra i l'ig humiglation particuler repoussar ag crevoscr.
On the other hand, she has never attempted Welsh in tesquo, and now she can save that particular humiliation for this evening.

4m Jugn

heloit /heˈlɔit/ noise, racket, din

rather, noise (often undesired) that impedes communication or listening

< 9C in Old Boral helued, ȝelloud “sound, noise”, from Old Dutch gelúd, past participle of lúdon “to make noise, to sound”.  Development of the Boral diphthong may be under continued influence of the Dutch, and early borrowings of initial /ge, gi/ are notoriously variable.

    Y heloit contondent no sta for un hom y port d'entranç crullant.

5m Jugn

in tesquo /ˌɪn.tɛsˈkwo/ in the wild, in real life, in practice

that is, in an unmonitored or unscripted environment

< 17C, from Scholastic Latin in tesquō with its modern meaning, literally meaning “in the wasteland, in the deserts”. Used originally in natural theory to contrast with older in experientiā “in an experimental setting”, it was coopted into general use in several European languages by the late 19C.

    L'oc solution effectuar in tesquo surcay mout defaglessem.

    Implementing this solution is often more difficult in practice.

    /lɔk ˌso.liˈtsjɔn ˌe.fɛkˈtjaʀ ˌɪntɛsˈkwo sɪʀˈke mut ˌde.fɛjˈlɛ.sɛm/

##

6m Jugn

    in translation from the collected correspondence of Michael Bervisson (17th June 1799–12th January 1849), political historian and social reformist; he and his wife Hilgart were influential in the Borrhatic League, especially with regard to calls for religious tolerance. This letter to his editors is on proposed alterations to his magnum opus Roma Æternal: Eç Vascel a These (An Eternal Rome: Ships of Theseus).

(received on the morning of Tuesday 5th November, 1844 N)
Kind sirs Osgaut and Yfaur,

May this missive find you and the Vithor Historical Institute in good health and well-mint. Samuel and Clovia do better for themselves every time they return from school, although their history tutor's opinions on the collapse of the Medieval Imperia are execrable. I expect they shall both be in the melee before this blasted book is published, in any case; Hilgart is already reading the illustrated costumery in the hopes that we might all be fashionable by March (kindly do not mention hats in her company!).

Only two points must I reiterate regarding the volume. First, I absolutely cannot condense The Wall and The Hedgerow – contrasting Hadrian's policies in Britain and in Borland is vital for understanding their different fates in the post-Classical age, for his reign marks the beginning of our island's greater autonomy. There is one correction: I have not been consistent in writing signa (for example in the name of Aquīs Balneīs), and you may feel free to add them wherever necessary.

Second, I would like to divert as much criticism as we might possibly anticipate (in particular one aquiline fellow who shall not sully my quill) and rebut those arguments directly in the text. To this end, might we extend the chapter Twin Keys to emphasise the ways both the Drengotian Constant Empire to the west and the Malchassian Romans to the east used the Church (in the last century of its unity) to support their claims to continuity with Rome? I shall have written the requisite pages by Revillon, I assure you.

Do not hesitate to reply forthwith,
Professor Michael Bervisson

7m Jugn

souscrit /suˈxʀɪt/ signature, logo

more generally something characteristic of a person, place or group

< late 15C in Middle Boral souscrit “signature, approval, authorisation”, past participle of souscrir “to sign, to authorise” (now obsolete), adapted from Latin subscrībō “I append, I write underneath”, after the practice of signing one's name of the end of documents. Extension to “characteristic feature” 18C; to “logo” 19C as an innovation of the Global Workshop decades.

    Y joun rey vil jamay souscrit dar all'oc concort abhorrent.

    The young king will never give his signature to this despicable treaty.

    /i ʒun ˈʀi vɪl jaˈme suˈxʀɪt daʀ aˌlɔk kɔnˈkɔʀt ˌab.hoˈʀɛnt/
