8m Jugn

havan /haˈvan/ harbour, port, docks

< 10C, from Old Boral havan “harbour” (in southern dialects), from Old Dutch havana with the same meaning. The motivation for the stress shift onto the second syllable is unclear, although compare the cognate haun “haven, safety” – being in Old Boral havn “harbour” – used at that time in the north and borrowed from Norrish.

    Y havan aye aug calm, e sta profont a mar bas meðes.

    The harbour was sheltered, and deep even at low tide.

    /i haˈvan ɛˈje oj ˈkalm | e ˌsta pʀoˈfɔnt a maʀ ˈbaz meˈðɛz/

##

9m Jugn

    extract from Peðer Hetsong's semi-autobiographical 1864 trevold The Land of Golden Lies and Sugared Fire (published originally in New Leudong Cambrick by Allourian Editions), one of the first Chrysian works of literature to enjoy Vetomundine success. Hetsong was of both Cathayan and British heritage, and spent his early adulthood travelling around Workshop Europe, returning to New Leudong in 1858.

Matheu aye malaiç e calt tandic i mene y patrician Morracan tras y manoir assombrið.
Matthew felt queasy and hot as he led the Morrack gentleman through the darkened house.

S'envount nell'oc conjuraçon no sta megl all'act por ci Sr Bocuz au luy menaçað descusion. Parcanç piour ja.
What he was doing now was no better than what Mr. Bocuz had threatened to expose him for. Perhaps even worse.

Y quanga vel certan le desmettr ða l'aye catoum pront d'ig seyon appos tojorn, Matheu partenent – ma l'oc sey fort raçon aðief por ig i l'occy.
The quanga would surely sack him if he had proof in hand of what he'd always suspected about Mathew – but for this, he might very well murder him.

Toð ig fis a cost, luy fale tan s'entrovar : er Gou Makei ne realtað y patrician attendent ?
Besides that, there was the question of whether Lady Mackay was, in fact, expecting the gentleman.

Ða l'en er, volois i noc luy digr la yon se situa sy cambr ?
If she were, wouldn't she have told him where her bedroom was?

Gou Makei se creye starc – e Matheu stima y domn pos un bon defis leyar por loit contr un joun domn de bel figleç calscon, s'il surcayeurn con rasc e cavel remorcað – ma pos i l'un leyar por loit contr un segr adult ?
Lady Mackay thought she was tough—and Matthew reckoned she could hold her own against any other gently-reared young lady, if it came to scratching and pulling hair—but would she be able to against a grown man?

Or, toll'oc for s'i no'r total raut de sy host nocturnal recevir, com ja pos i star.
That is, if she weren't perfectly thrilled by this nocturnal visit, as perhaps she might be.

10m Jugn

surcair /sɪʀˈkɛʀ/ [sɪːˈkɛː] to end up, to turn out, to transpire

-r conjugation, -s variant (past participle surcas, future stem surcarr-)

< 11C, from Old Boral surcarre, sovrcaire, a compound of sovr-, sur- “over, above” (from Latin prefix super- with the same meaning) and the verb carre, caire “to fall, to be defeated, to happen”. This a calque from Germanic verbs all meaning literally “to fall out”; the consensus regards Old Dutch útfallan as the most likely origin.

    Ne l'oc við temerer parmanent vos surcarreð mort.

    Keep up this reckless behaviour and you'll end up dead.

    /ne lɔk ˈvɪθ ˌte.meˈʀɛʀ ˌpaʀ.maˈnɛnt vo ˌsɪʀ.kaˈʀɛθ mɔʀt/

11m Jugn

catoum /kaˈtum/ seal, stamp

also proof, certificate; see verb catoumar “to validate, to certify”

< 14C in Middle Boral, borrowed either from French catoume or Vascon catuma (both meaning “seal, stamp”); in any case from Moorish Arabic خَتْم‎ (khatm) “seal” via Portingale catuma – this presumably had initial stress at some point but has penultimate stress in all later borrowings. Sense of certification present in the Gallic languages so is probably older than the Boral borrowing. Verb form is later (17C?) but is similarly a parallel development.

    Y doncel fis y quayer e rescau y catoum dy novel lettr.

    The girl put down her diary and broke the new letter's seal.

    /i dɔnˈtsɛl fɪz i kwɛˈjɛʀ e ʀeˈxo i kaˈtum di ˌno.vɛl ˈlɛ.tʀ̩/

##

12m Jugn

    taken from A Comprehensive Cyclopædia of Pre-Fracture Europe, written by Martin and Elisabeth Forman and illustrated by Goffrey Jordan; published 1937 by Winchester Scholastic Primers.

…was thankfully less variation across Europe in these units than in, for example, the league (as we have seen).

§The Second Tetrachy

Refers to the period of roughly one hundred and fifty years for which much of Europe was under the (occasionally theoretical) rule of empire. Known also – especially in older texts – as the Medieval Imperia, or as the Roman Decadence.

The most limited timeframe to which this title routinely refers is from 1091, with the consolidation of Bohemia and Lower Pannonia into the German Empire under Bertholdt II, until 1170, when the Emergency at Dijon led to the establishment of an independent Kingdom of Burgundy (and soon the dissolution of the Constant Empire). More usual is to include everything from the Norman-led subjugation of Kent (and installation of the House of Kernow in Dumnonia) in 1029 to the death of Daniel IV (the Drengotian domains being much divided among his family and friends) in 1191.

The four empires constituting the Second Tetrarchy were:

    the aforementioned Constant Empire, under the Drengot dynasty and comprising for most of its duration the kingdoms of West Francia, Wessex, Markland and Dumnonia.
    the German Empire, in particular under the Houghbrocken and Andex dual imperium, and including more than a dozen states – principally the Kingdom of Saxony, the Duchy of Willemy and the Kingdom of Bohemia.
    the Empire of the Hellenes (also called the Second Roman Empire), especially considered from its territorial maximum in 1026, controlling lands from Corsica to the shores of the Hazaran Sea.
    the Single Caliphate ruled from Cairo, primarily after the centralisation reforms of the early eleventh century (although the further territories such as the Admiralty of Cordoba or the Turkish lands were never as closely-ruled).

Even at the height of imperial control, regions within these states often had significant autonomy. In 1105, the Kingdom of Greater Sicily even went to war with…

13m Jugn

masctig /maxˈtaj/ authorisation, admission, ticket, pass

< 16C as masctig /maʃˈtiː/ “letter of marque, authorisation”, deverbal from masctighar “to authorise, to permit (especially as of a royal decree)”. This verb is borrowed from Middle Dutch maȝtiȝn “to authorise”, from maȝtiȝ “powerful, influential”, and originates in Classical German mahtīgas (see English mighty, Vallandic mātgur). Used to denote a physical ticket of admission from 19C, first in reference to theatrical production, later also for transport.

    Jo le creu joculant ne me dignt dou masctig volen 60⍚ valir!

    I thought he was joking when he said two tickets would cost 60⍚!

    /ʒo le ˈkʀaw ˌʒo.kiˈlant ne me ˈdajnt ˌdu maxˈtaj voˈlɛn siˈzant ʒeˈma vaˈlɪʀ/

14m Jugn

cogmaðer /ˌkɔj.maˈðɛʀ/ [ˌkɔj.mɐˈðɛː] holidaymaker, vacationer

that is, someone who is on holiday

< first attested in modern sense 1819 in the Peujon Rous a Viðor (The Vithor Red Dove, a weekly newspaper with antigovernmental leanings; it supported in particular the mozard protests against the Concert during the Second German War), from cogmað “time off, holiday” with the suffix -er, forming agents or adjectives. The word is seen earlier in 17C meaning “recreational, related to pastimes” but this is an independent usage already obselete by the late 18C. The noun cogmað itself from Old Boral coȝmað “leave, time off”, from Latin commeātus “supplies, convoy, leave”.

    L'hostel peninsoler sur Ausbagn fornira tout por y cogmaðer particuler.

    The peninsular hotel near Ausbagn will provide everything for the discerning holidaymaker.

    /lɔsˈtɛl peˌnɪn.soˈlɛʀ sɪʀ ozˈbɛjn ˌfɔʀ.niˈʀa tut pɔʀ i ˌkɔj.maˈðɛʀ paʀˌti.kiˈlɛʀ/

15m Jugn

    from Polish author Borys Cr̦emienj's 1912 trevold Z̦ebysj Vyședl (So You would Leave), released in Kentish 1924 as Ruing All I Say. The third in a set of post-secession middleage trevolds, each book following a character mentioned in passing in the previous.

Dy tarn y camin torque, passant doutr y forsc e l'eç apothecary, doutr eç nou de majon e de farm. Cos final, Tomaş trovau Andŗey asseyent sur picq tombr ag tarm dy bourg, y ciel sgardant haut. Y deskeðant sta ig l'aloud d'Andŗey se situa a sy cost a plaç d'a sy man.
The path from the tavern snaked past the forge and the herbalists, past clusters of houses and farmland. Finally, Tomaş found Andŗey sitting on a small hill at the edge of town, looking up at the sky. The troubling thing was that Andŗey's lute was by his side instead of in his hands.

Pre poðe Tomaş parolar, Andŗey tornau luy spiar, e la sta uncos pogntous e rendent tait ne sull'ogl. Sou y fosc meðes, Tomaş n'aye alcun defy ne l'acier ag massoir d'Andŗey ben l'ort de sy menton veint.
Before Tomaş could speak, Andŗey turned to look at him, and there was something sharp and silencing in his eyes. Even in the darkness, Tomaş had no trouble seeing the steely set to Andŗey's jaw, the lift of his chin. 

“Jo crey deïr te digr ig jo's t'amoraçað,” dis Andŗey, e con tan Tomaş forn condoit a y ci pousson de sy cour rendr stabr, il luy scapaurn comoscon slançant. “Me compt noc ða scey un part de tey, oc y bempart usant meðes, ancour Alexia amant ; saivað ig j'ay my gant jait dessou. Ða jamay te dis jo noc ig mell'auror e mell'occas son y tien, jo sceus noc me pardonnar.”
“I think I ought to tell you that I'm in love with you,” Andŗey said, and though Tomaş had been trained to steady the beating of his heart, he found it racing away from him. “It doesn't matter to me if part of you, even most of you, still loves Alexia; know that my gauntlet is cast. If I never told you that my sun rises and sets with you, I should never forgive myself.”

16m Jugn

descusion /ˌde.xiˈzjɔn/ revelation, betrayal, uncovering

especially the revealing of something secret

< first attested 1687 in Messegn's opera Montagn Inscas (Unclimbed Mountains), non-etymological nominalisation from descondr “to reveal, to betray, to tell (a secret)”. This verb from des-, a prefix that inverts meaning, and scondr “to hide, to cover up, to keep secret”, itself from Latin abscondō “I cover, I conceal”. This form presumably by analogy from pairs such as contondr ~ contusion “to strike ~ bruise”.

    Y descusion de vostrell'yam porra noc pardon cavir tan fagl.

    Your uncle's betrayal cannot be so easily forgiven.

17m Jugn

desmettr /dɛsˈmɛ.tʀ̩/ [dɪzˈmɛ.tɐ] to fire, to sack

in formal writing to shun, to not acknowledge

< 12C in Old Boral desmetre “to send away, to reject”, modelled after Latin dīmittō “I dismiss”, from dis- “apart, away” and mittō “I send, I release”. Senses of shunning from 14C in religious contexts and 16C more generally. Modern meaning from 19C, replacing laiscar “to leave, to let go”.

    Horreð vos il veln vos desmettr ða vos lou refusað ?

    Are you scared they'll fire you if you refuse?

    /hoˈʀɛθ vo ɪl ˈvɛ.ln̩ ˌvo dɛsˈmɛ.tʀ̩  ða ˈvo lu ˌʀe.fiˈzaθ/

##

18m Jugn

    in translation from the 1998 scholastic quire Sviluppi in Philosophia dal Periodo Revitalisto (in English, Developments in Philosophy since the Revitalist Period) by historical deixist Jovanna Ariperto, researcher at the Autonome University of Saint Marinus in Lombardy. 

(excerpted from Chapter 8, titled Forging the First Link: Problems of Infinite Regression)

…popularised by the works of Dufresne and Fayeda in the last decade of the seventeenth century, this theory asserted that all life had formed at the moment of Creation, and that each successive generation grew from miniature versions of themselves (called by Dufresne zoia, not to be confused with our modern use of the term for one-case life [1]), themselves containing yet-smaller forms for the grandchild generation, and so on. Contemporary diagrams showing a tiny human form inside a spermcase illustrate this theory along with the concurrent idea that the mother was merely the soil where the baby, planted by the father, grew.

Setting aside issues of predestination inherent in this approach—though of course this rendered the theory anathema or even heretical in the Augsburg dominia [2]—the immediate objection (see al-Siddik's 1696 work On the Children of Zeno) was that you would need to posit an infinite chain of progressively-smaller zoia to be present inside every male animal on the planet. There were two broad categories of response: first, Dufresne and many of the more theologically-minded argued that the Lord had merely provided enough zoia to last until the Apocalypse [3]. This argument could never satisfy all, however.

The response more interesting for our purposes is exemplified in Vallakis' 1702 treatise The Generic Impossibility of Self-Duplication within a Bounded Form. In the book Vallakis argues that any organism that can reproduce, or more generally anything capable of duplication, must have a sculptor guiding the division. The argument proceeds as follows:

    A self-duplicating bounded [4] structure A must contain within it all the structures necessary to create a copy of itself, which together we may call B. But then B contains everything necessary to create itself, say C. Thus we must have a descending chain of structures, each of which contains everything necessary to reconstruct the original A. But this is absurd, as A is bounded and cannot contain arbitrarily-small parts. Thus in fact A can divide only under the intervention of a guiding sculptor.

For several decades, thus, mainstream theory held only two opinions on the mechanism of life: either that all life contained an infinite chain of zoia within, or that each time new life was created it was guided in that moment by the plans of the Creator. This state of affairs would not change until the publishing in 1743 of…

—

[1] singular zoium, from Scholastic Latin and derived from a diminutive of Greek ζῷον “animal”. We would say “microbe” or “unicellular organism”.

[2] those lands under the religious authority of the Augsburg See.

[3] that is, the End Times, or the Second Coming.

[4] here intended to mean “of finite extent and containing finite information”, or something similar. A mathematical understanding of quantity and extent would not be formalised until the nineteenth century.

20m Jugn

aloud /aˈlud/ lute

also deit aloudan, literally lutist's fingers, to describe particularly nimble, subtle or dextrous work

< 13C, from late Old Boral alout, alaud “lute” etc., from Old Vascon alouda, aleut via Old French alaude (modern French laude /lod/ with rebracketing). This is from  Arabic اَلْعُود‎ (al-ʿūd “wood”), probably via a 10C Old Spanish alouda or similar. See also Roman aliuto, Barcelon leut; the masculine variant appears to have spread east from Aquitaine.

    Tras port oye jo un hom harp ben aloud crullant.

    Outside I could hear someone playing a harp or a lute.

21r Jugn

    in continued translation from the 1998 scholastic quire Sviluppi in Philosophia dal Periodo Revitalisto [1] by historical deixist Jovanna Ariperto, researcher at the Autonome University of Saint Marinus in Lombardy. 

(excerpted from Chapter 8, titled Forging the First Link: Problems of Infinite Regression)

…plans of the Creator. This state of affairs would not change until the publishing in 1743 of Fable du Relieur Mut [1] by Rachel Desselut, daughter of explorer and infamous libertine Lord Bouvoin. The relevant portion is reproduced here.

    And yet he could not take Marcuccio as his apprentice, for how was he to instruct a pupil in the art of bookbinding? But although Ignazo could not speak, he could write. For a year and a day he wrote, describing all his methods and arts, from the pedigree of sheep for the parchment, to the age of the oak tree for the ink. In this way he taught Marcuccio in detail the production of a book exactly like the one he had himself written, save that the copy would be utterly blank. Upon the last page, Ignazio wrote his final instruction: Et omnia hic scripta scribas illic—And all that is written here, you shall write therein. And thus Marcuccio received the book, and studied from it. And after many months did he return to Ignazio, twin books in his arms.

Desselut's work lays out the separation of Form and of Method, and how they allow for the unsculpted duplication of a bounded being. However, her work would go unrecognised for nearly two decades (due predominantly to the disruption of the Democratic Wars), during which time she would be forced to flee first to Norman Kent [2] and later to Provence, via Portingale. It was in these years that she first began corresponding with mathematician Raymond al Mustafa, who was at the time resident in Barcelon under the Duke of Aragon's patronage. Their letters offer a tantalising glimpse into early methodics [3], although both were more interested in the implications for life. Desselut inferred the existence of a vital language (in her own writing she called it the Divine Word) present in the cases [4] of the body. Despite later rumours, she probably did not believe this language was literally Hebrew, although she made several analogies in her publications.

In 1770 the first real self-describing book was written by an anonymous author and donated to the University of Hulm. The university has duplicated the book once every four years in as exact a way as possible, and the original, alongside the most recent copy, may be viewed in their Department of Heredity building. In a peculiar twist, since the early twentieth century the ink has had to be sourced from a different tree than is specified in the book, as the…

—

[1] Developments in Philosophy since the Revitalist Period; Parable of the Mute Bookbinder

[2] That is, Normandy and Flanders.

[3] Computing, or programming, although at this point it is not even at the level of, say, our Ada Lovelace and Charles Babbage.

[4] Cells: this vital language is of course DNA (known eventually in this world as heredian acid or, less commonly, as nucalic acid).

Y nouç casel, nommað tan y nux, er primer mis a regest annað 1713, posc bre y descovart toð recostroint all'heir Arnoutzen de við unicasel.
The case kernel or the nux [cell nucleus] was first documented in 1713, shortly after the younger Arnoutzen's revolutionary discovery of one-case life.

Ny ci diagram a sull'aïðour, y Lithovan Marion Hartman (cos frequentessem connuð com fin portant dy Var Russian poll'Austmar), nos veim casel vegetal—plausibr ugnlac sauvaç—stikettað cascun con “punctulus”, ben picq pognt.

##

27m Jugn

    recipe taken from the 1763 mealbook A Complete Lithing of the Towns and Cities of Markland, for the Edification of the Good Food Amateur, written by independent traveller Lord Carrow, Baron of Ebbasham [Epsom]. Although popular across Albion, Ireland and Borland (the Borlish translation being infamously inaccurate), it never sold in large numbers on the continent or further afield. His later volumes, An Account of the Vittles of the Various German Peoples and On the Sundry Uses of the Shadome Fruit [tomato], were more enduring, being republished several times even after his death.

The Lonxter [Lancaster] Yatherpot

Lonxter is a castle town on the banks of the Lone, being the northernmost city and river along the western coastline of Markland. Its signature dish is the yatherpot, a filling mutton or lamb meal to be prepared for a large family with its distinctive sliced tartoffer covering. The name is simply Merch for “together pot”, as it is a dish one can cook in a single large pot or bowl.

Wares (for six)
5ι butter or lard
3п6ι lamb or mutton per frangos [strips]
4 sheep's codnere [kidney], trimmed
2 large onions, 6 carrots, sliced
3ι rough flour
some salt, molasses, vinegar, spices per gustibus
1½ pint lamb or chick broth
3 leaves dried lorrer [bay leaf]
3п6ι tartoffer, peeled and sliced

Method

    Melt a little of the butter in a large shallow dish and sear the lamb in batches, remove to a plate, then repeat with 3 trimmed and sliced lamb kidneys, the onions and the carrots (these last two remaining).
    Dust with flour, leave for two minutes, then add the seasoning and broth, and allow to boil. Pour in the lamb, kidneys and lorrer and remove from the fire.
    Arrange the tartoffer by slices over the meat and then glaze with hot butter. Place on a lid and cook for 1+½ hours on a low fire.
    Once the tartoffer are well-browned, remove from the fire and serve immediately.

largeç /laʀˈgɛts/ a large quantity, much, plenty

(with a negative verb) little, scant, paucity

< 13C, a late Old Boral derivation of adjective larc, larg “broad, large, (dialect) many” with nominal ending -eç. The adjective from Latin largus “abundant, plentiful”; the ending from Latin suffix -itia, denoting condition or property.

    L'oc quil a largeç de roum la porreu nos dou dormir afont.

    There is plenty of room in this bed for us both to sleep in it.

Surrounded by three empires, Italy at the beginning of the twelfth century is a volatile place. image

Italy, 1120 N

    The Papal Dominium
        Ruled from Rome, Seat of all Christendom, by Pope Damian V (1110-1121). Born Humbert of Ravenna in 1047 N.
    Duchy of Tuscany
        Ruled from Florence by Duchess Matilda (1102-1146) of the Pisan dynasty
    Kingdom of Lombardy
        Ruled from Pavia, former capital of the Kingdom of the North Lombards, which fell to Lombardy in 987 N. Ruled by King Jacob III (1087-1130) from the Verona branch of the Adelchians.
    Republic of Jeans
        Led at this time by Console Franco Cicala of the Cicale merchants (1119-1122 ?).
    Republic of Venise
        At a momentary territorial maximum, having overtaken Ancona and its Corfu holding in 1112 N. Probably ruled by Parre Branco Magnossone (ca. 1120 - 1125, 1136-1142 N).

surcrescr /sɪʀˈkʀɛ.xʀ̩/ [sɪːˈkʀɛ.xɐ] to be in excess, to be extra, to be left over, to overflow

also surcrescent extra, additional; surcresc excess, addition, leftovers (both from 13C)
and par surcresc in addition, moreover, furthermore (from 16C, probably influenced by French pour/par surcroist with the same meaning)

< 11C in Old Boral sovrcrescre et al. “to overflow, to outgrow”, from sovr-/sur- “over” < Latin super- and crescre “to grow” < Latin crēscō “I increase, I grow”. Mostly displaced in the literal senses of overflowing by the end of the seventeenth century, with sovrar “to surpass, to overtake” < Latin superō “I surmount, I rise over” moving into this semantic space.

    Par surcresc, je Sambað nos porreu toð y surcrescent pascr.

3m Juglot

    Anscon's well-known climactic speech from Borlish playwright Osctra Jeichey's 1887 work L'Aragn (The Spider), which was also shown in theding across Europe and adapted to film many times, most notably the loose New Provincial 1930 retelling La Rogna, which swapped the sex of many of the characters (although not that of the protagonist, giving the film tovarick undertext rather unusual for the Mendevan polity at this time).

“Or vazdara jo pieð ne Tartary.
"Oh, kindly fuck off to Tartary.

Bravessem ne Scin, ne Cathay—yonoscon eç monð slognað, sou ciel la alcun hom porra vossy parollaç zucarrað oïr,
Even better, to China or Cathay—somewhere months away, a land where none can understand your fucking candied words,

vostrell'eç suggestion jonnovar me torquent e me tirant e m'ammenant vars me fornir coll'annihilation a my mesur!
your butterfly-innocent suggestions that twist and pull and guide me to build myself a bespoken annihilation!

Vos eð y mont atorn forgað ne livr tellard, toð inscaut par sign e histoir deluïscent,
You have forged the world around me into a mapbook, all inscribed with false signs and histories,

por vel jo vostrell'eç camin cadarað sevir e vostrell'eç conjuraçon accommettr!
that I might follow your distilled pathways and undertake your designs!

Un semman may soul dell'oc foltað e jo volois my sour meðes, my neuç occir ; erað vos desirant ig jo me suicy toð cas final ?
One week more of this madness and I should have killed my own sister, my niece; was I meant to take my own life in the end?

Ben creyað vos m'aïr anim corros tan aðief ig jo pos me resouvr all'ersteigon de vossy mal?
Or did you believe you had so thoroughly corroded my soul that I could endure the ersteigung [apex, crest, sforzando] of your evil?”

The German Empire at the height of the Second Tetrarchy in 1120 N is characterised by tensions between the dual kingdoms of Saxony and Bavarn—the empire at this point is ruled jointly by the Hoghbrocken and Andex dynasties, ruling respectively as King Brunow III from Bardwick and Conrad II from Regensburg. The imperial seat had not yet been fixed; in 1120 N they were administering the Empire from Frankford. Furthermore, the Empire is at this moment quite possibly the most centralised polity in Europe and Near Asia, although this state of affairs would not last for long…

8m Juglot

< from late 18C in various forms vasdar~vavdar~vodara, spreading from northern dialect to Damborn street slang, and then to other cities along the capital river. 9m Juglot

    scitation questions (in translation) from the  University Faculty of Domain History, from the course Religion in the Second Millenium: Belief, Community and Division, given Summer 2007 N following a reconfiguration of their course offering.

Question 1 (60 minutes)
Write a short speech aimed at the interested layman advocating the three religious developments of the last millenium in Europe which you hold to have been most influential. You should introduce the events with appropriate context, discuss their similarities and differences, and justify their importance. For example, you might choose one of

    the German Secession; the Great Fracture; the Deviance Movement

    the First Crusade; the Muslim migrations northward into the Russias and Tartary; the Prodigal Faiths from Mendeva and Cappatia

Question 2 (120 minutes)
This question focuses in particular on the emergence of Deviance Theology (the Anti-Paradise Movement) in the late eighteenth and early nineteenth centuries.

(i) What evidence is there that <Deviance> originates as a mistranslation from Wessern, and meant at first <defiance, rebellion>? Consider both the semantic plausibility and—crucially—the significance of <defiance> to the Promethean clubs across Albion; the term of course is better evidenced in their Danawan counterparts.

(ii) In both Albion and the Danaw Kingdom at this time there were significant religious minorities and the Church (of whichever stripe) was strongly associated with local authority. Was it these commonalities that made them fertile ground for Anti-Paradise thought?

(iii) To what extent was the resurgence of the Modest Arrangement in Spain and Gaul in reaction to these new religious ideas? Compare the fervence of Modest thinkers like the Marquis de Caillat or proto-Blue Marian Diana Theodora Queiroz to the philosophers of earlier Modest-like movements.

(iv) In your opinion, who is, or are, the most likely author(s) of the incendiary 1799 publication Et Nous Amerons les Fux (And We Will Love the Fire), a seminal work in Deviance Theology? Back up your theory with modern and contemporary sources, and contrast the likelihood of at least two other candidates.

11m Juglot

camin cadarað /kaˈmɪn ˌka.daˈʀaθ/ [kɐˈmɪn ˌka.dɐˈʀah] primrose path, easy life, life of hedonism, the road to hell

that is, a deceptively easy or appealing decision of series of decisions, which however lead one astray or to damnation—especially choices offered by a malevolent actor

< 17C, coined by writer and philosopher Caðoval Thoitom in his 1641 allegory Through the Heghlant Forest, or the Ruin of Little Lady Alissa, literally meaning “distilled pathway”. The noun camin “way” is an Old Boral borrowing, either from Norman or Spanish sailors, from Late Latin cammīnus “way”, itself from Gaulish. The adjective cadarað “distilled” is the past participle of cadarar, a slightly later borrowing from Spanish, presumably related to Arabic قطرات <qaṭarāt> “droplets”, in reference to the process of distillation.

    Y camin cadarað de við luxuir e crous a larc hom cogntið.

    The primrose path of a luxurious and hollow life has trapped many.

    /i kaˈmɪn ˌka.daˈʀaθ de ˈvɪθ lɪkˈsajʀ e ˈkʀuz a laʀk ˈɔm kɔjnˈtɪθ/

12m Juglot

    taken from The Latin Republics: Jewels under Tyrian Standards by Jacob Trelaune, published 1902 by the Yievle Brethin Primers and translated into Borlish when it was republished in the 1940s.

Malgrað, piratary spas graç all'Abanc Novomondin ne 1471 eð all'Ascendanç vascon coherent. A Marsegl, la stan gazou frequent aðief por ig plusour ageurn com ða y citað fos occupað ; donc y risc fuyeurn heilant vars scurtað certanessem.
However, piracy thrived with the Novomundine landfall in 1471 and the accompanying Ascendancy of Vascony. In Marsella, the raiding became so frequent that many behaved as though the city were occupied, the wealthy fleeing to safer pastures inland.

Y ci rekest por aïðanç stant fait a Rudolf VIII remaneurn plurem lon respos. Por cartað, l'er parcanç divertið pall'ig conflict bavarian continu, ma cos usual nos dirreu y rey joun er just curant for a luy (ben a cost de luy, a sell'amour nombrous) eð a festeul spendent sur lac.
The aid requests presented to Rudolf VIII went largely unanswered. Charitably, one might say he was distracted by the ongoing Bavarian conflict, but it is generally allowed that the young king was simply concerned only with himself (or by extension his many lovers) and with resplendent lakeside banquets.

Toð yemoy sell'Ersteigon ag closiscment dy siecr cinqueç. Segr Daniel Menton dy lign Amigòt au y soutien de Marc V de Franç garantið, coll'astuç dy general-orphan Guillen daus Sanz. Cossy preparað, luy e plusour famigl nobr d'autr deposern libertað deur Genef y veint-primer Jugn 1499.
Things came to a head in the last years of the fifteenth century. Lord Daniel Menton of the house of Amigòt had secured the aid of King Marc V of France, on the advice of the foundling-general Guillen daus Sanz. Thus prepared, he and several other noble families declared independence from Geneva on the twenty-first of June, 1499.

##

15m Juglot

    from a review in the Fiellas Dimenja (Sunday Leaves/Sheets), a Marsellan weekly newspaper incorporated in 1871. It focuses primarily on recreation and entertainment, unlike the older weeklies dating back to the sixteenth century, which were limited more to “high-brow” topics.

14th October, 1950

…welcomed back from the New Cambrick facilities; Captains Kerry and Montpiu landed safely at Fort daus Sanz early Friday morning.

REVIEW: Zachariah

A departure from Kiawaman's usual fare, this blacklayer [horror] tale takes us back to the 1820s amid the disintegration of the Long Peace and the beginning of Reaction War. From the gaslit streets of independent Boxa [~Novi Sad, Serbia] to the recovering shell of Stamboul [Istanbul/Constantinople], this adaptation of Petcov's classic trevold [novel] Majat vCherveno (The Red Man) tries to recreate the dread and mystery of watching our four heroes slowly discover the true nature of the eponymous Zachariah.

Unfortunately, the beautiful landscape shots and the addition of a rich Tavancine tourist (whose plotline is mostly borrowed from the book's Elenor storyline) are the high points of an otherwise messy film. In an attempt to incorporate some of the more traditional aspects of vampire folklore, Kiawaman grants Zachariah mystic powers and has the heroes defeat him by means of a sanctified wooden stake (not, as in the book and in popular wisdom, by burning). These new powers, in the hands of a better writer, could have set up a dynamic between Zachariah and Vania as artists of the same craft—instead, we are given the tired trope of a woman torn between the affections of two men. It doesn't help that the audience barely believes there is any choice to make; Vania has no chemistry with either lead.

The symbolism of fire and butterflies that permeated the book is also entirely absent from this adaptation. Apparently afraid to present Zachariah as light or good in any way, we get a cold manor house and bat-infested (!) roofs. Never mind the rich history of the vampire as a figure clinging to life in all its forms, in the blood of the living, in the dancing flames and in the metamorphosis of the butterfly. The film relies heavily on gory fights (with what is clearly wine as a bad stand-in for blood, in a disappointing reversal) and a pounding orchestra who leave the few quiet scenes no room to breathe.

This will be no classic: come only for the views (of beautiful forests, and a bevy of handsome actors, I concede) and two hours of mindless tragedy. - Jacint Brunat

16m Juglot

recoglenç /ˌʀe.kɔjˈlɛnts/ [ˌʀe.kʊjˈlɛ̃s] harvest, production, manufacture

< regular nominal derivation from recoglir “to reap, to harvest”, from Latin recolligō “to collect, to gather again”. First used outside an agricultural context in the seventeenth century, with an uptick and entry into general usage in the nineteeth during the Global Workshop decades.

    Y scaðr tissour Tiong parmis recoglenç d'accostur laboriosessem.

    The Tiong loom allowed clothing to be produced more efficiently.

    /i ˈxa.ðʀ̩ tiˈsuʀ ˈtjong paʀˈmɪz ˌʀe.kɔjˈlɛnts ˌda.kɔsˈtɪʀ laˌbo.ʀjoˈzɛ.sɛm/ 

##

17m Juglot

sunçon /sɪnˈtsɔn/ use, usage, consumption, utilisation

< from Old Boral (co)sumzon [ko.sʏmˈtsɔn] “use, usage”, either a synchronic nominal derivation from sumbre “to take, to use” (from Latin sūmō “I take, I acquire, I employ”), which gave Middle Boral sumir “to take, to use” before falling into disuse, or else a direct descendant from Latin nominalisation cōnsūmptiō “consumption, wasting”.

    Ac compt for sunçon e noc mandað.

    Here only usage counts, not prescription.

    /ak kɔmpt fɔʀ sɪnˈtsɔn e nɔk manˈdaθ/

18m Juglot

    the first few lines of a student's repsonse to 2013 Question 5, Paper 1 from the MSC standard course Alchemy Actual sou Contingent (Modern Alchemy in Context).

5. Descriað y sunçon (actual e ne histoir) de calamin.
5. Describe the usage, historical and modern, of calamine.

Calamin, nommað tan jast igniac, es matier reconnuð ne casc loy dy mont des antictað.
Calamine, or igniac jast [zinc oxide], is a substance known since antiquity across the world.

Ig inclus, ag siecr 3aN ja ern y Roman calamin benoçant por bras forgar, n'oc fondent con cuivr.
For example, as early as the 3rd century aN the Romans were using calamine to make brass, by smelting it with copper.

Plusour scet, y sestertius parmig (veyað y starç, un scet borlesc postimperial) fosn quinað ne bras posc y reform augustin de 23aN—il ig nommaurn orichalcum.
Several coins, including the sestertius (cf. the starce, an early Borlish coin) were minted from brass after the 23aN Augustine reforms—they called it orichalcum.

Calamin a y parsan d'orteyanç eð autr machy a piel soutenuð ny remed tras annað millener, eð ancour sarf i de nossy jorn com part ne crem a cavel.
Calamine has served as a treatment for rashes and other skin blemishes for thousands of years, and it is still used today as a component of hair creams.

Sur production atelier l'oc es matier hagr por gom faus fair, eð a mesur dell'eç dezein recent lamp a lumner PAD ant calamin afont son entrað ne benoç commun.
In workshop manufacture it is an essential part of pseudogum [synthetic rubber] production, and in the last few decades daplight lamps [LED lightbulbs] using calamine have entered common use.

19m Juglot

scet /xɛt/ [çɛt] coin, token

also one of the suits in (Romantic) playing cards, represented by a circle

< 12C, from early Kentish shett “coin, money, wealth” (replaced by 14C by originally-Norman coing “wedge” < Latin cuneus) via Anglian communities in Borland. Used for the card suit from 16C with the spread of the Romantic style across western Europe, calqued from Dutch munte “coin, piece of currency”.

    Y solt es un scet prent ne cuivr jusc 1134 e n'argent apres.

    The shilling is a coin made of copper until 1134 and silver afterwards.

    /i ˈsɔlt ɛz ɪn ˈxɛt ˈpʀɛnt ne ˈka.vʀ̩ ʒɪç ˈɔntsɛnt tʀɛŋˈkatʀ̩ e naʀˈʒɛnt aˈpʀɛz/

##

20m Juglot

parsan /paʀˈsan/ [pɐːˈsan] recovery, healing

< from 12C Old Boral parsan “cure, act of healing”, deverbal noun from parsanar “to cure, to heal (someone)"—this later shifts to intransitive "to get better, to recover” and probably pulls the noun along with it. This verb is a direct descendant of Latin persānō “I heal (someone) thoroughly or completely”, intensive form of sānō “I heal, I cure”; see also sānus > san “healthy”.

    Por y lign de Natagl e Joseph, oy no fo la spien a parsan.

    For Natalia and Joseph's line, there was now no hope of recovery.

    /pɔʀ i ˈlajn de naˈtɛjl e ʒoˈzɛf | ɔj no ˈfo la ˈspɛm a paʀˈsan/

##

21r Juglot

    taken from A Politic Mapbook of Middle-Age Europe (4th edition), by various authors, published 2005 by Winchester Scholastic

Albion and Ireland, 1028 Nascentiæ

This year is the last in pre-Drengotian Albion. 1029 will bring the Kernovan Conjuring and Norman Accession, and the succeeding decades will see what might have been the new steady state of these islands disintegrate.

In the south, Kent under Rodulf is stable and much expanded from its position in the 9th and early 10th centuries, having subdued Wessex (minus Anglia, already lost to Markland in the Treaty of Oxford) but suffered renewed Dumnonian pushes from the west. Canterbury's most powerful vassals lie to the north in Essex across the Thames, ably run by Alfred the Tall. The elderly patriarch Rodulf of Kent lingers on his deathbed after eight decades of political manœuvring.

To the west we have the ever-fractious kingdoms of Ireland, lately unified as but four (with the breaking of the Ultagh in the north-east): Tyrconnal, Connaght, Lenster and Munster. The tales of Tyrconnal and Lenster are dominated by wars at once with the Norrish to the east and the other Irish to the west, while the houses of Connaght and Munster vie for the position of High King of Ireland.

The north holds the confederate Kingdoms of the Danes, newly untethered from overseas rule. The once-mighty kingdom of Northumbria is rendered but one province of the whole, yet the city of Yorwick remains the richest city on Albion (thanks to economic ties in all directions). Still more northerly lie the two-minded Kingdom of Scotland and the last remains of Horther rule in Albion persist in Orkney and Shetland.

Between all these we find those bitter rivals, Markland and Wales. Cadwallan, erstwhile king of Wynneth, finds himself the unexpected ruler of all Wales—a fortuitous position of strength against equally formidable Markland under Oswin the Becoming.

22m Juglot

orteyanç /ɔʀˈtjants/ [ʊːˈtjãs] rash, skin abrasion or irritation

in metaphor an annoying person

< 14C faux-nominal extension of Middle Boral orty, ortig “rash, inflammation”, from Latin urtīca “stinging nettle” via Old Boral urtiȝ, ortiȝ. Used for various other skin illnesses in the mediæval and revitalist periods; used for people from 18C.

    Y Rey a un orteyanç se caut ancour ; scan mey pommat!

    The King has a rash again—bring me up some ointment!

    /i ˈʀi a ɪn ɔʀˈtjants se ˈkot anˈkuʀ | ˈxan mi poˈmat/

23m Juglot

com balanç /ˈkɔm baˈlants/ [ˌkɔm.bɐˈlãs] in return, as recompense, in exchange

< 15C, expression meaning “as an exchange”, from balanç “exchange, recompense, compensation, restitution”, 12C deverbal from Old Boral balançar “to balance, to equalise, to make fair”. This is a borrowing from Old French balancer, from balance “scales, balance”, from Late Latin bilanx “balance” (literally “two dishes”).

    Vos pouð noc peðir jo vos aðeil lon nien com balanç.

    You cannot ask that I grant you land for nothing in return.

    /vo puθ ˈnɔk peˈðɪʀ ʒo vo‿zaˈðil lɔn ˈʀjɛn kɔm baˈlants/

24m Juglot

    the first few lines from 1968 giftale [1] Escrivèn saus Murs a Pompei (We are Writing on the Walls of Pompeii), written by Theodòr fi Christou, New Provencial writer and former diplomat, having worked in Gurjat, Tecamaw and most notably mid-Establishment Ghana. 

Leonta trou y zetr parmy sy fougl facsimilað sur alchemy ag moment apen ig l'yem cas luy, sy sac spal vogdant tras sur y tabr a quelt.
Leonta finds the zetter [note] in among her alchemical copysheets almost as soon as she arrives home, emptying her shoulderbag onto the dining table.

Un hom sceus, i deye admettr, le passar lon alcun burden ne sy sac entr y clas aubon e l'oc hour.
There had, admittedly, been ample occasion for someone to slip it into her bag between the morning class and now.

Indoutabr a i le laiscað insurveilað unveg ben may a mesur de sy nombrous hour a majon livr dy facultað, un certan volum carnt parmy y ci roum labyrinthin stant untal emblem dell'Universtað a Sant Marin.
Doubtless she had left it unattended at least once in her several hours in the faculty bookhouse, searching for a particular volume among the labyrinthine spaces so emblematic of the University of Saint Marinus.

Ben parcanç meðes yon l'interromoy retorn n'y rout veil citaðer pedallant con un quignou acatant dy refectoir.
Or perhaps even as she stopped to buy a pastry from the refettorio, before pedalling on down the old town streets.

Nentamen, i vacillau pre y ragot rescað fair defleiçar.
Nonetheless, she hesitated before unfolding the torn scrap.

L'idea fol au stað a Marco, primer—hour demay passað ne zortlasce sgardant.
The foolish idea had been Marco's, at first—too many hours watching the blacklairs [horror films].

Y mejan por ascurar, l'au dit, ig ða vray y mont er gournað par dæmon obscur capabr de se delir des y memoir a ci lou veint, luy i pos toð cas en descovrir.
Assurance, he had said, that if indeed the world was controlled by shadowy demons who could wipe themselves from the minds of those who saw them, he could at least find out about it.

Cos retenuð au Leonta y nom Erculaneo triað, sou motif ig l'er alchemy studiant a noc histoir, e donc l'ig bourg vole noc plausibr se cavir svoccað par hasart.
Leonta had privately chosen the name Erculaneo, on the grounds that she was studying alchemy, not history, so the town was unlikely to come up by accident.

-

[1] that is, a book or other piece of media focusing on Italian places and people (or her diaspora) as part of the General Italian Fervour (whence GIF tale) of the 1960s.

25m Juglot

zetr /ˈzɛ.tʀ̩/ [ˈzɛt(ɐ)] note, memo

also zetrar /zeˈtʀaʀ/ make a note, write down a memo

< 19C, from Norman French zetre [zɛt] (with spelling pronunciation in Boral), originally from Bavarian zötter “slip of paper” via Italian zettere and Provencial zeter meaning “note, memo”. The Bavarian is an alteration of former 16C zettel (Saxon zedel) from Italian cedola < Latin schedula “leaf of paper”. Verbal derivation from 1909 in writing but attested in recording for a decade or more earlier.

    L'edition dy zetr scandalous Darchild akirtau Cant n'insorgenç.

    Publishing the scandalous Darchild memo sparked unrest in Kent.

    /ˌle.diˈtsjɔn di ˈzɛ.tʀ̩ ˌxan.daˈluz daʀˈtsjɪld* ˌa.kɪʀˈto kant ˌnɪn.sɔʀˈgɛnts/

*someone with a better accent might have [dɑːˈtʃʉːd].

< 18C, derived from adjective/noun facsimile “exact copy, duplicate”, a 16C borrowing from Scholastic Latin phrase fac simile “make similar”. See also late 19C Borlish tachygraph [typewriter] brand Faxim, a clipping of these.

History V: Europe from the Imperia to the Fracture (answers on sheet 233)

1. Catrin of Kernow was an influential figure in late twelfth-century Borland. But which king, who orchestrated the Realignment of Devon in 1225, was her younger brother?

2. Which of the following Cordoban cities was first to be captured by the Kingdom of Barcelon?
(ɑ) Toledo (β) Cunca (ɣ) Palma de Mayurca

3. The First Burning of London was in 1290, but approximately how many people were killed in the week-long disaster?
(ɑ) 60 (β) 400 (ɣ) 1200

4. Under whose mayorship was the city of Wrozlav admitted into the Stadbund in 1341, despite the protestations of Bardwick and Bremen?

5. In what year was the New Navarre Enterprise established by King Ambrose III of Vascony?

6. Savino the Just, first Marksman of Jerusalem (having declined any higher title) was, prior to his accession in 1119, part of the ruling family of which state?

7. The English folk tales of the Farmer Duke and his Masked Band are usually set during the reign of which Drengotian emperor?

8. What did Nicolà s Avignon do in 1347 that is said to have led to the final collapse of the Second Roman Empire?

9. A dispute over the location of the Redstone Chair between which two Albic states sparked the decades-long Wars of Fealty?

10. Who was the first woman to teach at a European university, in 1219 as part of the Sant'Antonnio University of Padow?

28m Juglot

pommat /poˈmat/ [pʊˈmat] ointment, unction, salve

< 16C from Roman pomada “ointment”, probably (?) via French pommade, from a collective of pomo “apple”, so named since many early ointments used a base of mashed apples. Formerly and dialectically used for cosmetic products but nowadays mostly restricted to medicinal pastes or gels. See for example pommat hebetant “topical painkiller”.

    Val nos landreu y ferið e soulor mettreu un pommat contr zoia dessur.

    /ˈval no lanˈdʀaw i feˈʀɪθ e suˈlɔʀ meˈtʀaw ɪn poˈmat ˈkɔn.tʀ zoˈja deˈsɪʀ/

    We should wash the wound and then apply an anti-bacterial salve to it.

29m Juglot

quignou /kwajˈnu/ [kwɐjˈnu] meat pie, savoury pastry dish, esp. hand-held

< from 15C late Middle Boral quignou “pastry snack” [kwiːˈnu], from Kernow quignow “pastry” [kwiˈɲu], of uncertain origin. Probably French or Aquitain originally, although no cognates are known in any Romance or British language (Vaskish cœnú “small enclosed pie” is probably related but the direction of borrowing is unknown).

    Y megl quignou d'armað cavibr nell'oc bourg vien de Cas Pamfey.

    /i ˈmil kwajˈnu daʀˈmaθ kaˈvi.bʀ̩ ne.lɔk ˈbuʀg ˈvjɛn de ˈkaz paɱˈfi/

    The best worker's pie you can get in this town comes from Pamfey's.

30m Juglot

    from the first chapter of 1890 masquira tale Men of Jet and Diamond (Dynnon o Vuhuð a Diamont), written originally in Cambrick by Hasinic Welshman Cadogan Torrior and published by Blackburn Editions. A rather late example of the first wave of the genre, it was modestly popular in eastern Mendeva, but it became more widely known after the 1951 release of Jet White (Blanc Sabaje) in New Provence, a film adaptation of Torrior's book.

Y vertað suraðoint fos ig, digr y vesteul ag davarn sta stivað.
It would have been an exaggeration to say that the hotel's lobby was crowded.

Nentamen, Zoe Pentros descovroy i poðe noc s'asseïr for ne s'addant a plaç mi dy convoc actif d'un hom la ben dy soltum affloisað d'un autr.
Nonetheless, Zoë Pentross found she couldn't take a seat without inserting herself into someone's lively conversation or another's relaxed solitude.

A loy i tene pos, appenent sur y pladisc, cla portant ne man scert e vin Empordan (bon cos ajoutant) ne man distr.
Instead she remained standing at the bar, keys in her left hand and a gratifyingly-good Empordan wine in her right.

“Soð vos ac por l'avent dy Rey novel a Nadacou veir ?” Un segr haut e slanc colluïsc Zoe sur y pladisc, remplisc peðent all'hontarn con saluð.
“Are you here to see the new King arrive in Nadacow?” A tall, slender man joined Zoe at the bar, signalling the tender for a refill.

< first attested 14C as esvocar “call forth, summon”, either directly taken/calqued from Latin ēvocō “I lure, entice, summon” or a synchronic derivation from vocar “call, ask for, backon”, itself from Latin vocō with the same sense.

1r Aost

suraðoir /ˌsiʀaˈðɔjʀ/ [ˌsi.ʀɐˈðɔ.jɐ] to exaggerate, to make too big, to outsize, to overstate

in particular, y vertað suraðoir to exaggerate the truth

< 18C, from sur- “over, above” < Latin super-, affixed to aðoir “to grow, to increase”, from Latin adaugere “I make greater, I augment”.

    Nos poum noc y gra suraðoir dell'oc combat.

    We cannot overstate the importance of this battle.

##

2m Aost

    taken from the 2008 fourth edition of condensed study materials for New Vithor course Histoir Concorrent, s16 : Mort, Vascel eð y Motion Celest (in English, Concurrence History of the 16th Century: Death, Ships and the Movement of the Heavens).

…it is usual to consider the feudal system entirely defunct by 1550 N, at least outside of insular nations like Burgundy or Awry. There were a variety of reasons for this collapse, some more widely accepted than others.

Often cited is a labour shortage of serfs due to the Second Great Dying, allowing those peasants who remained to demand more favourable conditions from their lords. This is evidenced by the correlation [Iscombe 1967] observed between population loss and liberalisation (as well as how quickly the nascent middle class grew). For example, Borland under Joseph III lost over four hundred thousand people, one-seventh of its population; serfdom (already on its last legs) was legally abolished in 1564 N, but by that point it had not de facto existed on the island for a generation.

One counterexample to the above trend is Vascony, which, thanks to Novomundine profits in furs and other goods, actually saw in increase in population from 1500 to 1550 (excluding Portingale) due to immigration. The end of the Vascon Ascendancy with the War of the Two Queens would end feudalism in Vascony by the end of the century, however.

(It is worth mentioning that this was not the main factor in ending feudalism in the east, where the Islamic conquests of Kiew and Russian Cazany upended the nobility more directly.)

One, perhaps unexpected, effect of the decline of the feudal system was the strengthening of laws such as sumptuary codes. These were intended [Rone, 1988] to reinforce the social distinctions between serf, freedman and noble—although states had had similar laws almost since Classical times, enforcement of these rules became more stringent and draconian. There is even one account of an Emma Chasteron being executed in Dovres for wearing “silk in saphires purpel and petal embroderie” to church…

3m Aost

davarn /daˈvaʀn/ [dɐˈvɑːn] resort, grand hotel

(in opposition to an inn or hostel)

< 19C from Cambrick y davarn “the hotel”, in particular a reference to world-famous Hasinic resort town Y Davarn Heul “The Sun Hotel”. This word is an Old Welsh borrowing from Latin taberna “shop, inn”, making it a doublet of Borlish tarn “pub, café”.

    Nos n'eu redon aðief por semman passar n'untal davarn.

    /no ˈnaw ʀeˈdɔn aˈðjɛf pɔʀ seˈman paˈsaʀ nɪnˈtal daˈvaʀn/

    We don't have enough money on hand to stay in a hotel like that.

4m Aost

pladisc /plaˈdix/ [plɐˈdɪç] bar, counter, esp. one serving alcohol

< 12C in Old Boral as pladdisc or earlier plat disc /ˌplad:iʃ/, “counter”, literally “flat board”. The adjective is from Vulgar Latin plattus “flat”, from Greek πλατύς; the noun is from Norrish diskr “plate”, which is probably cognate with Latin discus “discus, dish”.

    I lançau un pavar sur pladisc lon y tarner sgardant.

    He threw a coin onto the counter without looking at the innkeeper.

    /i lanˈtso ɪn paˈvaʀ sɪʀ plaˈdɪx lɔn y taʀˈnɛʀ sgaʀˈdant/

5m Aost

    taken from Post-Drengotian Albion: the Long Thirteenth Century, a reference textbook in concurrence history written by Professor Nuraddin Venzer and published 1973 by the New Lincoln School.

A mesur dell'eç annað, y centuir a David IV eð a Augost combattoirn por autortað sur y bourg nombrous all'estuer Severn.
In these years, the armies of David IV and King August battled for control of the various towns on the Severn estuary.

Bristou meðes fo emposseïð deur gournanç Wessaxon un dezein ben may pres 1270 N.
Even Bristow was wrested from Wessern authority for at least a decade around 1270 N.

Cossy er y concarnç a Dorogn par Ivan de Cartau (accommis nell'enç lon soutien de Rey David) yent pre y fin dy siecr trecem.
Thus was Ivan of Cartaw's conquest of Devon (undertaken at first without the support of King David) achieved by the end of the century.

Y moment apen ig David aðeleu y Taleð Dorogner, malgrað, ignitaurn y tijon usual entr lou e Marclont.
Almost as soon as David declared the Tallath of Devon, however, did the usual tensions with Markland ignite.

Y novel rey Andre II ant ment martial, stant nevoð ag rey derran, n'au alcun dy poliðeç de sell'yam.
The martial-minded new king Andrew II, nephew of the previous king, shared none of his uncle's diplomacy.

I relançau y Rix Mallor con gazou sur Carvantel ne prinvir 1301 N, l'abbaðy Sant Brigid original incendiað enter durant.
He restarted the Mallor Wars with the Sack of Car Vantel (Rexam) in 1301 N, during which the original St. Brigid's Abbey burnt to the ground.

6m Aost

ajoutar /ˌa.ʒuˈtaʀ/ [ˌa.ʝʊˈtɑː] to gratify, to please, to help out

that is, to make (someone) thankful or pleased

< first attested 13C but presumably extant in early Old Boral, from Vulgar Latin adiuvitō, derived from iuvō “I help, I save”. Probably at some point a semi-learned form due to the lack of lenition.

    Rey Aðelouf soloy y var contr y cantesc por sy sou-nobr ajoutar.

    King Athelwolf declared war on the Kentish to gratify the noble families.

    /ˈʀi ˌa.ðeˈluf soˈlɔj aˈbat ˈkɔn.tʀ‿i kanˈtɛx pɔʀ si suˈnɔ.bʀ‿a.ʒuˈtaʀ/

7m Aost

Dorogn /doˈʀɔjn/ [dʊˈʀɔjn] Devon, Cornwall

that is, the southwesternmost part of Albion [Great Britain]

also dorogner from Devon, from Cornwall

< 12C in Old Boral as Doroȝne “Devon”, from earlier Donnoȝna, itself from Latin Dumnonia “land of the Dumnonii”, referring to the British group resident there in Roman times. The Latin comes from the Classical British root duβn “deep”, related to Cambrick dωvn “deep”. See also French Dourogne, Leon Domnòix with the same sense.

    Y preu havan y nogt passað la ne tar brittesc es Iscom, a Dorogn.

    The first British harbour we'll spend the night at is Iscom in Devon.

    /i ˈpʀaw haˈvan i ˈnɔjt paˈsaθ la ne ˈtaʀ bʀiˈtɛx ɛz iˈxɔm a doˈʀɔjn/

##

8m Aost

    taken from textbook Albic Medicine in the Workshop Decades, Part V: Alchemic Detaxion, written by Dr Harold Westcamp and published 1994 by the Conster Health Edifice, funded by the Markland Ministry for Health.

…as in the writing of physician Dr Mickel Cráyenschot, noted for being an early supporter of Deviance Theology and public tovarick in France even as it embraced the Modest Arrangement.

But the application of copper's counter-zoic [anti-microbial] manner with the furthest-reaching consequences can only be the stauron retainer [intra-uterine device]. Nonetheless it is important to separate our modern point of view from the contemporary. What seem to us like obvious components of the retainer often took dozens of years to innovate. For example, the T-cross of the modern retainer doesn't arise until the St. Agrippina Medical House 1877 patent—earlier designs included rings and helices!

The theoretical barrier to developing this kind of prevention was recognising that copper metal could kill sperm cases as well as zoia. Although copper's counter-zoic manner had been documented since 1789 (see the work of alchemist Suxúmawaca bint as-Sáffat), extending this observation to sperm would have to wait until 1824, in the aftermath of the Second German War. Former mozardisto and farm theorist Ricardo Ivarrachezurí wrote in that year that “the seed of the bull, the ram and the stallion alike are made impotent in storing them for some hours in a syringe of copper; in this we can suppose we see the same mechanism that dispels the cholera from water in a copper cauldron”.

In fact, Ivarrachezurí published these findings as a warning to other farmers and horse breeders not to use copper in their fecundation projects…

##

9m Aost

sar /saʀ/ [sɑː] net, fishnet, mesh, also in general a network, an interconnected system

in particular y sar (epistol), literally “the letter mesh”, referring to the global network of communication devices and other computers, known in English as “the mesh”.

< 12C, from Vask sare “net”, one of the many naval words taken from Vascon fishermen and later from their sea-traders: see hagr “mast” and bakilau “cod”, also from Vask. Extension to general networks first attested 1836; use for the mesh calqued from French mail épistre, itself a reference to 1968 parachthon tale Starsail, published in Rone by Ashford Pasquier.

    Posc pieç minut er y sar remorcað des y mar torvant.

    After a few minutes the net was pulled out of the troubled sea.

    /pɔx ˈpjɛts miˈnɪt ɛʀ y ˈsaʀ ˌʀe.mɔʀˈkaθ dɛz i ˈmaʀ toʀˈvant/

##

10m Aost

var souvr /var ˈsu.vʀ̩/ declare war, wage war

literally set war loose, release war

< expression from 16C in poet Drominir's work Verdur Roug, which drew on his experience fighting in the War for the Stavang Crown. The verb is a direct descendant of Latin solvō “I loosen, explain, break up, cancel, unfurl” (although it does not retain the full range of meanings!).

    All'yent de May Connagt sta prest a var souvr.

    By the beginning of May Connaught was ready to declare war.

    /aˈljɛnt de ˈme koˈnɛjt sta ˈpʀɛst a aˈbat ˈsu.vʀ̩/

11m Aost

    excerpt from and Borlish translation of New Skies, Old Stories (in Provincial French Novè Cial, Anzan Conte) by Simon d'Estiala, published 1980 by Prindor Marsillès.

Parcanç es i temerer, y decadenç a trevogt blasmar unic ag morostað n'Europ posc y var.
It is perhaps foolhardy to attribute the decline of Voidtale trevolds solely to the gloom of post-war Europe.

Nos posseu tan digr, parmig, l'idea de novel œcumen splorar e de novel cultur encontrar n'au plu attragnt ; l'ovartur caddicivenean laiscau pog “tar enigmatic” por descovrir,
We might say, for example, that the idea of exploring new lands and meeting new civilisations had lost its shine, with the opening of Caddicivene leaving few “mysterious lands” to discover,

eð y sar campaner defassant parmis a gent ne toð loy de communeyar cos veloç fort may (particuler, apprendr ig politia lontan stan magn ordineressem a sy figuration!)
and the developing steeple-mesh allowing people everywhere to communicate far more rapidly (in particular, to discover that distant nations were often much less exotic than one imagined!).

Toð cas final, romanç parachthon partenent, y dezein milloy dy siecr novoç sta untal nazir.
In any case, when it comes to parachthon romance, the middle of the nineteenth century was somewhat of a nadir.

Posc meðes au Europ parsan fait e fo l'abondanç general dy ci annað Atelier soluð grant, y form er destarrið pall'ort de stoir masquira.
Even once Europe had recovered and the general prosperity of the Workshop decades had truly got going, the genre was displaced by the rise of masquira fiction.

12m Aost

dromig /dʀoˈmaj/ blackberry, bramble, (sometimes in general) drupe

< 9C in Old Boral rommiȝ “bramble”, from early Borland Romance romige, from Vulgar Latin rumīce < Latin rumex, rumicis “sorrel”. Later influenced by druf “stone fruit”, from Latin drūpa.

    Y ci Nou Provençal son connuð par lour pantort a dromig.

    The New Provincials are known for their blackberry pies.

    /i tsi ˈnu ˌpʀo.vɛnˈtsal son koˈnɪθ paʀ ˈluʀ panˈtɔʀt dʀoˈmaj/

13m Aost

retenour stauron /ˌre.teˈnuʀ stoˈʀɔn/ IUD, intrauterine device, (in-setting) stauron retainer

informally, un stauron or (rarely) un retenour

< first used in Borlish in 1890s N, based on Vascon retenor stauròn or possibly Romish ritinore stauron; analogous terms had been in the scientific literature since the 1876 Sant'Agrippa publication. The noun is an agentive from retenir “to withhold, restrain, keep, preserve”, literally “hold again”, itself modelled after Latin retineō “I hold back, repress, protect”. The adjective, meaning “cross-shaped”, is from a mock-neuter form of Ancient Greek σταυρός (staurós) “stake,  crucifix”.

    Vossy preu stauron es cavibr n'un dy sanatoir a Ausbagn.

    You can get your first IUD from one of the Ausbagn clinics.

    /vɔstʀ‿i ˈpʀaw stoˈʀɔn ɛz caˈvi.bʀ̩ nɪn di ˌsa.naˈtɔjʀ a ozˈbɛjn/

##

14m Aost

y plurem /i ˈpli.ʀɛm/ [ɪ ˈpli.ʀɪm] for the most part, particularly, especially

< 14C in Middle Boral i plurm “mostly” (various spellings), literally “the greatest amount”. The determiner from Old Boral il “the”, from Latin ille “that”, probably via a vulgar form īle. The noun is a direct descendant of Latin adjective plūrimus “the most, very many”, suppletive superlative form of multus “many” (modern Boral mout “often”).

    J'am noc y plurem raðar a Ausçagm ; la son tombr demay.

    I don't for the most part like cycling in Ausçagm; it's too hilly.

    /ˈʒam nɔk i ˈpliʀɛm ʀaˈðaʀ a ozˈbɛjn | la sɔn ˈtɔm.bʀ̩ deˈme/

##

15m Aost

    from the collected study notes for the course 1471–1517: War, Peace and Exploration after the Novomundine Landfall, 37th avenue in Concurrence History at the Tremonnow Open School.

Of course, Vascony was not the only nearby state attempting to reach Asia by sea. Ambrose's nemesis Munir al-Hamdawi of Morrack had in 1463 established a rival marine enterprise in the New World Company (شركة العالم الجديد), and just five years later the ship Orion (الجَبَار) voyaged from Anfa [Casablanca] to Aden. They made port in Vascon Sant Adrian [Bioko] and encountered Chinese merchants in Mombasa, whose reports to the Southern Emperor would change the course of history forever (see 1524-1600: The Collision and the Fracture).

After the Landfall, the Company's efforts were swiftly redirected to the Novomund [the New World] (as were the naval efforts, however paltry, of most of Europe at this time). However, a notable exception was the relatively-new Kingdom of Britain, where Howel's ships had already set up resupply ports along the African coast. Having profited from the ongoing Wars of Fealty which raged across the rest of Albion [Great Britain], the king was convinced more opportunity lay in connecting the European market directly to Asia. Doubtless he also wanted to displace the Borlish supremacy in the northern trade (although this ambition would not be achieved in his lifetime).

In any case, the tale of the twin voyages of the Cynthia and the Phœbos (of Morrack and Britain respectively) remains to this day one of the greatest stories of exploration and discovery the world has ever known, measuring up to Homer's Odyssey and Quoming's Peoples of the Sea. Directed by Umanick and Chinese traders, mac Kellot's Phœbos sailed to Gurjat, Madray and eventually made port at Borune in 1516. This was at the time almost the south-eastern extremity of the known Vetomund.

Meanwhile, the Cynthia under al-Casmi (himself a Persian immigrant to Morrack) had no less interesting a journey…

##

16m Aost

raðar /ʀaˈðaʀ/ [ʀɐˈðɑː] to cycle, to ride a bike

< early 20C, shortened along with rað “bicycle” from 19C heverrað “bicycle, velocipede”. This borrowed from Saxon heverrath (retention of the fricative suggests a coastal source, perhaps) “bicycle”, from hevel “lever, pedal” and rath “wheel”.

    Un accaçour raðant tras l'icon, y film situant certan n'asc Bel-Jou.

    A cycling postman crosses the screen, fixing the film in the Grant-Jeu period.

    /ɪn ˌa.kaˈtsuʀ ʀaˈðant tʀaz liˈkɔn | i ˈfɪlm siˈtjant tsɛʀˈtan nax bɛlˈʒu/

##

17m Aost

    excerpted from the book Des Delt vars Dois : Y Stoir d'Educaçon (From Tablets to Slates: The Story of Education), in the style of an academic textbook but intended for a broad audience, written by Theign Roufler and released 2016 by Cordin Editions.

Durant y siecr catorç ençau nos a normalisation real parcevir dy model mitað-asc d'universtað tras Europ, de Galway a Gratz.
It is during the fourteenth century that we see true standardisation of the mediaeval university system across Europe, from Galway to Gratz.

Y Reyaum a Boral dell'oc hour triant parmig, Lyce Noccair addoceu sy studiant nell'eç Cinq Art Romannesc.
For example, in the Kingdom of Borland at this time, Noccair Lyceum taught its students the Five Romantic Arts.

L'eç stan de natur l'art prosequent dy set domain classic a studiar (e fort ern il tan connuð contemporane par nom d'art classic ben liberal).
These were in essence the descendants of the seven Classical areas of study (and indeed they were also known at the time as the Classical or liberal arts).

Y Cinq Art ern Literatur, Argument, Accont, Geometry e Music.
The Five Arts were Literature, Argument, Reckoning, Geometry and Songcraft.

Ag commet dy siecr cinqueç diminoy l'oc model paulatim; facultað nombrous addau souject novel, ig inclus histoir, lengaç eð alchemy.
The start of the fifteenth century saw the gradual decline of this system, with various institutions introducing new subjects, such as history, languages and alchemy.

18m Aost

delt /dɛlt/ tablet, esp. of clay; more generally document, record

< attested from 17C, originally from Greek δέλτος (déltos) “writing-tablet, piece of writing” (cf. Imperial delton “book”), possibly of Semitic origin. Probably via Napolitan delto “tablet”, leading some to argue that it is a post-Classical borrowing.

    Y parol sur y delt stan febr demay; jo poðe noc lou leir.

    The writing on the tablet was too faded for me to read.

    /i paˈʀɔl sɪʀ i ˈdɛlt stanˈfɛ.bʀ̩ deˈme | ʒo poˈðe nɔk lu ˈliʀ/

##

19m Aost

prosequent /ˌpʀo.zeˈkwɛnt/ descendant, progeny, something proceeding from a source, accompaniment

< 15C in various European languages, from Scholastic Latin prōsequēns “following, escorting, accompanying”, from prō- “forward, in front” + sequor “I follow, I pursue”. Use extended to harmony in music by 18C, and soon after to supporters of a cause or person.

    Sell'abiltað ne clavir es grant aðief por prosequent fair, e bogr tan may.

    His keyboard-playing ability is good enough to accompany, and no better.

    /seˌla.bɪlˈtaθ ne klaˈvɪʀ ɛz ˈgʀant aˈðjɛf pɔʀ ˌpʀo.zeˈkwɛnt fɛʀ | e ˈlɔn tan ˈme/

##

20m Aost

       from the collected study notes for the course 1471–1517: War, Peace and Exploration after the Novomundine Landfall, 37th avenue in Concurrence History at the Tremonnow Open School.

…the south-eastern extremity of the known Vetomund.

Meanwhile, the Cynthia under al-Casmi (himself a Persian immigrant to Morrack) had no less interesting a journey. First making port at the various Arcabil and Meridian outposts, already a significant source of wealth, the ship continued along the Cappatian coast*. Here in al-Casmi's logbooks we read of the Dowahin straits and island (from the Arabic for “smoke”, after the many smoke-towers the captain writes of seeing the inhabitants of the island build, and which dissuaded him from making landfall thereon).

(*Remember that the continent would not be so named for several more decades yet.)

But the bulk of the Cynthia's journey would come next, as the ship entered the southern Horizic Ocean. At first they sailed north along the jagged coastline, possibly as far as Maboutian lands, although al-Casmis records are unclear. In any case they eventually sailed west into the blue. It is not entirely clear which Horizic Isles the Cynthia encountered on this part of the voyage. What is known is that on midsummer of 1515 they alighted on Jazirat Asnam (the encounter being infamous for its acrimony), and that on 11 Shaban 921 the crew began to perform the salat forward, not aft, as the astronomer aboard—one Taisir as-Sadik—predicted they had passed the longitude of the antipode of Mecca.*

(*Further recall that truly accurate longitude measurement would not be achieved until the invention of the timehold in the mid-seventeenth century.)

The next spring the Cynthia finally arrived in a place almost recognisable as Asia, though the inhabitants were as dark-skinned as anyone in the Arab world. This archipelago was hence named Asia Cahal (or in modern parlance Cahalla), and in the records of al-Casmi's time on Bijai (the largest island thereof) there is an account of an old folktale; one which will explain why the Cynthia, on leaving Bijai, went not west, but south…

##

21r Aost

triant parmig /tʀiˈant paʀˈmaj/ for example, as an example

< 17C, literally “choosing among them”, existing alongside and eventually displacing several similar constructions. The verb triar “choose, pick” is from Old Boral tri(ð)ar “sort, sift”, with pronunciation at least influenced by Old French trier, with the same meaning. This from late Latin trītō “I chop, rub”, with meaning shift through use in granum terere “beat the corn from the chaff”. The adverb parmig derives from parmy “among, alongside”, literally “by the middle”, from Latin per medium.

    Lour y pas faus triant parmig, cossireð y Semman a Tonneðr.

    As an example of what they did wrong, consider the Week of Thunder.

    /luʀ i ˈpaz foz tʀiˈant paʀˈmaj | ˌko.siˈʀɛθ i seˈman a toˈnɛ.ðʀ̩/

##

22m Aost

dois /dɔjz/ touchpad, touchscreen, smartphone; in-universe English tach or slate

< late 20C, clipping of ardois “slate, slab”, also “screen, display”, referring to touchscreens originally in the brandname TachArdois (evoking Greek τᾰχῠ́ς • takhús “swift, quick”) and the generic term ardois tactil “tactile slate”. The noun ardois is from Old Boral ardoise, ardosc, ardesc “slate”, borrowed at various points from Old French ardoise < Late Latin ardesia, probably of Gaulish origin.

    J'oyeu l'ig connart fatuous rompoy trey dois n'un monð.

    I heard that clumsy idiot broke three tachs in one month.

    /ʒoˈjaw laj koˈnaʀt faˈtjuz ʀɔmˈpɔj tʀi ˈdɔjz nɪn ˈmɔnθ/

23m Aost

    story excerpt from Callot Rhig's 1834 collection of short stories Aur Nercow (Fool's Gold), exploring the disconnect between what we say we want, and what will truly make us happy.

Diabr sta i feliç a se trovar ac, ða meðes oy passau i jorn sou perigl d'applatiscenç par col livr.
He was damned lucky to be here, even if he now lived in danger of being crushed by a book landslide.

Arthur fo vas ag var ogtoç eld, e fo reuluð posc nouf annað descovrent i sta l'ivan extrane d'un famigl lon gravitað mendrem.
Arthur had gone to war at eighteen, and come back nine years later to find himself the extraneous child of a family of little import.

Sur mar ben ne Madray er i vis com cim forgað, un ven dont slança experiç vocationner cordamn fruyant e tout souvant.
At sea or in Madray he'd been a forged expert, a wellspring of professional expertise who knew the ropes and had seen it all.

Cas luy a Carvantel, i fo denuð ancour joun segr, hom a pog addoçment eð a alcun histoir empley.
Back in Carvantel he'd become a young man again, one with little training and no experience.

Pre y var un atelier carpenter au le benoçað un apprendent, ma l'ig parisce remotessem a jouneç.
He'd been apprenticed to a carpenter's shop before the war, but that felt like a lifetime ago.

For sarve i ac a hom occir, stant dissuas.
All he was good at now was killing people, which was discouraged.

##

24m Aost

romannesc /ˌʀo.maˈnɛx/ [ˌʀo.mɐˈnɛç] civilised, popular, folk, romantic, quaint

< 10C in rumanesc etc. “of the Roman peoples”, which is to say of Borland, Italy, Iberia and France, from roman “Roman” with adjectival suffix -esc < Germanic -iskas. Soon (by late 11C) used to denote popular or folk stories, music and so on, as opposed to Classical or ecclesiastical works, or any works in Latin. Already by 17C it means “quaint, old-fashioned, charming”, but more positive senses returned in the late 18C with the Romantic Revival of the Long Peace.

    L'eç cont mi-mythos a rey e volour son nommað stoir romannesc.

    These semi-mythical stories of kings and thieves are called romantic tales.

    /lɛts ˈkɔnt mi.miˈθɔz a ˈʀi e voˈluʀ sɔn noˈmaθ stɔjʀ ˌʀo.maˈnɛx/

##

25m Aost

diminoir /ˌdi.miˈnɔjʀ/ [ˌdi.mɪˈnɔ.jɐ] to weaken, shrink, decline, diminish

< 12C in Old Boral diminoer “lessen, weaken”, a semi-learned borrowing of Latin dīminuō “I shatter, weaken”. Along with attriboir “assign”, its verbal conjugation changed by analogy with verbs descending from Latin -struō. However the past participle remains diminuð, rather than the expected *diminoit.

    Y luissoir d'aur diminois for posc y pouvranç a Daclont fo treblað.

    The lust for gold only weakened once Dackland's population had tripled.

    /i lajˈsɔjʀ ˈdoʀ ˌdi.miˈnɔjz fɔʀ pɔx i puˈvʀants a dakˈlɔnt fo tʀeˈblaθ/

26m Aost

    questions taken from the January 2003 N scitation documents for the course 1471 - 1517: War, Peace and Exploration after the Novomundine Landfall, 37th avenue in Concurrence History at the Tremonnow Open School (formerly the Brethin Mesh Institute). [continued from 13m Avril]

Question 3 (sixty minutes)

(i) Answer the following short questions on the Vascon Ascendancy in under 100 words each.

    (α) Who is the most probable out of the many people who are claimed to have been lovers of King Ambrose III?
    (β) What caused the infamous sinking of the Estellas daus Sanz, which would have been the second lone ship to make the Atlantic crossing?
    (γ) Why did the War for the Barcelon Succession last for only 4 days?
    (δ) Where did Vascony trade for the oranges and other citrus fruits required by the Sandrine Design?
    (ε) When was the strict iamb 5' first used in Vascon poetry?

(ii) To what extent did the fact that the northern parts of Mendeva were less immediately profitable than the East lead to the end of the Vascon Ascendancy?

Question 4 (forty minutes)

EITHER

(i) Summarise the arguments for and against the veracity of al-Casmi's account of the tale of the Great Southern Island, including recent hereditarian findings and their implications for patterns of migration, and contemporary Tecamaw accounts of the decline of star navigation.

OR

(ii) Discuss the societal effects of the reintroduction of long-distance boating and sail on the Tecamaw nation and the Bijai, with evidence taken from the various written records of the Borunesk Great Meeting of 1517.

##

27m Aost

coul tar /kul taʀ/ [kuː tɑː] landslide, rockslide, avalanche (both literally and metaphorically—a deluge of tasks or other unwanted things)

> 15C, literally “flow of earth”, from coul “flow” (now obsoleted by newer colanç), a deverbal of colar “to flow” < Latin cōlō “I strain, filter” plus tar “earth”, directly from Latin terra “ground, soil, dirt”. The word coul is nowadays used only in this expression and, lately, one-time formations such as coul livr “book-slide”, etc.

    Posc je sy nascenç la's un coul tar desig plou lort.

    After his birthday there was a landslide due to heavy rain.

    /pɔx ʒe si naˈxɛnts laz ɪn ˈkul ˈtaʀ deˈzaj ˈplu ˈlɔrt/

##

28m Aost

cim forgað /ˈtsɪm fɔʀˈgaθ/ [ˌtsɪɱ.fʊːˈgah] seasoned expert, person well-versed in their field

> 18C, literally “forged top”, where here cim “top, point, cap” uses an archaic sense of “expert, authority”, the word coming from Latin cȳma “sprout (as of cabbage), hollow sphere”, from Greek. The adjective is the past participle of forgar “to make, to forge”, from Latin fabricō “I build” (presumably via dialect forms in Old Boral, or perhaps early Norman French? See attested foircare).

    L'eç defy farraun de mey un cim forgað.

    These challenges will make me a seasoned expert.

    /lɛts deˈfi faˈʀon de ˈmi ɪn ˈtsɪm fɔʀˈgaθ/

29m Aost

    Extract taken from the Borlish version (translated by Benjamin Rocquot in 1923) of Dutch biography Lát os Danse: De Hartoin 'n Avosche (Let us Dance: The Duchess d'Avosche), chronicling and dramatising the life of sixteenth-century noblewoman Adaillé d'Avosche (1524-1608), a duchy in southern Willemy. 

    She was infamous during her later life for her great influence in the court of David of France, with rumours that she was in fact his mother persisting for the rest of his reign. The 1589 scandal involving her eldest grandson Déricque temporarily cost her some capital, and although she soon regained her position, King David took the opportunity to continue the centralisation policies typical of contemporary Europe.

De Roger e Carolin d'Avosc nascoirn ogt ivan, dont Adagle sta tressem.
Roger and Caroline d'Avosche had eight children, and Adaillé was the third.

Sy dou fraðr antir moroy com sugtour (un certan desig Scon Grant Morrenç), tan com sy fraðr postir Peðr, e sy jounessem sour Anna.
Her two older brothers died in infancy (at least one in the Second Great Dying), as well as her younger brother Peter, and her youngest sister Anne.

Cossy sta i durant y grant plurem ag jouneç l'heriter appos dy ducað.
Thus she was for the great majority of her childhood the heir presumptive of the duchy.

L'eç prefaction, toglað afont coll'eç condoçment de preu sy noðriç Elisa Arnoutszen (cosvour dy sanat parnommað)
These expectations, combined with the instructions of at first her nurse Elisa Arnoutszen (cousin of the renowned physician)

e soulor sy ci aðelent, animaurn sy natur entrovant eð ingenious.
and later her tutors, encouraged her inquisitive and intelligent nature.

30m Aost

condoçment /ˌkɔn.dɔtsˈmɛnt/ [ˌkɔn.dʊsˈmɛnt] training, education, instruction, apprenticeship

> 14C Middle Boral nominal derivation from condocir “to train, to instruct, to take as an apprentice”, possibly a direct descendant of Latin condoceō of the same meaning, but more likely a borrowing (any earlier attestations are uncertain).

    Jo porray noc le stikettar prest a condoçment porsevir.

    I cannot say that she is ready to continue her training.
