##

31r Aost

dissuadir /ˌdi.sjaˈdɪʀ/ [ˌdi.sjɐˈdɪː] to dissuade, to discourage, to oppose

< 16C, borrowed from Latin dissuādeō “I urge differently, advise against”, from prefix dis- “apart, reversal” with the verb suādeō “I recommend, advise, persuade”. Inherits past tense and participle forms in -s (dissuas etc) from Latin and retains them by analogy with similar verbs, mostly in the third declension.

    Nos deyeu vos dissuadir de l'eç monc a Golfhaun fascar.

    We must advise you not to anger the Gulfhaven monks.

    /no diˈjaw vo ˌdi.sjaˈdɪʀ de lɛts ˈmɔnk a gɔlfˈhon faˈxaʀ/ 

##

1r Septembr

    short extracts taken from Y Stoir d'Istr Boral (The Story of Borland), second edition, a school history textbook published 1993 by Cordin Editions, one of the foremost guilds producing educational books for national schools.

…we will perhaps never know if the conquering Romans called our land īnsula boreālis “Island in the North” before they arrived in force at the daybreak of the first millenium, or if the name is a post hoc creation after the native Kelt name, enis borag “Island of the Dawn” (so called for its easterly position with respect to Albion and Ireland)…

…there remains little outside contemporary Roman accounts of the Bora Ale, the Borland Kelts, bar geographical features such as the river Duðam (from du dammo “at the river”) and, possibly, personal names such as Ivocq and Haðr (though for most of these we also have alternate explanations)…

…in Ptolemy's description of the Pritannoi we can understand he referred to the entirety of the Insular Kelts as a whole; this grouping was later divided, first by the English Strait at some point before Caesar's first invasion of Albion, and later by the Irish Sea, although of course this neat partition is confused by the Scots and by the Welsh communities along the Rustigh…

…the latter years of the eighth century, as the Viking Age dawned. The last purely English war of the time was the Merch subjugation of East Anglia, simultaneously establishing the Great Border between the mouths of the Severn and the Thames (see the Oxonian 796 N chronicle), and inciting a renewed influx into the petty kingdoms of Angland and Southbar…

##

2m Septembr

sugtour /sajˈtuʀ/ [sɐjˈtʊː] toddler, infant, very young child

also sapling, young plant

< 13C in late Old Boral suȝtour “suckler, one who breastfeeds”, nominal derivation from suȝtar (modern sugtar) “suckle, breastfeed”, a direct descendant from late Latin sūgitō “I suckle”. This is a derivation apparently exclusive to Borland from sūgō “I suck, take in” (see also issugt “dried, boring”, from the same root).

    Y joun segr entrau ny varous, sugtour fassað ag peitrin.

    The young man entered the shop, an infant swaddled on his chest.

    /i ˈʒun ˈsi.jʀ̩ ɛnˈtʀo ni vaˈʀuz | sajˈtuʀ faˈsaθ ɛj piˈtʀɪn/

##

3m Septembr

cosvour /koˈsvuʀ/ [kʊzˈvʊː] cousin, relative, in general similar thing

< Unclear. Attested in late 8C in a bewildering variety of forms—suer, cossorn, sourin, cusoondre, among many others. Presumably somehow related to Latin cōnsobrīnus “first cousin”, whence Norman quosin, Vascon cotrì and Romish coginne, for example. However, influence from Old English swēor “father-in-law, cousin” is almost guaranteed.

    Vos derreð sona luïr de cosvour febr n'y duc acconnoscent.

    You will have to pose as distant cousins when you are introduced to the duke.

    /vo deˈʀɛθ soˈna lajˈiʀ de koˈsvuʀ ˈfɛ.bʀ̩ ni ˈdɪk ˌa.koˈnɔ.xɛnt/

##

4m Septembr

    from the 1914 short lovetale Chargez-moy Garir (Command that I be Healed), written by longtime author and Millstone veteran (serving in the Second Medic Force of Valois) Honorine Maignard. She was the daughter of Grant-Jeu Lombard diplomat Audace Saviano, who was instrumental in the establishment of the Italian Concert in 1891.

I no's dur a continuar soll'apres dy mort de sy marið.
It isn't difficult to go on in the wake of her husband's death.

Parveg sent Claire l'ig sey ne bon mont.
Sometimes Claire feels like it should be.

Ig l'au y cainç tan toccað, ig l'au parmis tan direct all'aðombracer Mr Becond le dirisc n'ouvr sug, y mort aus le fragnt.
Considering her hand in things, that she'd let herself be so readily manipulated into doing Mr Becond's dirty work, it should have taken her apart.

L'aus l'incendiað toð a tar.
It should have burned her down to the ground.

Ma a loy—a loy i le refay.
But instead—instead it remakes her.

Y celebr Claire Robiquet pren martel por aïðar con y recostroinç, Auscapel stant cicatricið ja ancour pall'abat dy Grant Centuir, acr a farm laiscað crous, blazað ;
The illustrious Claire Robiquet steps up to help with the reconstruction,  scarred all over again by the Grand Army's fight, acres of farmland left a burned-out husk;

e Claire se pon avant un grauf e resaif y signfianç d'un objectif aïr.
and Claire stands at a grave and rediscovers what it means to have purpose.

##

5m Septembr

parnommar /ˌpaʀ.noˈmaʀ/ [ˌpɑː.nʊˈmɑː] to celebrate, make famous, idolise, canonise, worship

< 12C parnomar “to canonise” but probably earlier, seen especially in religious contexts as a vernacular translation of Latin canōnizō “I recognise as a saint”. Formed from par- “through, thoroughly” < Latin per- with nommar “to name, to call, to recognise as”, a verbal derivation from nom “name”. Extended to secular use by 15C. Nowadays most common as the participle parnommað “famous, celebrated”.

    Cascif fout vossy cognit un parnommað ?

    Why the fuck are you undercover as a celebrity?

    /kaˈxɪf fut vɔsˈtʀ‿i kɔjˈnɪt ɪn ˌpaʀ.noˈmaθ/

##

6m Septembr

aðelent /ˌa.ðeˈlɛnt/ [ˌa.ðɪˈlɛn(t)] tutor, teacher (especially privately-employed)

< 11C in later Old Boral, participle derivation from aðelir “to grant land” (> modern “to improve”), first referring to the stewards in the royal court responsible for granting land to lower nobles, such as barons. Later shifted along with the verb to guardians and tutors of the aristocracy, and by 18C also to privately-employed tutors in general.

    Y position d'Anscon com aðelent ag joun heriter apporta magn advantaç.

    Anscon's position as tutor to the young heir brought many advantages.

    /i ˌpo.ziˈtsjɔn  danˈxɔn kɔm ˌa.ðeˈlɛnt ɛj ʒun ˌe.ʀiˈtɛʀ ˌa.pɔʀˈta mɛjn ˌad.vanˈtats/

##

7m Septembr

    start of a letter sent in 1828 via steeplepost from Pentrose (a port town on the south coast of Borland) to Axton (a rapidly-growing industry town which at the time was located in French Guillenne [1], in the duchy of Avosche, known to Paris as Grand-Aix).

    The Rustigh Strait postal crossing from Ausbagn to Texel was not yet connected with air-steeples. In fact, the eventual connection only lasted a generation or so before a barrick line [electric telegraph cable] was laid down—one of the first such lines, beaten by Dovre-Caleis, which were successfully joined in 1852.

To the inestimable Niklás de Fecht,

Or should I be saying Nicolas Defechet, perhaps? You will at the very least do me the service of not moving to Paris, city of greed and carrion [2], even if you must learn some Proper French for the sake of your wife. (I have always preferred the Roun standard on aesthetic grounds, although I cannot entirely forget politics even here!) As Hortense is certainly reading, I shall pass on Clara's well-wishes and reiterate that you are welcome to make use of the Vauhan lencorve [line of credit]; just ask for Marduth at the Tyche Bank.

Pether's lot are also destined, it seems to leave fair, perfidious Albion. Despite my continuing assurances of welcome here they are determined to sail the great Mendevan river to whatever future they can find in Hasiny [3]. I must admit the prospect of being thousands of miles from any Dutchman has its appeal. (Forgive me! The last decade has made provincials of us all—mayhap even Provincials! [4])

The library has finally provided me with Dümmeyer's treatise on collection and delivery; I will not appease you by pretending to have read it, but be assured I will find the hours for it this very week…

——————–
[1] Corresponding approximately to Alsace, Lorraine and the Netherlands.

[2] The Kingdom of France was seen to have profited unjustly from the Second German War, gaining significant territory while (according to many) not investing much manpower or material resources.

[3] A Mendevan [North American] state now legally separate from Britain [a state comprising Wales, Devon, Kernow and parts of northern Ireland and Albion], corresponding roughly to East Texas, Louisiana, Arkansas, Oklahoma, and western Mississippi.

[4] This pun on “inhabitants of Provence” and “rural, sheltered people” is not uncommon in letters at this time, but not well understood in retrospect.

##

8m Septembr

martel prendr /maʀˈtɛl ˈpʀɛn.dʀ̩/ to step up, pitch in, help out

< attested from 15C in Kigman's poetry, an idiom literally meaning “to take the hammer”. The noun is from later Latin martellus, diminutive of a variant of marculus “hammer”. The verb is a direct descendant of Latin prehendō “I take, grasp, seize”.

    Y rey Eustasc meðes pris martel posc y quagment 1755 a Lisbon.

    King Eustace himself stepped up after the 1755 Lisbon earthquake.

    /i ˈri awˈstats meˈðɛz ˈprɪz marˈtɛl pɔx i kwagˈmɛnt seˈtɛtsɛnt ˌtsɪnkwanˈtsɪnk a lɪzˈbɔn/

##

9m Septembr

varous /vaˈʀuz/ shop, store, warehouse, stock, especially for non-food items

< 14C as varhous “warehouse, storage place”, from some Germanic compound, most probably Horther or Low Saxon. Earlier attestation of an Old English *wærhūs are disputed. In any case, from Classical German roots varō “goods, wares” and hūsam “house”. Meaning “shop, place to buy things” since late 18C.

    Jo fo yent tost demay eð y varous cociner no sta ovart.

    I had arrived too early and the kitchen store wasn't open.

    /ʒo fo ˈjɛnt ˈtɔst deˈme e‿ˈði vaˈruz ˌko.tsiˈnɛr no ˈsta oˈvart/ 

##

10m Septembr

    from academic textbook In Context: The Vascon Ascendancy by Katrin Hindman, contributing to the In Context series, published by Nottinger University Primers through the 1980s and 90s.

Y Reyaum Vascon a Navarr eð Aquitain asseyeu ag commet dy regn d'Ambrosc III un eðegl moneter vital.
The Vascon Kingdom of Navarre and Aquitaine at the start of Ambrose [Ambròix] III's reign inhabitated a key economic position.

Y païs sta l'onc entr y ci gran magl trocant republican ny Miterran (particuler posc Port Calè redugnt a mesur dy fenç 1449)
It was the link between the great republican trade networks of the Middlesea (especially since taking Port Calè in the 1449 partition)

eð y colanç marcer accelerandessem des Albion goineð e des, aust ancour, y citað colluïsc Theudesc e bautesc.
and the accelerating flow of goods from Welsh Albion and, further east, the Dutch and Graven guild cities.

Cant Andalus abattoy insorgenç politic—dou cris a succession e l'abansanc 1457 ne Lisbon, com eç primer—
While Andalus struggled with political unrest—two consecutive succession crises and the 1457 Lisbon Massacre, to start with—

Ambrosc careu influyenç vascon spandr de star un mojol dy magl trocant vars denir un font de recoglenç mejan per se.
Ambrose sought to extend Vascon influence from being a trade-mesh hub to becoming a seat of resource production in itself.

Dominanç brittesc sull'Atlantic au lontemp famigl pescour vasq frustrað,
British [Celtic] dominion over the Atlantic had long frustrated Vask fishing families,

dont ci sodal prepondraurn ja parmy l'aristocracy e posseyeurn forç politic remarcabr ny cort a Ambrosc.
members of which still predominated in the aristocracy and held significant political power in Ambrose's court.

Pall'oc façon sta y stabliscment dy Novel Enterpris Navarran un manouvr treblað :
In this way the founding of the New Navarre Enterprise was a three-fold manœuvre:

primer, oc reitfiau y position de Vascony com forniscour e porçonnour suprem de març trocað par Europ enter ;
first, it solidified Vascony's position as supreme supplier and distributor of trade goods throughout Europe;

dovem, oc dau a sy vassal y possendessem un rout vars autortað e risceç y thron noc menaçant ;
second, it gave his most powerful vassals an avenue to power and riches that did not threaten his position,

treim (ig degnant i poðe bogr savir), oc mis Vascony ne plaç por y descovart y signfiandessem ny siecr cinqueç acavar…
third (though he could not know this), it set up Vascony to make the most important discovery of the fifteenth century…

11m Septembr

quagir /kwaˈgɪr/ [kwɐˈgɪː] to tremble, shiver, quake

also quagment /kwagˈment/ a trembling, a shiver, an earthquake

< 9C in Old Boral, attested in various forms: quagiar, kegar, cokir among others. From Old English cwacian “quake"—eventually settled into -ir¹ conjugation with past participle quait /kwEt/. Noun from 14C, first seen in reference to the 1348 Furlain earthquake.

    "Jo poð noc oc sgardar,” dis i, vois quagent.

    “I can't watch this,” she said, her voice trembling.

    /ʒo ˈpɔθ nɔk ˈɔk sgarˈdar | dɪz ˈi | ˈvɔjz kwaˈgɛnt/

##

12m Septembr

eðegl /eˈðijl/ [ɪˈðijʊ] nook, alcove, niche

also position, controlled territory, strategic holding

< first attested 10C as edeȝl “small room, defensive cover”, showing that the martial meaning was a very early semantic extension. Descends directly (though presumably from a northern form, given the incomplete lenition of stressed /d/) from Latin ædicula “small temple, chapel, house, or room”, diminutive of ædis “temple, room”. No religious sense is confirmed in the Boral; compare Lombard edicola “kiosk, stand < shrine, alcove”.

    Jo slançau all'eðegl, attenent a respirar calmessem.

    I rushed to the alcove, trying to breathe more quietly.

    /ʒo slanˈtso a.leˈðijl | aˈtɛ.nɛnt a ˌrɛs.piˈrar kalˈmɛ.sɛm/

##

13m Septembr

    remainder of a letter sent in 1828 via steeplepost from Pentrose to , from the mathematician Darren Brodus (of the Vithor New University but at the time in convalescence at his family home) to his brother-in-law Niklás der Fecht, himself a professor at the  Benet School.

(Pentrose in red,  in blue)
image

…library has finally provided me with Dümmeyer's treatise on collection and delivery; I will not appease you by pretending to have read it, but be assured I will find the hours for it this very week! Dear Johan insists it is all atmospherical and futile nonsense, but then Johan trusts no mathematics since Craddock's book on distance room. Honestly I'm surprised he consents to use even the mechanical methods, but I suppose he isn't quite bone-headed enough to make his workshop designing more difficult for himself.

Have you any plans to go south this summer? I hear they shall reopen the arena in Verona to performances—they say Bruno's Wings Afire will be the first shown there since the war! I had the pleasure of knowing her brother Jacom before she was ever known to the wider world (apparently they are astonishingly similar in appearance, even for twins); we fought together at Hulm in 21. That was an astonishingly-mismanaged offensive and in all honestly prolonged the war for months, but I mustn't start this rant, or I will never end.

Jacom had nothing but praise for his sister and their parents, in any case (although the desire for a warm bed and the call of peacetime Furlain no doubt inflenced his recollection), so I cannot do anything but recommend the opera to you. If in the end you hate the singing you will at least have the pleasure of restored Roman architecture!

Your friend,
Darren Brodus

##

14m Septembr

redugr /reˈda.jr̩/ [ʀɪˈda.jɐ] to quell, subdue, subjugate; to diminish, reduce, bring down

< 9C in Old Boral reduȝre /reˈdʉɣ.rə/ “to quell (as of military resistance)”, semi-learned borrowing from Mediæval Latin redūcō idem, patterning after conduȝre < condūcō. Non-miltary senses a common Romance development and may reflect evolving use in Scholastic Latin; attested in Boral by 15C.

Y novel rout vil y burden redugr de març portar a Damborn.

The new road will make it easier to ferry goods to Dambourne.

/i noˈvɛl ˈrut vɪl i bɪrˈdɛn reˈda.jr̩ de ˈmarts pɔrˈtar a damˈbɔrn/

##

15m Septembr

març /ˈmarts/ [ˈmɑːs] goods, wares, products; things to sell, produce or trade

also adjective marcer /marˈtsɛr/ [mɑːˈtsɛː] commercial, relating to trade
commarç /koˈmarts/ [kʊˈmɑːs] trade, commerce, exchange

< first attested 10C in Old Boral març “wares”, seen used as the equivalent of Old English ƿara “wares”. This comes from Latin merx “merchandise, commodity”. Abstract noun commarç < commercium may have had influence on the specific sound changes. Adjective marcer in use from 14C, with ending -er < Latin -ārius, which forms adjectives from nouns and verbs.

    La sera març foyer ag marcað je Lun ne Haukirç.

    There will be small wares at the Monday market in Haukirch.

    /la seˈra ˈmarts fɔˈjɛr ɛj marˈkaθ ʒe ˈlɪn ne hoˈkɪrts/

##

16m Septembr

    extracts from Medic, Cur Tey: Y Preu Siecr de Santrafeu (Physician, Heal Thyself: The First Century of Santrafew), a Borlish-language history of one of the earliest New Provincial settlements, located at the mouth of the Becouin River [Charles River, Massachusetts]. The book focuses mostly on diary extracts from various settlers.

Y reverend Carol Zancoud sta cos plausibr y parson l'influyendessem vars y defas moneter dy bourg.
The reverend Carol Zancoud was possibly the most important factor in the town's economic development.

I luïsceu l'ig sona por y bourg des 1584 fin a morir 1630, untal possenç politic aðoint
He ministered to the town from 1584 until his death in 1630, gaining such political power

ig Sauveir d'Orz (bourgmer 1620–26) dis lonc tesmoin “Un parol soul dy reverend sceus y stif animar a me mortfiar.”
that Sauveir d'Orz (mayor 1620–26) reportedly said “One word from the reverend could have had me executed by the mob.”

Pre lugnt de siecr settoç ja l'au autortað emposseïð sur ci accaçað marcer de Provenç.
By the dawn of the seventeenth century he had already assumed authority over what Provence sent to trade.

Oc sevent vien strait des y quayer a sy figl primer, Natagl :
What follows is an extract from the diary of his eldest child Natalie:

Tant con y carc usual de març calscon desirað dy Concert Mastoiset sbolognar con
Along with the usual bevy of whichever goods the Concert wishes to foist upon the Mastoisèt

(bon Paðr crey nien aðerraun nostrell'amicabiltað tal com eramn e far may accaçant,
(Father believes nothing will do as much good for our diplomacy as sending more bronze and iron,

ben sinoc trevaglour demay por ig effojar e forgar nossy cavibr),
or at least more men to mine and smith any we can dig up for ourselves),

Marco sta vellig aðief por y ci hebdomader Marseglan accomblar, donc eu nos novel de veil mont y preu veg dell'annað.
Marco was good enough to stockpile the Marsella weekly papers, so we have news of the old country for the first time all year.

##

17m Septembr

fenç /fɛnts/ split, division, breakup; cleft, rift, crack

< 6C, first attested in very early Old Boral/late Vernacular Latin fenza /ˈfen.tsɐ/ “cutting, splitting, cleaving”, developing through the Middle Ages to Old Boral fenç “splitting, division”. Presumably a haplologised form of Latin findentia “a cleaving, a splitting” (stress shifted in Borland Latin to initial, as in all third-conjugation verbs), nominalisation of findō “I split, cleave, break up”. The extension in meaning to the concrete “a cleft, rift, crack” evidenced from 12C.

    Vos pouð noc for pegntar sur un fenç ny mur.

    You can't just paint over a crack in the wall.

    /vo ˈpuθ nɔk fɔr pinˈtar sɪr ɪn ˈfɛnts ni ˈmɪr/

##

18m Septembr

abansanc /ˌa.banˈsank/ [ˌa.bɐnˈzaŋk] massacre, bloodbath, rout

< 14C, slightly irregular derivation from abagn sanc /aˈbaɲ sank/ “bathes in blood”. The verb is from (now obsolete) abagnar “to bathe in, to submerge under”, verbal derivation of bagn “bath” < Latin balneum “bath(room)”. The noun is from Vulgar Latin sanguem “blood”, an alteration of sanguis, sanguinem (but forms like sangre do survive into Old Boral and later in some dialects).

    Y siesc eð abansanc sevent a Wackefell denommau Rey David.

    The siege and subsequent Wackefell massacre destroyed King David's reputation.

    /i ˈsjɛx e‿ˌða.banˈsank seˈvɛnt a ˌva.keˈfɛl ˌde.noˈmo ri daˈvɪd/

##

19m September

    excerpted from 1997 academic textbook Del Christianizamento dell'Isole Norroveste (On the Christianisation of the Northwestern Isles), written by historian of mediæval religion Dijaca Rofotto, working as a researcher and archivist for the Librario Soprano (Sovereign Library) of the Autonome University of Saint Marinus.

…survived, however, in those few remaining monasteries spared from the Danish raids (note in particular the Chronicle of Carlile, which survives only in copy at the Tremonnow Brethin House, having presumably been ferried across the Irish Sea before the monastery at Carlile burnt down in around 1013 N).

But to understand the final years of Wulfstan the Younger of Northumber, we must look two centuries earlier, to the very first Viking raids on the shores of Albion and Ireland. The tales of similar raids on Borland towns—and on monasteries in particular—would surely have been common knowledge by the time they spread westward, sacking Halgrive in the summer of 764.

Nonetheless, the English kingdoms were prepared for these raids (and eventually, invasions) to vastly different degrees. Compare the fates of Merch Lincon to Northumbrian Yorwick: the former never fell, and in fact became the most heavily-fortified town in Markland for the several decades it served as the de facto border of Danish rule. In part this was the inevitable result of tensions along the Welsh border; even during the relatively-long period of peacetime both Theodric the Awefast and Owain of Brochwel maintained ever-more levy forces to police the long Severn Line.

By contrast, Yorwick almost gave itself over to Dane rule. Though convincing records are either fragmentary or clearly biased (even Brethin writing in this period cannot be entirely trusted), it is generally believed that the alderman's court threw him out pre-emptively as a sop, hoping to offer enough treasure freely to the invaders that they could avoid a total destruction of the city. Yorwick's key economic position within Dane-ruled Albion in the next century certainly suggest these efforts were not unsuccessful…

##

20m Septembr

influïr /ˌɪn.flajˈjɪr/ [ˌɪɱ.flɐˈjɪː] to influence, to affect, to manipulate (someone)

also influyenç influence, power

< 15C, Middle Boral borrowing from Latin īnfluō “I flow in, crowd in, insinuate, invade”, with conjugation modeled after that of luïr “to play” < lūdō (itself an analogical development).

    Vostrell'yam poð gent influïr ne ci Palaç Jagovin meðes.

    Your uncle can influence people in even the Jacobin Estates.

    /voˈstɾɛˈljam pɔθ ˈʒɛnt ˌin.flajˈjɪr ne tsi paˈlats ˌʒa.goˈvɪn meˈðɛz/

##

21r Septembr

spandr /ˈspan.dr̩/ [ˈspan.dɐ] to expand, broaden, spread out, extend, widen

variant in -s of -r conjugation (past participle spas)

< 10C in Hostilian's Histories in reference to increase of territory. Straightforward derivation from Latin expandō “I spread out, expand, explain”, from ex- “out” and pandō “I spread, unfold”. Remote past forms in -oy by analogy with similar verbs.

    Saveð vos cascif aug span ne gelant ?

    Do you know why water expands when it freezes?

    /saˈvɛθ vo kaˈxɪf oj ˈspan ne ʒeˈlant/

##

22m Septembr

    excerpted from Paç Mondial Accommettent (Waging World Peace), written by Neuvort writer Dion Becolla and published in 1968. Unlike many so-called giftales, this tale owes more to the masquira genre of a century before—it is even set in the late 1880s, the height of the Grant-Jeu period.

“Lucas Marcuszen ?” dis Sr Barracat, posc l'ouçon de novel associað a Benjamin oïr.
“Lucas Marcuszen?” Mr. Barracat said, when he heard Benjamin's choice for his new partner.

“Bon trianç. Ma la's uncos vos valent savir pre decidr final.”
“Good choice. But there is something you should know before making a final decision.”

“Jo connosc deja l'es dannesc, bossir,” dis Benjamin. Certan des autr agent vascon scanen reclam cas y nation a Lucas,
“I'm already aware he's Danish, sir,” Benjamin said. A number of the other Vascon agents took issue with Lucas' nationality,

suggernt ig, com outem, i no'r for comfiabr tandic y ci envou a JY no fosn y ci a Collujon combattent sou ciel ovart.
suggesting that, at best, he could only be trusted as long as JY's interests weren't in open conflict with the Collusion's.

Parveg s'entrova Benjamin carraçon il apponen tout ig, s'y Bel Joy denoy violent, JY vel obligað sociar con Vascony.
Benjamin sometimes wondered why they assumed that, if the Good Game became violent, JY would automatically side with Vascony.

I sta un organisment tras tarm, cas final. Calscon parmy pos fieltað descovrir sevrað.
It was an international organisation, after all. Any of them could face divided loyalties.

“Eð i retien comport allagr con, donc i sera nominal un souject, ða nien autr.”
“And he's still on good terms with them, so he must at least nominally be a subject.”

Barracat ig ignorau agil. “Oc son casc agent dannesc. Marcuszen, soul, es tan kinæd admis.”
Barracat brushed that aside. “All of the Danish agents are. Marcuszen, however, is also an admitted thayen.”

##

23m Septembr

mojol /moˈʒɔl/ [mʊˈʝɔw] hub; centrepoint, midpoint; common ground

< attested 10C as mojol “hub of a wheel” (metaphorical senses seen much later but the semantic leap is clear enough), from Latin modiolus “hub” < modius “a measure of grain or dry volume, being about 2½ gallons” (compare modern Boral moisc, a similar unit). Justification for the derivation well-attested but unclear.

    Yorvic ne siecr deg denoy mojol commarç poll'eiðel dannesc.

    Tenth-century York became a trade hub for the Danish lands.

    /jɔrˈvɪk ne ˈsjɛ.kr̩ dij deˈnɔj moˈʒɔl koˈmarts po.liˈðɛl daˈnɛx/

##

24m Septembr

sodal /soˈdal/ [sʊˈdaw] member (of a group), family member, element, component

< 16C across Romance languages in Revitalism texts, directly borrowed from Scholastic Latin sodālis “member of a group or association”, influenced in meaning by sodālitās “group, association, fraternity” (the original Latin sodālis means “companion, mate, fellow, comrade”). Used for objects as well as people from 18C, but popularised in mathematics by its use in the Borlish translation of Dümmeyer's seminal work on set theory.

    Implausibr sta l'oc amigtað entr soldart e sodal dy cort a Rey David.

    This friendship of a soldier and a member of King David's court was unlikely.

25m Septembr

    excerpted from David Huhn's economic history textbook Stadstahten: Sichrer Haven (City-States: Safe Harbor), a treatise on the role of independent trade cities in the development of mercantilism. The book was published in 1923 with funding from the Lunburg Schazhaus.

…close of the thirteenth century as a convenient cutoff. The fiction of a surviving German Empire was laid to rest with the corpse of the first Kingdom of Saxony, with the domain of the Hoghbrocken dynasty reduced to their core lands upstream in Lepzi and Magdeburg. The newly-reduced Margrave Baldur of Saxony, being only 4 years old at the time, was understandably outmanœuvred by the nascent Stadbund.

But the guild-states known variously as the Stadbund, the Samslutning or the North Collision (we see in Latin documents usually collisio theodisc) did not, of course, appear fully-formed at the dawn of the fourteenth century. We can trace the roots of intercivic contracts restricting tariffs, tolls and other fees back to the early twelfth century. Indeed, even at the height of the Mediæval Imperia, traditionally portrayed as a world (or a least a Europe) of central authority, mayors of the towns in the low Elbe and its tributaries were collaborating independently of their respective lieges.

We must look at early Bardwick, which along with Hamburg and Lunburg comprised the founding core—and later the trading heart—of the Stadbund. Its particular interest stems from its dual position as court of the Hoghbrocken kings and as a highly mercantile trade city. King Ruprecht II in particular will serve as a useful…

26m Septembr

prepondrar /ˌpre.pɔnˈdrar/ [ˌpʀe.pʊnˈdʀɑː] to preponderate, be in the majority, outnumber, outweigh

< late 15C, from Latin præponderō “I outweigh, preponderate” and modelled synchronically as pre- with pondrar “to weigh down, sag, be heavy, encumber”. This is from Latin ponderō “I weigh, ponder, weigh up”, a verbal derivation of pondus “weight” (see also modern Boral pendr “to hang, drape”, a cognate).

    Alor deum nos raccular, dec l'enmig nos prepondra.

    Then we had to retreat as the enemy outnumbered us.

    /aˈlɔr dawm no ˌra.kiˈlar | dɛk lɛnˈmaj no ˌpre.pɔnˈdra/

27m Septembr

manouvr /maˈnu.vr̩/ [mɐˈnu.vɐ] tactic, manœuvre, turn (of a game), act

< from Old Boral manowre “labour, handiwork”, from Mediæval Latin manopera, manuopera “work done by hand”, from manu “hand” plus operor “I work”. The semantic shifts to military action by 12C, possibly following (or leading) similar shift in French etc. Used in sport and games from 15C.

    Il porraun bogr y manouvr paregl repeðir souscen.

“Noc ig Sy Nobiltað veir, per se,” demorau Zoe, se sarvent dell'occasion por tornar avars e sy novel soiç sgardar fort. Cavel fosc e coutrað cort ; oc no stant usual parmy gent ant lonc caðen ancestral veunt proism, ma dy tegnt visacer val le pesar noc enter foran.
“Not to see His Grace, per se,” Zoe demurs, taking the opportunity to turn and look properly at her new companion. Dark hair, closely-shorn—not typical among those with long family ties in the area, though his complexion suggested he wasn't entirely foreign.

“O, clar comoscon!” Y soury prolongant tras y visaç dy segr es delicat e curious. Un hom raut par enigma, parcanç, ben toð cas oc volent le supportar por gra de collocq. “Vos conjuraçonnað se pondr sull'un rout deur nossy tragres souscen, j'appon ?”
“Ah, of course!” The smile that spreads across the man's face is charming and curious. Someone delighted at mystery, perhaps, or at least someone willing to humour it for the sake of conversation. “You plan to stand one street over from tomorrow's parade, I take it?”

2m Octobr

y mont se creir /i ˈmɔnt se ˈkrir/ to be arrogant, think highly of oneself, think the world of oneself

< 17C, literally “believe oneself (to be) the world”, survivor from a host of similar expressions floating around at the end of the seventeenth century. The noun is a direct descendant of Latin mundus “ornaments, decorations” via a Mediæval Latin sense “universe, world, mankind”. The verb is also straightforwardly from Latin crēdō “I believe, trust”.

3m Octobr

auvorn /oˈvɔrn/ [ʊˈvɔːn] a lot, broadly, copiously, to a great extent

< attested from 11C in Old Boral aworne /awˈwoɾnə/ (among other forms) “generously, with plenty, abundantly”, presumably from Latin phrase ab ūrnā “out of a vessel/water jar”. Irregular lowering of long <ū> not completely explained but long vowels were in any case rare before <rC> clusters. The adverb became obsolete by 15C but was reintroduced to common speech by its use in popular historical trevolds in the late eighteenth century.

    Jo spiau ty femn beint auvorn ag tarn floy.

    I saw your wife drinking copiously at the riverside pub.

    /ʒo ˈspjo ti ˈfɛmn bint oˈvɔrn ɛj ˈtarn flɔj/

7m Octobr

    excerpted from Borlish book Lugnt Ros Sur Ont Lugnt (Rosy Dawn on Glittering Waves), a popular account of the development of seafarers since the 1471 Landfall. Written by amateur historian Descard Ausogn in 1929, it was criticised for its uncritical view of pirate culture, and in particular his depiction of their role in the Romantic Wars.

…example of early international cooperation, the Bordew-Audaz canal was finally completed in 1679, following over a decade of delays due ultimately to Vascony's losses to Andalus in the south.

These great infrastrusture projects characterise the Romantic period, although these decades would not gain that appellation for another century. The name is first used after the Democratic Wars, when the latter half of the seventeenth century was seen, nonsensically, as the last golden age of refinement and civilisation. It is hard not to contrast these insular, squabbling European states of the 1660s with, say, the great geoscopic voyages of mac Kellot and al-Casmi of the previous century, or the renewed Novomundine interest of the next.

Nonetheless, it is in this time that we can see the familiar modern map begin to emerge across Europe. The shape of Vascony begins to stabilise at last, and to the east the German marksmen and great mayors contemplate, perhaps, the idea of cooperation. Even the fractious religious politics further south threaten to give way to the more secular animosities of popular unrest and land grabs.

In particular, Naples finally finds itself unshackled, neither from defunct Greek imperial rule, nor from the heathens of Sicily, nor from the Papal embrace of Rome. With this context King Ladislau's obsessive focus on military strength, and in particular the Marina Ottima, seems entirely reasonable…

9m Octobr

gahað /gaˈaθ/ jet, lignite; a brilliant black colour

< 11C in Old Boral gaað “jet”, directly from Latin gagātēs “hard, black asphalt”, from Greek. Use for colour probably via heraldry; gaað (and its cognates, including Norman gais) contrasted with sabl in referring to a shining black, as in enamel. Later also used mostly for a connotation of value.

11m Octobr

por gra de /pɔr ˈgra de/ for the sake, benefit of, on behalf of

< late Old Boral 12C, unchanged in meaning. Development as a fixed expression continued until by 15C we see primarily por gra de mey over por my gra (for my sake). The word gra “need, benefit” does not retain those meanings in modern Boral (keeping only “extent, degree, importance”), and is itself probably a contamination of Latin gravis “heavy, serious” by grātus “pleasing, agreeable, beloved” (which gives modern grað “will, wont, way”).

    Ke oc farreð vos toð cas, façað oc por gra dy Segr.

    As you will act thus regardless, act in the name of the Lord.

    /ke ɔk faˈrɛθ vo tɔθ ˈkaz | faˈtsaθ ɔk por ˈgra di ˈsi.jr̩/

12m Octobr

demorar /ˌde.moˈrar/ [ˌde.mʊˈʀɑː] to demur, delay, put off, postpone

< 12C in early Middle Boral, taken from Church Latin dēmorāre “to detain, hold up, delay”, a regularisation of deponent Latin verb dēmoror “I detain, linger”, itself derived from mora “delay”. Used especially of evasive speech, with connotation of nefarious motive.

    Jo vey alcun raçon por nos demoreu.

    I see no reason we should delay.

    /ʒo ˈvi aˈgɪn raˈtsɔn pɔr no ˌde.moˈraw/

PART Γ: Answer the following questions in a single word. (maximum 5 minutes)

1. In which county, ruled by Count Jerròn, did the 1170 "Emergency" take place, utterly destroying the keep and leading to the secession of Burgundy from (and eventually the dissolution of) the Constant Empire?
- Answer: Dijon

2. What relation was King Adalmar of Bavaria to his successor Empress Katharina of Germany, the last ruler of a united Germany for over five hundred years?
- Answer: Grand-uncle

3. Hamburg was the first German city to formally declare as not subject to the rule of the Emperor, in 1207 N. But which city, almost as powerful a member of the nascent Collision (the Stadbund) as Hamburg herself, followed suit just one year later?
Answer: Dorstad

4. Musab as-Sakr of Ispella led an early faction for independence from the Single Caliphate in the last decade of the twelfth century. But during what activity did he fall to his death, aged 40, postponing such revolutionary ideas for another two generations?
Answer: Prayer

5. Archbishop Wolfstan the Ascete of Yorwick was executed by Emperor Victor II on All Saint's Day 1189 N for theft of royal property.  What items, made of gold, had he stolen?
Answer: Vambraces

15m Octobr

tegnt visacer /ˈtint ˌvi.zaˈtsɛr/ [tɪɱˌvi.zɐˈtsɛ:] complexion, facial appearance

< 18C, literally "facial tint", first used in the early ethnographies translated into Boral. The noun tegnt "tint, shade, colour, dye" is from the Latin tinctus "dipped in, treated, coloured, tinged", past participle of tingō "I wet, moisten, dip, immerse" (whence Boral tegndr). The adjective is a standard derivation from visaç "face, visage", from Old Boral visazȝ "face, regard, look". This is as if from Latin *visāticum, though it is likely a later derivation, from vis < visus "look". Justification for the retention of short <i> is unclear but forms with <e> are entirely unattested.

Lour y tegnt visacer suggeren jorn larc sou sol blanc.

Their complexions were indicative of many days in the sun.

/lur i ˈtint ˌvi.zaˈtsɛr ˌsɪg.ʒeˈrɛn ʒɔrn lark su ˈsɔl blank/

16m Octobr

    extract and Borlish translation from textbook Albic Medicine in the Workshop Decades, Part V: Alchemic Detaxion, written by Dr Harold Westcamp and published 1994 by the Conster Health Edifice, funded by the Markland Ministry for Health.

Portant y benoç por y natur contr-zoia a cuivr ant ci issent spandent y laðessem no sera for y retenour stauron.
But the application of copper's counter-zoic [1] manner with the furthest-reaching consequences can only be the stauron retainer [2].

Nentamen, majour es ig nossy sgart modern sevrar deull'ig temporane. Y ci nos appariscent com sodal evident dy stauron recaren mout dezein d'annað por yembr.
Nonetheless it is important to separate our modern point of view from the contemporary. What seem to us like obvious components of the retainer often took dozens of years to innovate.

The theoretical barrier to developing this kind of prevention was recognising that copper metal could kill sperm cases as well as zoia.

Ig degnant y natur contr-zoia a cuivr au stað mis a regest a e posc 1789 (ig inclus y ci ouvr d'alchemist Suxúmawaca bint as-Sáffat), l'oc parceuçon spandr fin a sperm covrir vole noc avenir pre 1824, ny traiscnant dy Var Theudesc Dovem.
Although copper's counter-zoic manner had been documented since 1789 (see the work of alchemist Suxúmawaca bint as-Sáffat), extending this observation to sperm would have to wait until 1824, in the aftermath of the Second German War.

[1] anti-microbial

[2] intra-uterine device

17m Octobr

tragres /traˈgrɛz/ parade, march, charge

< 13C, originally restricted to military charges and infantry attacks, by 16C it had extended to formalised military parades and by 19C to civilian events also. A deverbal formation from Old Boral tragressar “to charge, go forth” (now also “to parade, to march”), a regularisation after the past participle from early *tragreir, from Latin trānsgredior “I step across, climb over” (with analogical shortening of the prefix trāns- as in trādūcō, for example).

    L'aucoum clamaurn tandic ençau y tragres.

    The crowd cheered as the parade began.

    /loˈkum klaˈmorn̩ tanˈdɪk ɛnˈtso i traˈgrɛz/

18m Octobr

spuðar/spiˈðar/ [spɪˈðɑː] to reject, abhor, refuse, repel

< from as early as 8C in Old Boral ispuðar “to reject, spit out, throw out”, also “despise, loathe” (but those senses are lost by Middle Boral). This is from Late Latin exspūtō “I spit out, eject”, a regularisation via the passive participle of third-declension exspuō, which has the same meaning. First used of magnetic material in the fifteenth century, in translation from Classical texts.

    Euspeð saveu luy vale le spuðar, ma oy er i attragnt apar.

    Elspeth realised she ought to reject him, but now she was intrigued.

    /awˈspɛθ saˈvaw laj vaˈle le spiˈðar | pi ˈɔj ɛr i aˈtrɛjnt aˈpar/

19m Octobr

    excerpted from a 1934 copy of the Fiellas Dimenja, a Marsellan entertainment newspaper incorporated in 1871 by the Guiraud Colloquium. At this time they enjoyed considerable international readership in Vascony and New Provence (it being republished synchronously in Santrafew), and indeed was even read in Collusion territory, despite the rising tensions of the post-Millstone Europe.

...replaced with new blood as Vréqueu Margouille qualifies for the Kentish team going into the fourth LGT. Despite rumours that lineball is looking westward to Hasiny next year, the bilateral committee has refused to comment.

FILM IN REVIEW
- Hωn a Kyvodoð (The Ascendant), in odyssey from the 7th of July

I shall be honest—the timing of this release so soon after the solstice perhaps let me better indulge the obviously-propagandist elements of Dormaget's latest historical courtly drama. The Vascon Ascendancy is no new ground for the screen, with Agochino's more personal Sou Ambroxo (I am Ambrose) defining the popular perception of Vascony's longest-reigning king. Nevertheless, I find The Ascendant to be a charming retelling of those turbulent years after the 1471 Landfall, not least in its choice to focus on the more minor personages of Ambrose's court.

As an example (though her Vask is terrible in the few scenes they stray from Cambrick), Lyneth Madox plays Jata, the seamstress-wife of an Enterprise sailor. It is through her eyes that we can understand the whole of the city of Bilvaw as it is changed by the Crown's attentions. Throughout, Madox gives Jata a steel resolve and implacable practicality sadly lacking in today's usual dramatic protagonists.

Domaget uses the narrow view to illuminate the broad in every aspect of his filmmaking, and we really see the benefits of such an approach here. If the scenes were confined to the palaces and castles of Huzatre and Cartaw, it would be hard to connect the Welsh tangles in Albion (one does rather wish the film had spent less time on the Wars of Fealty, but such things are inevitable when Britain sets a film in the fifteenth century) to the second marriage of Alexandra of Vascony and her famed voyage. Instead we can feel the sense of parental obligation warring with wanderlust in both threads of this filmic tapestry.

The Ascendant will doubtless be a staple of the summer screen, and it well deserves the honour. For the best experience I recommend you spend the afternoon drinking Empordan and singing as many songs of glory as you can think of.
–Jacint Brunat

20m Octobr

nijommar /ˌniˈʒoˈmar/ [ˌni.ʝʊˈmɑː] to bind books, (metaphorically) to compile, to bring together, (in alchemy) to synthesise

< 10C in Old Boral neȝjo(n)mar, a derivation modelled after earlier neȝjonment, "bookbinding" (modern Boral nijomment "binding, compilation, synthesis"). The noun is presumably a faux derivation from *neȝjon, neȝsion (although these are not attested), from Latin nexiō "tying together, fastening". This is the nominalisation of nectō "I bind, tie, fasten", whence via Middle Boral semi-learned borrowing we also have connectr "to connect,  link up" and connexion "connection, link".

    Frederic rovau : "Cu nijommau l'oc livr tan ligs ?"

    Frederick asked "Who bound this book so smoothly?"

    /ˌfre.deˈrɪk roˈvo | ki ˌni.ʒoˈmo lɔk ˈli.vr̩ tan ˈlajz/

21r Octobr

pagrmen /pɛjrˈmɛn/ [ˌpɛ.ʝɐˈmɛn] parchment, vellum, (erroneously) papyrus; scroll, codex

< 8C in those few monastic records that began to be written in the vernacular (in particular, the Tobarus source) in the nascent Kingdom of Borland. Often at first parȝmente with spurious ending as though from a -mentum nominal, this comes from Latin pergamīna “parchment” (see that the final syllable would normally yield -min but for the aforementioned analogy), originally referring to the Greek city of Pergamon, where it was invented as an alternative to Egyptian papyrus.

    L'oc reliquat tien y pagrmen original dell'Accort Ducal.

    /lɔk ˌre.liˈkwat ˈtjɛn y pɛjrˈmɛn oˌri.ʒiˈnal ˌde.laˈkɔrt diˈkal/

##

22m Octobr

    excerpted from Part VI: The Republic of Provence, from The Latin Republics: Jewels under Tyrian Standards, a history of the mercantile republics on the Middlesea from 1000 to 1800 N written by Jacob Trelaune and published 1902 by the Yievle Brethin Primers.

…would not be formally recognised until Burgundy's defeat two years later at the Battle of Margès, it was this event which cemented House Amigòt's position as primus inter pares in the new republic. 

At least, that is, for the next eighteen years of manoeuvring against a hungry Barcelon and their piratical allies who de facto ruled Marsella (it is generally understood that Lord Amigòt [Daniel Menton] established Marsella as the capital of Provence in large part to closely monitor Jondumal's fleets, about which see Part III: Old Jeans). By the end of his rule in 1517, Lord Amigòt had solidified the borders with Burgundy and Barcelon, with Narbona left with double obligations to Menton and to Jaume III.

It is also supposed—Amigòt did not do us the service of keeping a chronicle—that his rule lasted eighteen years to allow Menton to ensure his chosen successor enjoyed near-unanimous support of the Concert, the Provincial legislative house modelled after the Venisians'. His first choice Raimond Aurellex, who had married his youngest sister and whose brother Lohis had died leading the charge at the Battle of Satillew, died in a hunting accident in 1511 (and despite the popular understanding, this particular incident was likely not an assassination).

In the end, the Concert elected Menton's cousin Antoin Amigòt to lead the Concert under the close attention and martial guidance of daus Sanz, through whom Daniel Menton doubtless exercised his influence long after his nominal stepping down. But military advice would be of only limited use to Antoin, as in 1519 a new threat arrived in Europe not seen since the days of the German Secession. The Second Great Dying struck naval powers early and mercilessly, and Provence was to be no exception…

23m Octobr

dereðr /deˈre.ðr̩/ [dɪˈʀɛ.ðɐ] in the back, on the other side, opposite; also finally, as the last thing/act

< 8C in Old Boral deredre “behind” (as an adverb), either directly from Vulgar Latin deretro or a later derivation from Old Boral redre “behind” (as a preposition), which itself gives modern reðr with the same meaning. The adverb was replaced in its original sense by ados < a- + dos “back” < Latin dorsum, certainly by 16C but perhaps earlier.

    Nos vism dou segr entrar ny varous dereðr.

24m Octobr

stemn /ˈstɛ.mn̩/ [ˈste.mɐn] lineage, heritage, pedigree, breed

< attested in 11C in Old Boral istemne “tree trunk, stem”, from Old English stemn “stem, trunk, mast”. The metaphor of “ancestry is a tree” is pervasive in the language, and the literal meaning had been entirely displaced by native taisc/tronc (depending on dialect) by the fourteenth century. In modern times it is usually restricted to animal pedigree—using it of humans may be mocking or parodic.

    Il voun noc un coval d'untal stemn haut antal preç bas!

    They won't sell a horse with such a good pedigree at such a low price!

    /ɪl ˈvun nɔk ɪn koˈval dɪnˈtal ˈstɛ.mn̩ ˈhot anˈtal ˈprɛts ˈbaz/

Burnows is considered to have masterfully followed his readership as they grew up; this new series enjoyed great success among those who read Back to Front as children.

28m Octobr

    translation into Borlish and English of a recipe for Frigotta de Rei Jaume (King James Stew), taken from mealbook Pasti Mezziterreni (Middlesea Meals), written in 1880 by traveller Josforo Sant'Angelo.

Preu cos : l'amand tostir fin a aurfiað e lou pisar ne pouvr gros. An frigsoir lað l'ougl raccaldar, e soulor l'amand, agl, ugnlac eð arjon frigr par cinqueç minut atorn, ben fin ag leum sein mol.
First bake the almonds until golden and grind to a coarse powder. In a broad pan heat the oil, then gently fry the almond, garlic, onion and pepper for roughly fifteen minutes, or until the vegetables are soft.

Scon cos : y gran pevr e demy a vurt mettr e toglar con. Posc dou minut un dragm ag vin mettr e parmettr de boglir pres apart. Or y rougl scadom ben scadom pisað mettr, y remanent ag vin scencar dessur, e tout cocinar cinqueç minut may.
Add the drypepper and half the herbs and stir together. After two minutes add a dram of the wine and allow it to mostly boil off. Now add the shadome sauce or chopped shadomes, pour in the rest of the wine, and cook for fifteen minutes more.

Tarç cos : y zossa a pescot saleyar e lou appoyar ny rougl. Y frigsoir covauclir e laiscar cinq minut, fin ag pescot sein just cogt. Y repasc descovrir, appendr con y remanent ag vurt (ben y sunt calscon desirað) eð y saf jait dessur. Le dar ne bol con pan dur.
Salt the strips of fish and push them into the sauce. Cover the pan and leave for five minutes, until the fish is just cooked. Uncover, add the rest of the herbs (or whichever amount suits) and cast the juice over the dish. Serve bowled with hardbread.

30m Octobr

atras /aˈtraz/ [ɐˈtʀaz] across (as an adverb), over, from one side to the other

< first seen as one word in 12C atras “across”, making it one of the last positional adverbs to enter common use—earlier a tras is attested with the same meaning. The root is from preposition tras “across”, from Latin trāns “across, beyond” (one of the few monosyllables in -s which is not the nominative of a noun, and so a rare example of final -s from Latin being retained into Boral).

    Ja sta la un band de figl corrent atras.

    There was already a group of kids running to the other side.

    /ˈʒa sta la ɪn ˈband de ˈfajl koˈrɛnt aˈtraz/

##

31r Octobr

    in translation from L'Inclusion Deviante di Conti Polari (The Deviance Incorporation of Folk Tales), 1970 sociological work by Dr Tanasio Capretti, theologian and humanist at the University of Florence. It was published simultaneously with dozens of other related books from academies across the world, in order to coincide with the hundred and twentieth anniversary of the Laic Declarations and the concommittant realignment in the mid-nineteenth century that heralded the Good Game period.

Chapter 14: La Caccia Salvaggia (The Wild Hunt)

Just as the Danes attributed the thunder to the wrath of Thor, so too we can imagine whence came the tales of a great army of hunting party of ghosts, riding and shouting across the night sky. The howling winds, after all, sound not so differently from the howls of hunting dogs. From isolated hamlets north of the Humber to snow-blanketed Alpine villages, the motherly advice given to all children when they hear the Great Wild Hunt (or the Herlathing, or the Wütendes Heer) passing overheard is the same: stay inside, look away, do your best to pretend they aren't there.

Most scholars today accept that the various folk traditions of a ghostly hunt stem from a single ancient source, at least in Europe (the similar Lanhyatian stories are of course unrelated). Many of these tales come specifically from the Danes' Oskarrej via cultural transmission in the Viking Age—for example, in Borland the story of the cauç ragnt shows clear signs of Danish origin (not least that it is usually led by Balegr, a common kenning for Odin), but the tales often incorporate the graveyard fiddlers, who are idiosyncratic to the island.

Like so many of these tales, what had been amoral or even virtuous characters in the early mediæval period (see Chapter VI, and the works of Braun & Kindler) were transformed by Christianisation into devils and demonic figures. It should therefore be of no surprise that the early Deviance Movement seized the imagery of the Wild Hunt for themselves. Cráyenschot's celebrated Ad Pentaculum spends much of the runtime detailing Jochem's induction into the Yacht, including one…

1r November

vriðel /vriˈðɛl/ [vʀɪˈðɛw] curtains, drapes, blinds, (in figurative use) veil, barrier, concealment

< from 10C in Old Boral vriðel “curtains; bandages, wrapped cloth”, a nominal diminutive derivation from vriðar “to wrap, plait, bind (of cloth)”; the verb became obsolete by the Middle Boral period, displaced by fasçar (modern fassar). This verb came from Old English wrīþan “to twist, wrap, bind”, from Classical German wrīþan “to weave, twist”. This also gives French ridew “curtains”, probably via High German ridil “ribbon” (note that Norman French retains cortine, from Late Latin cōrtīna “curtain”).

    Un hom au y vriðel jognt por alcun lumner yemois y cambr.

    Someone had closed the curtains so no light would enter the bedroom.

    /ɪn ˈɔm o i vriˈðɛl ˈʒɔjnt pɔr aˈgɪn lɪmˈnɛr jɛˈmɔjz i ˈkam.br̩/

2m Novembr

folker /fɔlˈkɛr/ [fʊwˈkɛː] fern, bracken, thick brambles; countryside covered in these plants, especially when it is thus impassable; (in mathematics) fractal, self-similar figure

< first attested 11C in various forms as Old Boral folcair, falcaȝr, felcair,… “fern, bracken”. This comes from a reconstructed Vulgar Latin *filicāria (“thicket of ferns”), although there were probably variants with inital vowel as <a> or <u>. This noun is a late-derivation collective of Latin filix “fern”, probably from Greek. Despite popular attempts to link the Borlish form to Old English folc “people, nation, crowd”, this is semantically untenable.

    Folker pendr tras port poð aer cordiallessem dar a vossy repaðr.

    Hanging ferns outside can make your home feel more welcoming.

3m Novembr

    in translation to Borlish and English from the 1998 scholastic quire Sviluppi in Philosophia dal Periodo Revitalisto (Developments in Philosophy since the Revitalist Period) by historical deixist Jovanna Ariperto, researcher at the Autonome University of Saint Marinus. 

Renduð connuðessem par y ci ouvr a Dufresne e Fayeda y dezein final de siecr settoç, l'oc apponment affirmau toð við aurn se formað ag moment de Creation.
Popularised by the works of Dufresne and Fayeda in the last decade of the seventeenth century, this theory asserted that all life had formed at the moment of Creation.

I parmaneu n'affirmant casc veunt prosequent crescoirn des gendr minuscr de lou (nommað zoia par Dufresne, valent noc oc broðiar con y benoç actual dy parol við unicasel descrint), lou meðes form minusclessem stant y generation grand-figl, et cetera.
It further stated that each successive generation grew from miniature versions of themselves (which Dufresne called zoia, not to be confused with our modern use of the term for one-case life), themselves containing yet-smaller forms for the grandchild generation, and so on.

Figur temporane un form human piquessem afont ne casel sperm mostrant illustrn l'oc apponment l'un eð y creinç y dou ig un maðr no sta for hum y babe (semnað dy paðr) la aðoint.
Contemporary diagrams showing a tiny human form inside a spermcase illustrate both this theory and the impression that the mother was merely the soil where the baby, planted by the father, grew.

Ci attriboint, problem de predestination innað coll'oc parceuçon—pu certan y ci rendoirn l'apponment abhorrent ben heres ny domain Augsbourger—
Setting aside issues of predestination inherent in this approach—though of course this rendered the theory anathema or even heretical in the Augsburg dominia—

y reclam pront (veyað d'al-Siddik l'ouvr 1696 Ag Figl a Zeno) sta y fait de recarnt un caðen infinið de zoia jamay picquessem postular, voluð hamtar ne casc animal mascr sur y Tar.
the immediate objection (see al-Siddik's 1696 work On the Children of Zeno) was that you would need to posit an infinite chain of progressively-smaller zoia to be present inside every male animal on the planet.

4m Novembr

lousc /lux/ glimmer, glint, gleam, shine

< 12C in late Old Boral lousc /luʃ/, having already its current sense. It is a deverbal noun from lougir “to gleam, glimmer”, which comes from earlier louger/fouger < Latin fulgeō “I flash, I lighten” (see also fourraç “lightning”, from the same source). The initial consonant arises likely due to semantic influence from luȝr “to shine” and lumnair “light”.

    Sy parol tenen coler, ma jo vis un lousc ricotant aç ogl.

traiscnant /trexˈnant/ [tʀɪçˈnan(t)] aftermath, trail, wake

< 15C in Middle Boral, at first literally “trail, train (thing dragging behind)”, then extended to metaphorical use by late 17C; it is the present participle of traiscnar “to drag behind, trail, lag, be late”. ##

6m Novembr

    taken from A Mapbook Compendium of Europe, from the Division of Rome until the German Secession (450–1250 N), published by the Tommarth Open School of History in 1997. It is used as part of the supplementary reference materials provided for students sitting history scitations in many Merch universities.

The Drengotian Empire, named for its ruling family the Drengot (formerly the ruling ducal family of Normandy) had by 1120 N maintained stable borders for several decades. The empire owes this impression of peace in part to the decentralised structure of the realm: Tolose even held a standing army for its perennial skirmishes with neighbouring Barcelon.

But of course none were more independent in the so-called Constant Empire (a name used only in retrospect in an attempt to lend the realm gravitas, referencing Rome's District of Constantius) than Kernow and Devon, who a century earlier had been vital to the Norman conquest of Kent. Indeed, in 1121 N King Vigo of Greater Devon would attack Wales without the support of Emperor Roger II.

Roger II spent most of his reign in Paris, with his younger brother Matthew ruling in his name in London. By 1120 N, much of the empire's treasury has been spent on defending the long eastern border with Willemy. In particular, the construction of a new Great Castle at Vésonçon had been completed the year before, the last point in a defensive line spanning from Flanders to the Alps.

7m Novembr

broccat /broˈkat/ [bʀʊˈkat] brocade, thick silk fabric, usually embossed with embroidered patterns and perhaps gold thread; by extension, gaudy or over-formal clothing

< 14C in Middle Boral broccat "brocade", probably directly from Italian broccato "embossed cloth" via naval trade, but possibly via Burgund or another French variety (see also cascat "waterfall"). The Italian is from brocco "small nail", ultimately of Gaulish Kelt origin. Pejorative sense from the early 19C, developing in the Straightforward Fashion popular across northern Europe at that time.

    Y vriðel stan ny broccat y ci annað a sy paðr ramenant.

    The curtains were made of brocade redolent of her father's generation.

##

8m Novembr

zoia /zoˈja/ [zʊjˈja] microbe, microorganism; any of the one-celled forms of life

< late 17C in translation of medical and biological texts, from Scholatic Latin zoia "microbe", originaly a neuter plural but quickly used as a singular. The Latin formed from Greek *zoion (ζῷῐον /zo.i.on/), literally "small animal". For a period in the late 19C some attempted to restrict its use to microbes without kernel [nucleus] but this never caught on. Whether to include bdella [viruses] as zoia was more contentious, and is still inconsistent across modern languages.

    Memorað vos y zoia cholera produgnt ?

    Do you remember which bacteria cause cholera?

    /ˌme.moˈraθ vo i zoˈja ˌko.leˈra proˈdajnt/

9m Novembr

    excerpt with English translation from the 1872 trevold Un Onomatoir Parçal dy Novel Londr (An Incomplete Dictionary of the New London), written by Hadassa Docquerl, heiress to the barony of Livaucr in the north. Probably the most famous Vetomundine work from the Futurism period of parachthon romance, it follows a tourist in the unspecified future visiting a rebuilt London some decades after it was destroyed in a so-called "Annihilation War". This book is also notable for containing the first use of "slate" to refer to a screen which can display information.

Thomas refey sell'azig e sortoy ny colloir dy carrot, s'affignt all'aucoum a gent attenent descendr.
Thomas remade his shoes and stood up into the car's corridor, joining the throng of people waiting to disembark.

The long windows descended, revealing a teeming stationful of hatted figures huddling under raincatchers to avoid the inclement weather.

Reðr lou spanoirn ardois scaut dessur con hour d'yenç eð y novel cottidian; Thomas descifrau uncos l'ambasctour Cambresc partenent pre y parol collaurn dessou dy pareiveðr.
Behind them stretched slates full of arrival times and the daily news; Thomas made out something about the Cambrick ambassador before the line slid under the sill.

Cos final y port dy carrot rollaurn atras, donc Thomas fleiçau y valigs e seveu l'eç autr sojournour sur y quarf.
At last the car doors slid open, so Thomas collapsed his suitcase and followed the other passengers out onto the platform.

10m Novembr

coyenç /koˈjɛnts/ [kʊjˈjɛns] dam, guard, barrier; prevention, protection

< 12C in late Old Boral coyenç "dam, barrier", a nominalisation (which is still transparent in the modern language) from coyir "to restrain, hold back, block" (modern coïr "to prevent"). Usage spread to abstract senses of prevention by the late 17C; see the phrase coyenç sanater "preventative medicine" as used in the contemporary scholastic literature.

    L'ig coyenç accommet tan un pont a pieton.

11m Novembr

fissar /fiˈsar/ [fɪˈsɑː] to hiss, fizz, whistle

< from Middle Boral fisçar "to whistle, hiss, sing (of birds)" (extension to fizzing liquids since 17C, birdsong sense overtaken by kisir around the same time), probably from Late Latin fistulō "I whistle, blow through a pipe". There was likely onomatopoeic alteration in the mediæval period; some even suggest that there is no direct connection to the Latin. Said Latin form is a verbal derivation from fistula "pipe, tube, hollow reed or cane, shepherd's pipe", from findō "I cleave, split".

    Des fissau y bogloir, jo le pris deur y foy .

    When the kettle began to whistle I took it out of the fire.

    /dez fiˈso i bɔjˈlɔjr, ʒo le ˈprɪz dawr i ˈfɔj/

    excerpted from the 14th October 1950 edition of the Fiellas Dimenja (Sunday Sheets), a Marsellan weekly newspaper focusing primarily on recreation and entertainment. It was incorporated in 1871 by Corsican businessman Toscobardo Matutte and held by 1950 the record for the longest uninterrupted weekly mass-market publication, without interruption either by the Millstone War (although it briefly moved its printers to Manòsca during the worst of the bombings) nor by the 1937 Veldsvindung [1] and pursuant general poverty.

...[this film] will be no classic: come only for the views (of beautiful forests, and a bevy of handsome actors, I concede) and two hours of mindless tragedy. - Jacint Brunat

NEWS AFIELD: SCANDAL! Hasinic [2] PRINCE ADMITS 30⅊ MILLION OF DEBT

Prince Pether Tamaha, lesser-known son of the illustrious Hasinic monarchy, has admitted to the press that he owes thirty million poise (over twenty million trone) in debt to speculation companies and to national banks across the continent from Mashick to Ambrosia [3]. Furthermore, his grandfather the King has declined to bail him out, in an unprecedented move.

Longtime speculation has held that the young prince (who hosted a lavish thirtieth birthday celebration in the Davarn Heul [4] this February, with over five thousand attendees) has been regularly running through his funds and relying on his family's coffers. "For King Maldwen to now stem the flow of gold to his grandchild indicates one of two things," writes Matties Gramunt, Provincial ambassador to Gulf Mendeva [5]. "Either personal relations between the prince and his family have deteriorated to a serious nazir, or else the Tamaha monarchy is itself in dire financial straits." The steward convoker to the family has so far refused to comment on the issue.

The prince himself has not been seen in public since his participation in the Taishan [6] centenary celebration at the border city of...

___

[1] global economic recession. [2] from Hasiny, a state occupying much of the territory of IRL east Texas, Oklahoma and Louisiana. [3] roughly, Mexico and eastern Canada. [4] The Sun Hotel, a world-famous resort town. [5] North America's southern coast. [6] from Taisha, a Gulf Mendevan state (cognate with Texas).

##

13m Novembr

temporane /tɛmˌpo.raˈne/ [tɪmˌpo.ʀɐˈne] contemporary, concurrent, simlutaneous; (adverb) at that time, then

< from 16C as temporane "contemporaneous", borrowed directly from Scholastic Latin temporāneus with the same sense. The word classically meant "timely, opportune", but the sense had shifted by the late mediæval period. It is an adjectival derivation from tempus, tempora "time, period".

    Sell'ouvr es y cim de parform temporane.

    His work is the pinnacle of contemporary theatre.

    /seˈlu.vr̩ ɛz i ˈtsɪm de parˈfɔrm tɛmˌpo.raˈne/

14m Novembr

scencar /xɛnˈkar/ [çɪŋˈkɑː] to pour (out), to give or offer a drink, to toast (with preposition scencar a)

< 10C in Old Boral scencar /ʃenˈkar/ "to pour, serve a drink", from Old English scencian with the same sense (palatal dissimilation may have occured, or there may be Norrish influence; see also Kentish shink "to spill"). Its use meaning "to toast (someone)" is attested from the thirteenth century.

    Vayað ny salon e scencheð tey un dragm a brandvin.

    Go to the parlor and pour yourself a dram of brandy.

    /vɛˈjaθ ni saˈlɔn e xɛnˈkɛθ ti ɪn ˈdrɛjm a brandˈvɪn/

15m Novembr

    excerpted in translation to Boral and to English from the 1930 parachthon lovetale Galatheo, jait'O Sciença do Ben e do Mao (Galatheus, or the Knowledge of Good and Evil), written by Marcio Dourao during the Portingale Anarchy. Banned in Vascony and Morrack for its background politics (and, unofficially, for its main plot; neither polity had quite forgotten their roles in the Modest Arrangement of the previous century), it was nonetheless popular elsewhere, and was published in over twenty languages.

Cas final, posc toð y ci loit e combat, toð y calver e dolour, y majon livr de Faraje beut pog solution a Drico.
In the end, after all the struggles and fighting, all the strife and pain, Faraje's library offers Driquo few answers.

La no son nien ny majon oy for memoir—e tan gast de deyaç partið jaçol.
There is nothing in the library now but memories—and ghosts of duties long passed.

Drico es stivað de noc savir, e stivaðessem de desirar savir.
Driquo is tired of not knowing and more tired of wanting to know. 

Eç dezein a i phantasm aurfiað porseut con titr e nom l'echovant afont com campan slognað, le convoccant a repaðrir an loy seyon inconnuð, un loy—l'es ençant a comprendr—jamay volent luy parmettr saluð.
For decades he has chased golden phantoms with a title and a name ringing in his mind like distant church bells, calling him home towards a place he doesn't know, and which now he's starting to finally realise isn't ever going to welcome him.

16m Novembr

ascuranç /ˌa.xiˈrants/ [ˌa.çɪˈʀans] certificate, certification, licence; patent, proof of authenticity

< first used in legal records in 14C ascuranç "licence, certification, legal permission" in reference to a Pentros brewery. It is a nominalisation of ascurar "to ensure, to secure", itself an (older) verbal derivarion from scur "safe, secure". This comes from Latin sēcūrus "careless, fearless, safe"; only the final sense survived even into Old Boral.

    Y Minister a Famigl va m'accaçar sell'ascuranç a mort!

    The Ministry for Family must send me her death certificate!

17m Novembr

sorgr /ˈsɔr.gr̩/ [ˈsɔː.gɐ] to get up, to stand up, to rise; to act, to do work

< first attested very early in 7C Mediæval Latin/Old Boral surgre /ˈsorgrə/ "to rise, get up", from Latin surgō with the same sense. It acquired more active senses in the Middle Boral period, at which point its conjugation had also solidified as an s-type R verb (with past participle sors).

    Sorgað, monteð sur y vec e scandað y scagl aç banc ovart.

    Stand up, get on the bus and climb the stairs to the open-air seats.

18m Novembr

    scitation questions taken from the 1986 paper for the course Scoclat e Coscou : Cappatia par y Kintanç Mondial (Chocolate and Rubber: Cappatia during the Twining Century), taught by the Faculty of Domain History at the New Vithor University in Borland.

PART B: The Bróderrat Wayports in Cappatia

1. Read the following excerpt from Haddani's notoriously-innaccurate Empire of the Sun and answer the subsequent questions.

“It was the pride of Emperor Matthias II that brought the Germans across the Atlantic in search of new lands and peoples in the mid-sixteenth century. News of the joint Morrack and British voyages had inflamed all Europe with an irrepressible wanderlust, and while the Kazmid and Vascon enterprises had firmly established themselves as sole merchants in eastern Mendeva and Horizic Cappatia, few had yet mapped the Atlantic coastline of Cappatia. Thus there was an attainable goal for the Emperor to assign to the great fleet at his disposal: discover all that these lands had to offer, from Berpittang wood to crimson Mackow feathers.”

a) With reference to the Neuwillem Bróderratcronike (1561-97), evaluate Haddani's framing of the factors that led to the German exploration of Atlantic Cappatia in the latter half of the sixteenth century.

b) To what extent was Emperor Matthias II (1542-68) able to influence the mercantile decisions of the Stadbund at all? Compare the stance of the Drachman merchant dynasty towards the various monarchic European powers with which they dealt, and the underlying economic conditions leading to these dynamics.

c) How separately can we consider the activities of the Bróderrat in establishing Neuwillem, Parnow and other wayports from the similar activities of Morrack in Mashick and Tavancy; what collaboration and cross-pollination of methods and technologies, if any, occurred between the Vetomundine companies?

##

19m Novembr

accapellar /aˌka.peˈlar/ [ɐ.ka.pɪˈlɑː] to don a hat, to put a hat on

< attested, at least in the past participial form accapellað “wearing a hat” (the only form in productive use, though the finite verb occurs sporadically), since the fourteenth century. It is modelled after other verbs in a-X-ar, such as arrimbandar “to beribbon”, abancar “to make landfall” and akirtar “to ignite”. Specifically, it is derived from capel “hat”, from Vulgar Latin cappellus with the same meaning, itself a diminutive from Latin cappa “cape, cloak”.

    Lou dou accapellaurn e sorteurn accougl.

    They both put on their hats and left together.

    /lu du aˌka.peˈlo.rn̩ e sɔrˈtaw.rn̩ aˈkujl/

##

20m Novembr

pareiveðr /ˌpa.riˈvɛ.ðr̩/ [ˌpa.ʀɪˈvɛ.ðɐ] windowsill, ledge, skirting

< attested since 18C as parey-veðr “windowsill”, modelled after French pareil-verre with the same meaning (Boral otherwise having very few such verb-noun compounds). Literally meaning “levels the glass”, the verb comes from parey “same, equal”, a dialect form from Vulgar Latin pariclus, from Latin pār “even, equal”. The noun veðr is directly from Latin vitrum “glass”. The hyphen was dropped in the 1870 reform a dreçment which standardised Boral spelling.

    Y cat au y novel vas battuð dy pareiveðɾ.

    The cat had knocked the new vase off the windowsill.

    /i ˈkat o i noˈvɛl ˈvaz baˈtɪθ di ˌpa.riˈvɛ.ðr̩/

##

21r Novembr

    excerpted in Boral translation from Saxon mathematician Johans Dümmeyer's 1827 publication Sammel und Sendungen (Sets and Functions), a seminal work that attempted to resolve some of the disagreements in the mathematical community regarding the proper definition of limits, the continuum, and related concepts.

Nos approismau y matier com Ferenc (Geometriæ Inquadrabiles, 1819) par casc concreuçon des axiom dað admettent.
We take the approach of Ferenk (Geometriæ Inquadrabiles, 1819) in allowing any concrescence of given axioms.

Por y continuum cos particuler caurreu nos uncos starchessem : dou concreuçon calscon (y cinq soumission enonçað ne capparol 7 suffignt) son de natur jammel.
For the continuum in particular we will obtain something stronger: any two concrescences (satisfying the five propositions listed in Chapter 7) are, essentially, identical.

Un bustr senglessem vil y method illustrar. Nos eu idea naðeu a mission s des font W vars bers X ; l'oc attriboy a casc sodal ne W l'un ne X.
A simpler example will demonstrate the principle. We have an intuitive idea of a function s from source W to target X, assigning to each element of W one from X.

Por l'oc mission costroir com cougl manifest, nos poum calscon de dou noçon ja noscon vis benoçar.
To construct this function explicitly as a set, we may make use of either of two notions we have already seen.

Primer, nos definisc ig mission son porçonment dy som W+X la afont casc porçon inclun un sodal exact des X.
First, we define functions to be partitions of the disjoint union W⊔X in which each part contains precisely one element of X.

Cossy cogleu nos con un x ne X y cougl (voit, possibr!) de w ne W, suffignt ig w↦x.
Thereby we collect with an x in X the (possibly empty!) collection of w in W for which w↦x.

##

22m Novembr

yenç /ˈjɛnts/ arrival, advent, appearance, approach

< attested since 10C in Old Boral iemnce “success, attainment, procurement”, from Latin ementia “purchase, procurement”, a nominal derivation from emō “I buy, acquire”. The meaning of the noun had shifted to “seizing, reaching” by 13C, and then to its modern sense two centuries later. The corresponding verb iemr “to manage, attain” (modern yembr “to obtain, reach, arrive”) appears to have followed the noun in its meaning.

    L'yenç ag fest sauvaç resors y bourg kieð.

    The arrival of the wild party enlivened the quiet town.

    /ˈljɛnts ɛj ˈfɛst soˈvats reˈzɔrz i ˈburg ˈkjɛθ/

##

23m Novembr

laçploy /latsˈplɔj/ rain shelter, gazebo

< used from 1803 in Boral, originally hyphenated, meaning “catches rain”. It is a calque of French pare-pluye “keeps rain away”. The verb laçar “to catch, intercept, parry, deflect” comes from Old Boral latiar “to catch, snatch, grab” (probably with /tʃ/ although this is controversial; the phoneme if it existed appeared only in loans). This is a borrowing from Old English læċċan with the same sense. The noun ploy “rain” descends directly from Latin pluvia, meaning the same thing.

    Nos erau aziant sou laçploy, l'yenç ag vec attendent.

    We stood at the shelter, waiting for the bus to arrive.

    /no zeˈro aˈzjant su latsˈplɔj | ˈljɛnts ɛj ˈvɛk aˈtɛn.dɛnt/

##

24m Novembr

    in translation from 1970 historical review Conjugation, Operation e Diabolica in Musica : Y Commouð Deviant Primal (The Early Deviance Movement), written by Borlish popular historian Catrin Veçount, published to coincide with the hundred and twentieth anniversary of the Laic Declarations.

…now arrive on our own shores, for we cannot ignore Monçating any longer.

The sixth Baron Monçating was born to Lord Josua and Lady Evangelin in the winter of 1770 at their estate in Launembrig. Lord Josua, inevitably, was himself a controversial figure in Damvath society for several reasons: having been born in the Cappatian staddomain, having married a noblewoman of Warnine descent (far too recently for Vetomundine sensibilities), and finally having inherited the Barony of Haubrath from his second cousin following a decade-long legal battle (one of the longest legal disputes in Borland history). His decision thereupon to dissolve the senior title and retain only Monçating was the final nail in the coffin for any hope of respectability.

Nonetheless they remained in Borland (complaining endlessly about the climate, if accounts in their acquaintances' letters are to be believed!) and bore six children, of whom our hero Simon was the eldest. Raised in the shadow of scandal, of legal and political battles, Simon Monçating learnt early how thinly-justified the power structures of the world could be. The Launembrig estate hosted regular gatherings of theorists and philosophers; the earliest writings we have in Simon's hand are from a tutor's worksheet carried by accident to Denmark in the papers of Jan Prestr in 1779 (author of Discriptione Bestiarum Suboles Ferentis “On the Classification of the Child-Bearing Beasts”).

However interesting his childhood—by eighteen Simon could play the clavier and the torriot, and speak Latin, Dutch, and Vascon (and even Warnine, according to his defamers)—we remember the sixth Lord Monçating today for his later escapades…

25m Novembr

stivað /stiˈvaθ/ [stɪˈvah] crowded, stuffed; fed up, frustrated

< attested almost continuously, at least in its literal sense of “stuffed, packed, crammed in”; see Old Boral istivaða in the eighth-century Halvuy chronicle, for example. The word is transparently the past participle of stivar “to stuff, pack, crowd”, from Latin stīpō “I compress, cram, surround”. Its metaphorical use for “frustrated, fed up, having had enough” is seen in writing only from 15C but is suspected to be an old usage.

    Vazdara! Jo's stivað de vossy connardaç!

    Fuck off! I'm fed up with your fucking bullshit!

    /ˌvaz.daˈra ‖ ʒɔz stiˈvaθ de ˈvɔstr‿i ˌko.narˈdats/

##

26m Novembr

quarf /ˈkwarf/ wharf, quay, jetty, pier; also (train) station

< attested from 10C in Old Boral western dialects (various forms) carf, querf “breakwater, riverbank; langing place for boats”, from Old English hwearf “heap, embankment, wharf”. There was possible influence from Old Norrish hverf “turning, curve” (the cognate to hwearf), although this is contested. The semantics of quarf broadened to pile-supported structures like piers by the seventeenth century, displacing biðr “pier, jetty” (from Vulgar Latin petricus, related to petra “stone”).

    Dou picq najel yemen ag quarf, cargað de pescot.

    Two small boats arrived a the quay, laden with fish.

    /ˈdu pɪk naˈʒɛl jeˈmɛn ɛj ˈkwarf | karˈgaθ de peˈxɔt/

27m Novembr

    in Borlish translation from Willem author Carlyn van Stede's 1965 giftale Zolang Gout dór myn Ádren Noh Vlóye (While Gold Yet Runs in My Veins), a parachthonic take on the genre, following several characters with extraordinary abilities across Revitalist Italy and Hungary.

“Cal som nos faint ?” rou Jovanna, tandic Marta met ogl sur rig a immoubr, cascun parmy ne stat demolið com picq.
“What are we doing?” Jovanna asks, while Marta eyes a row of buildings, all of them in pretty bad repair.

“Bors coglent,” respon Marta, y mur sgardant. L'es sanessem a paravant, ma ancour es i fortessem nommað veil a joun, donc… “Jovanna, promet mey uncos,” dig i serious. “Atten noc all'oc tey meðes fin a star treç eld ben may.”
“Gathering funds,” Marta answers, looking at the wall. She is healthier than she was, but she's still on the greying side of old, so… “Jovanna, promise me something,” she says seriously. “Don't try this yourself until you're at least thirteen.”

Y joun fay sclar d'ogl. “Jo poð noc contar ?”
The kid blinks at her. “I can't count?”

Marta soupir. “Jo vil ig t'instroir—remain ac.”

The girl just gives her a mostly puzzled look—then lets out an incredulous sound as Marta takes the wall at a slight jog, putting her foot on it to push upwards, just like she used to several lifetimes ago and—

Zo, i's—ovrant concret.
Oh, it's—actually working.
